Chapter 1: Chronic heart failure
  Mini Chapter 1.1: Universal Definition of HF   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.2: Classification
    Lesson 1.2.1: Class I (Mild): Patients with cardiac disease but NO limitation of physical activity.
    Lesson 1.2.2: Class II (Mild): Patients with cardiac disease but slight limitation of physical activity.
    Lesson 1.2.3: Class III (Moderate): Patients with cardiac disease but marked limitation of physical activity.
  Mini Chapter 1.3: Epidemiology
    Lesson 1.3.1: The lifetime risk of HF at age 55 years is 33% for men and 28% for women.
  Mini Chapter 1.4: Pathophysiology of HFrEF
    Lesson 1.4.1: Left Ventricular Remodelling
    Lesson 1.4.2: Alteration in myocyte structure.
    Lesson 1.4.3: Alteration in myocyte biology.
    Lesson 1.4.4: Alteration in LV geometry.
    Lesson 1.4.5: Neurohormonal Mechanisms
    Lesson 1.4.6: Activation of sympathetic nervous system
    Lesson 1.4.7: Activation of RAAS system
    Lesson 1.4.8: Cardiac Myocyte Hypertrophy
    Lesson 1.4.9: Neurohormonal Alterations of Renal Function
    Lesson 1.4.10: Neurohormonal Alterations in the Peripheral Vasculature
  Mini Chapter 1.5: Aetiology
    Lesson 1.5.1: HF with reduced EF (Systolic HF)
    Lesson 1.5.2: Large transmural Q‐wave MI.
    Lesson 1.5.3: HF with preserved EF (Diastolic HF)
    Lesson 1.5.4: Intrinsic RV dysfunction: ARVD or RV infarct.
  Mini Chapter 1.6: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 1.6.1: Congestive findings (1)
    Lesson 1.6.2: Exertional dyspnea: this may be a manifestation of
    Lesson 1.6.3: Bendopnea: shortness of breath when leaning forward. Seen in cases of advanced HF.
    Lesson 1.6.4: Increased jugular venous pulsation (JVP) ≥ 8 cmH2O
    Lesson 1.6.5: Crackles/pulmonary edema/pleural effusions.
  Mini Chapter 1.7: The most reliable congestive findings are
    Lesson 1.7.1: Low‐output findings: (also known as “cold” signs) correlate with a more advanced HF stage.
    Lesson 1.7.2: Severe fatigue, increased time to recover after exercise.
    Lesson 1.7.3: Cold, clammy extremities.
    Lesson 1.7.4: Valvar theory: sudden limitation of longitudinal expansion of LV wall during diastole
    Lesson 1.7.5: Compensatory tachycardia.
    Lesson 1.7.6: Abdominal pain may result from functional bowel ischemia or from liver distension.
  Mini Chapter 1.8: Investigations
    Lesson 1.8.1: Diagnostic tests in all patients with suspected HF
    Lesson 1.8.2: Q waves usually point towards ischemic cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 1.8.3: Advanced AV block is frequently seen in myocarditis and infiltrative disease.
    Lesson 1.8.4: LBBB with QRS > 130 ms has therapeutic implications (benefit from CRT).
    Lesson 1.8.5: BNP takes 1 hour to rise and may be initially normal in hyperacute HF.
    Lesson 1.8.6: BNP is mostly cleared by tissue peptidase, but is partially cleared renally.
    Lesson 1.8.7: BNP tends to be higher in the elderly, in women, in AF, in renal failure, and in cirrhosis.
    Lesson 1.8.8: Heart failure
    Lesson 1.8.9: Advanced age
    Lesson 1.8.10: Acute coronary syndromes
    Lesson 1.8.11: Ischemic stroke
    Lesson 1.8.12: Pulmonary embolism
    Lesson 1.8.13: Subarachnoid hemorrhage
    Lesson 1.8.14: Myocarditis
    Lesson 1.8.15: Renal dysfunction
    Lesson 1.8.16: Left ventricular hypertrophy.
    Lesson 1.8.17: Liver dysfunction (mainly cirrhosis with ascites)
    Lesson 1.8.18: Hypertrophic or restrictive cardiomyopathy
    Lesson 1.8.19: Paraneoplastic syndrome
    Lesson 1.8.20: Valvular heart disease
    Lesson 1.8.21: Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease
    Lesson 1.8.22: Atrail and ventricular tachyarrhythmia
    Lesson 1.8.23: Severe infection (including sepsis and pneumonia)
    Lesson 1.8.24: Heart contusion
    Lesson 1.8.25: Severe burns
    Lesson 1.8.26: Cardioversion, ICD shock
    Lesson 1.8.27: Anemia
    Lesson 1.8.28: Surgical procedures involving the heart.
    Lesson 1.8.29: Severe metabolic or hormonal abnormalities
    Lesson 1.8.30: Pulmonary hypertension
    Lesson 1.8.31: TAPSE; abnormal TAPSE < 17 mm indicates RV systolic dysfunction and
    Lesson 1.8.32: Echo assesses for other forms of HF
    Lesson 1.8.33: Isolated RV failure with its various causes (e.g., pulmonary hypertension, ASD).
    Lesson 1.8.34: Right sided HF mimickers: constrictive pericarditis, tamponade.
    Lesson 1.8.35: Specialized diagnostic tests for selected patients with chronic heart failure
    Lesson 1.8.36: Cardiac MRI has four major applications in HF
    Lesson 1.8.37: Diagnosis of myocarditis in unexplained cardiomyopathy or unexplained, large troponin elevation.
    Lesson 1.8.38: Viability assessment in ischemic cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 1.8.39: Patients with a history of symptomatic ventricular arrhythmia or aborted cardiac arrest.
    Lesson 1.8.40: Stress echocardiography
    Lesson 1.8.41: In patients with unexplained dyspnoea and suspected HFpEF, stress echo help clarify the diagnosis.
  Mini Chapter 1.9: Diagnostic tests in patients with suspected HF   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.10: The following tests are recommended in all patients with suspected chronic heart failure
    Lesson 1.10.1: BNP/NT-proBNP
    Lesson 1.10.2: 12-lead ECG
    Lesson 1.10.3: Transthoracic echocardiography
    Lesson 1.10.4: Chest radiography (X-ray)
  Mini Chapter 1.11: CMR   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

  Mini Chapter 1.15: Cardiopulmonary exercise testing should be considered to
    Lesson 1.15.1: Optimize prescription of exercise training.
    Lesson 1.15.2: Identify the cause of unexplained dyspnoea and/or exercise intolerance.
  Mini Chapter 1.16: Right heart catheterization   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.17: Right heart catheterization should be considered   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.18: EMB   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.19: Treatment of HFrEF   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.20: There are three major goals of treatment for patients with HFrEF   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.21: Three compensatory mechanisms occur in HF and are ultimately harmful
    Lesson 1.21.1: Pharmacological Treatment
    Lesson 1.21.2: How to use?
    Lesson 1.21.3: Be cautious in cases of
    Lesson 1.21.4: If urea, creatinine, or K+ does rise excessively
    Lesson 1.21.5: Reverse the deleterious and apoptotic effects of catecholamines on the heart.
    Lesson 1.21.6: Reverse remodeling and reduce cardiac chamber size by reducing LV wall stress.
    Lesson 1.21.7: Prevent arrhythmias.
    Lesson 1.21.8: The three agents that have been shown to improve survival and outcomes in HF are
    Lesson 1.21.9: Keys to β‐blocker therapy
    Lesson 1.21.10: Start “low and slow”: Patients have to be euvolemic and stable.
    Lesson 1.21.11: The benefit in HF mainly results from
    Lesson 1.21.12: Blockade of the myocardial aldosterone receptors;
    Lesson 1.21.13: Increase in potassium (antiarrythmic effect);
    Lesson 1.21.14: Reduction of the tubular resistance to loop diuretics.
    Lesson 1.21.15: If gynecomastia develops, eplerenone may be used instead of spironolactone.
    Lesson 1.21.16: How to use
    Lesson 1.21.17: Start with a low dose and consider dose up-titration after 4-8 weeks.
    Lesson 1.21.18: Enhancing diuresis, natriuresis and myocardial relaxation and anti-remodelling.
    Lesson 1.21.19: Inhibiting renin and aldosterone secretion.
    Lesson 1.21.20: Start with a low dose.
    Lesson 1.21.21: Double the dose at not less than 2-week intervals in the community, monitoring tolerability.
    Lesson 1.21.22: Identify the risk factors predisposing to ketoacidosis and eliminate them if possible.
    Lesson 1.21.23: Monitor fluid balance regularly. Consider an adjustment of diuretic therapy and fluid intake.
    Lesson 1.21.24: Re-check (Urea, creatinine, K+) 1-2 weeks after an initiation and after any increase in dose.
    Lesson 1.21.25: Insufficient diuretic response/diuretic resistance
    Lesson 1.21.26: Check adherence and fluid/salt intake.
    Lesson 1.21.27: Increase a dose of diuretic.
    Lesson 1.21.28: Consider switching from furosemide to bumetanide or torasemide.
    Lesson 1.21.29: Add an MRA/increase dose of an MRA.
    Lesson 1.21.30: Combine loop diuretic and thiazide/metolazone.
    Lesson 1.21.31: Administer loop diuretic twice (or more times) daily or on empty stomach.
    Lesson 1.21.32: Consider short-term i.v. infusion of loop diuretic.
    Lesson 1.21.33: Consider ultrafiltration.
    Lesson 1.21.34: The combination may also be used instead of ACE‐I/ARB in case of intolerance to ACE‐I/ARB.
    Lesson 1.21.35: Digoxin
    Lesson 1.21.36: Cardiac myosin activator (Omecamtiv mecarbil)

  Mini Chapter 1.35: Digoxin
    Lesson 1.35.1: Device Treatment
    Lesson 1.35.2: As a primary prevention, ICD is indicated in patients with cardiomyopathy and EF < 35%
    Lesson 1.35.3: Wait > 40 days after MI. RCTs showed no benefit of ICD implantation within 40 days after a MI.
  Mini Chapter 1.36: Secondary prevention   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.37: Primary prevention   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.38: In HFrEF patients with sinus rhythm
    Lesson 1.38.1: CRT is recommended if QRS duration ≥ 150 ms, and LBBB QRS morphology.
    Lesson 1.38.2: CRT should be considered if QRS duration 130-149 ms, and LBBB QRS morphology.
    Lesson 1.38.3: CRT should be considered if QRS duration ≥ 150 ms, and non-LBBB QRS morphology
    Lesson 1.38.4: CRT may be considered if QRS duration 130-149 ms, and non-LBBB QRS morphology
  Mini Chapter 1.39: In patients with AF   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.40: For Antibradycardic pacing
    Lesson 1.40.1: Other therapeutic measures
    Lesson 1.40.2: Avoidance of harmful medications
    Lesson 1.40.3: Non‐dihydropyridines CCBs (negative inotropic effect) (1).
    Lesson 1.40.4: Diabetes medications: Thiazolidinediones (sodium retention).
    Lesson 1.40.5: Cilostazol (antiplatelet drug and a vasodilator; ventricular tachyarrhythmias).
    Lesson 1.40.6: Licorice (mineralocorticoid excess; sodium retention and hypokalemia).
  Mini Chapter 1.41: Prognosis
    Lesson 1.41.1: Predictors of HF hospitalizations: AF, higher BMI, higher HbA1c, and low eGFR.
    Lesson 1.41.2: Several factors negatively affect prognosis in HF patients
    Lesson 1.41.3: Severely decreased EF (< 30%).
    Lesson 1.41.4: Refractory functional class IIIB or IV (1‐year mortality 30-50%).
  Mini Chapter 1.42: Prevention of heart failure   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.43: In order to prevent or delay the onset of HF
    Lesson 1.43.1: Treatment of hypertension is recommended.
    Lesson 1.43.2: Treatment with statins is recommended in patients at high risk of CV disease.
    Lesson 1.43.3: SGLT2 inhibitors (canagliflozin, dapagliflozin, empagliflozin, ertugliflozin, sotagliflozin) are
    Lesson 1.43.4: Counselling against sedentary habit, obesity, cigarette smoking, and alcohol abuse is
  Mini Chapter 1.44: Follow-up of chronic heart failure   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.45: Co-morbidities   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.46: Importance of co-morbidities in patients with heart failure
    Lesson 1.46.1: Aggravate HF symptoms and further impair quality of life.
    Lesson 1.46.2: Contribute to the burden of hospitalizations and mortality.
    Lesson 1.46.3: Drugs used to treat co-morbidities may worsen HF (e.g. NSAIDs given for arthritis).
    Lesson 1.46.4: Interaction between drugs used to treat HF and those used to treat co-morbidities.
    Lesson 1.46.5: Chronic coronary syndrome
    Lesson 1.46.6: Diltiazem and verapamil increase HF-related events in patients with HFrEF and are contraindicated.
    Lesson 1.46.7: Atrial Fibrillation
    Lesson 1.46.8: Potential causes or precipitating factors (e.g, hyperthyroidism, MV disease) should be corrected.
    Lesson 1.46.9: Valvular heart disease: (For more details, See chapter 12)
    Lesson 1.46.10: Hypertension
    Lesson 1.46.11: Treatment of HFrEF is similar in hypertensive and normotensive patients.
    Lesson 1.46.12: Cancer
    Lesson 1.46.13: Stroke
    Lesson 1.46.14: Diabetes
    Lesson 1.46.15: Thyroid disorders
    Lesson 1.46.16: Obesity
    Lesson 1.46.17: Frailty, cachexia, sarcopenia
    Lesson 1.46.18: Anaemia and Iron deficiency
    Lesson 1.46.19: Iron deficiency impairs myocardial and musculoskeletal metabolism, even in the absence of anemia.
    Lesson 1.46.20: Kidney dysfunction
    Lesson 1.46.21: Electrolyte disorders
    Lesson 1.46.22: In patients with K+ levels of 4.5-5.0 mEq/L
    Lesson 1.46.23: RAAS inhibitor therapy can be initiated/uptitrated.
    Lesson 1.46.24: If K+ levels rise above 5.0 mEq/L, initiate an approved K+-lowering agent.
    Lesson 1.46.25: In patients with K+ levels of 5.0-6.5 mEq/L
    Lesson 1.46.26: An approved K+-lowering agent should be initiated.
    Lesson 1.46.27: If levels < 5.0 mEq/L are detected, uptitrate RAAS inhibitor.
    Lesson 1.46.28: In patients with K+ levels of > 6.5 mEq/L
    Lesson 1.46.29: It is recommended to discontinue/reduce RAAS inhibitor.
    Lesson 1.46.30: Lung disease
    Lesson 1.46.31: Sleep-disordered breathing
    Lesson 1.46.32: Polysomnography is the gold standard test for sleep disorders.
    Lesson 1.46.33: Gout and arthritis
    Lesson 1.46.34: Erectile dysfunction
  Mini Chapter 1.47: Iron deficiency Anemia   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.48: Intravenous iron supplementation with ferric carboxymaltose or ferric derisomaltose   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.49: Diabete and chronic kidney disease   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.50: Cancer
    Lesson 1.50.1: Diagnosis
  Mini Chapter 1.51: LV diastolic dysfunction/raised LV filling pressures
    Lesson 1.51.1: > 35 (SR) or
    Lesson 1.51.2: > 105 (AF) pg/mL
    Lesson 1.51.3: > 125 (SR) or
    Lesson 1.51.4: > 365 (AF) pg/mL
    Lesson 1.51.5: ≥ 95 g/m2 (Female)
    Lesson 1.51.6: ≥ 115 g/m2 (Male)
    Lesson 1.51.7: RWT > 0.42
    Lesson 1.51.8: > 34 mL/m2 (SR)
    Lesson 1.51.9: > 40 mL/m (AF)
    Lesson 1.51.10: > 2.8 m/s
    Lesson 1.51.11: Treatment
    Lesson 1.51.12: For Women (all LVEF spectrum) and Men with EF < 55-60% (1)
    Lesson 1.51.13: ACEIs are not considered a reasonable alternative (due to lack of benefit in the PEP-CHF trial).
    Lesson 1.51.14: Why special category
    Lesson 1.51.15: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 1.51.16: Changes in extraceullar matrix: restoration of ECM content and organization.
    Lesson 1.51.17: Abnormal energetics: e.g., tachycardia‐induced cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 1.51.18: Stress‐related cardiomyopathies: e.g., takotsubo cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 1.51.19: Predictors of recovery: the following parameters are more likely to have recovery
    Lesson 1.51.20: Clinical Parameters: Female gender, non‐ischemic aetiology, shorter duration of HF, LBBB in CRT.
    Lesson 1.51.21: How to achieve recovery?
    Lesson 1.51.22: Removal of the causative agent: e.g., cessation of alcohol in alcoholic cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 1.51.23: Pharmacological therapy (GDMT) for HFrEF.
    Lesson 1.51.24: Device therapy e.g LVAD and CRT.
    Lesson 1.51.25: How to maintain recovery ?
    Lesson 1.51.26: Predictors of relapse
    Lesson 1.51.27: Older age.
    Lesson 1.51.28: The need for persistent diuretics.
    Lesson 1.51.29: Discontinuation of HF medication.
    Lesson 1.51.30: Lower glomerular filtration rate.
    Lesson 1.51.31: Follow up: Follow up visits should be every 6 months for at least 3 years, then yearly.
    Lesson 1.51.32: Definition
    Lesson 1.51.33: Diagnosis
    Lesson 1.51.34: Imaging can be a useful tool in detecting patients with atrial myopathy.
    Lesson 1.51.35: Management
    Lesson 1.51.36: Women with pre-existing HF have higher risk of CV complications including HF decompensation.
    Lesson 1.51.37: Follow up
    Lesson 1.51.38: For women in mWHO II-III: Bimonthly assessments.
    Lesson 1.51.39: For women with pre-existing HF in mWHO III: monthly assessments.
    Lesson 1.51.40: Clinical presentation
    Lesson 1.51.41: Patients frequently present with acute HF, ventricular arrhythmias and/or cardiac arrest.
    Lesson 1.51.42: Drug therapy
    Lesson 1.51.43: During pregnancy
    Lesson 1.51.44: After delivery
    Lesson 1.51.45: In PPCM with cardiogenic shock
    Lesson 1.51.46: Prognosis and counseling
    Lesson 1.51.47: Definition: All the following criteria must be present despite optimal medical treatment
    Lesson 1.51.48: LVEF ≤ 30%.
    Lesson 1.51.49: Isolated RV failure (e.g., ARVC).
    Lesson 1.51.50: Non-operable severe valve abnormalities.
    Lesson 1.51.51: Non-operable severe congenital abnormalities.
    Lesson 1.51.52: Referal to HF specialized unit
    Lesson 1.51.53: INTERMACS profiles
    Lesson 1.51.54: End-of-life care
    Lesson 1.51.55: Patients with heart failure in whom end-of-life care should be considered
    Lesson 1.51.56: Progressive functional decline (physical and mental) and dependence in activities of daily living.
    Lesson 1.51.57: Severe heart failure symptoms with poor QOL despite optimal medical and device therapy.
    Lesson 1.51.58: Frequent admissions to hospital or serious episodes of decompensation despite optimal treatment.
    Lesson 1.51.59: Heart transplantation and MCS ruled out.
    Lesson 1.51.60: Cardiac cachexia.
    Lesson 1.51.61: Clinically judged to be close to end of life.
    Lesson 1.51.62: Anxiety and depression: adequate conventional treatment should be offered.

Chapter 2: Acute heart failure
  Mini Chapter 2.1: Acute HF presentation encompasses three syndromes   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.2: Triggers of acute decompensation
    Lesson 2.2.1: Non‐compliance with medical therapy, salt and fluid restriction.
    Lesson 2.2.2: Increased sympathetic drive, stress-related cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 2.2.3: Drug-induced (alcohol, NSAIDs, steroids, recreational drugs, and cardiotoxic chemotherapeutics).
  Mini Chapter 2.3: In systolic or diastolic dysfunction, three untoward effects occur   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.4: Notes
    Lesson 2.4.1: 40-50% of acute heart failure episodes have no known precipitant.
  Mini Chapter 2.5: Classification
    Lesson 2.5.1: Increased afterload
    Lesson 2.5.2: RV dysfunction and/or -Severe cardiac
    Lesson 2.5.3: Sodium and water retention
    Lesson 2.5.4: Valvular heart disease
    Lesson 2.5.5: LVEDP and PCWP: Increased -LVEDP and PCWP
    Lesson 2.5.6: LVEDP and PCWP
    Lesson 2.5.7: Cardiac output
    Lesson 2.5.8: SBP: Normal to low
    Lesson 2.5.9: SBP: Normal to high
    Lesson 2.5.10: SBP: Low
    Lesson 2.5.11: Wet and warm or
    Lesson 2.5.12: Wet and warm
    Lesson 2.5.13: Wet and cold
    Lesson 2.5.14: Diuretics
    Lesson 2.5.15: Diuretics for peripheral -Inotropic agents/
    Lesson 2.5.16: Vasodilators
    Lesson 2.5.17: Inotropic/vasopressors
    Lesson 2.5.18: Short-term MCS or RRT if
    Lesson 2.5.19: Inotropic/vasopressors -Short-term MCS
    Lesson 2.5.20: Short-term MCS or RRT
  Mini Chapter 2.6: Clinical picture/Profiles
    Lesson 2.6.1: Orthopnea (90% sensitive), crackles (uncommon), peripheral edema, pleural effusions, ascites.
    Lesson 2.6.2: Elevated JVP, S3, loud P2 that is heard at the lower left sternum or apex (usually with split S2).
    Lesson 2.6.3: Cool and/or cyanotic extremities.
    Lesson 2.6.4: Severe fatigue, drowsiness, Cheyne-Stokes respiration.
    Lesson 2.6.5: Severe worsening of renal failure.
    Lesson 2.6.6: Hyponatremia.
    Lesson 2.6.7: Poor or no response to diuretics.
    Lesson 2.6.8: Class I: No clinical signs of HF.
    Lesson 2.6.9: Class II: HF with rales and S3 gallop.
    Lesson 2.6.10: Class III: Frank acute pulmonary oedema.
  Mini Chapter 2.7: Investigations
    Lesson 2.7.1: Echocardiography
    Lesson 2.7.2: Laboratory tests
  Mini Chapter 2.8: Routine tests   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.9: For Prognostic assessment and treatment   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.10: For assessment of causes and comorbidities   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.11: Treatment   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.12: Management of AHF consists of
    Lesson 2.12.1: Urgent Care
    Lesson 2.12.2: ↓ Venous return → ↓ RV preload → ↓ LV preload.
    Lesson 2.12.3: ↑ Pulmonary vascular resistance → ↑ RV afterload → RV enlargement → ↓ LV Compliance.
    Lesson 2.12.4: Hospitalization
    Lesson 2.12.5: Criteria for ICU/CCU admission: include any of the following
    Lesson 2.12.6: Need for intubation (or already intubated).
    Lesson 2.12.7: Signs/symptoms of hypoperfusion.
    Lesson 2.12.8: Oxygen saturation (SaO2) < 90% (despite supplemental oxygen).
    Lesson 2.12.9: Use of accessory muscles for breathing, respiratory rate > 25/min.
    Lesson 2.12.10: Heart rate < 40 or > 130 bpm, SBP < 90 mmHg.
    Lesson 2.12.11: Diagnosis and treatment of triggers
    Lesson 2.12.12: Tachyarrhythmias or severe bradycardia/conduction disturbance
  Mini Chapter 2.13: Oxygen and ventilatory support   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.14: Diuretics   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.15: Vasodilators   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.16: Inotropic agents   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.17: Vasopressors   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.18: Other drugs
    Lesson 2.18.1: Discharge
    Lesson 2.18.2: Criteria for discharge
    Lesson 2.18.3: Treatment of exacerbating factors.
  Mini Chapter 2.19: Prognosis
    Lesson 2.19.1: There are four major prognostic risk factors
    Lesson 2.19.2: ↑ BUN or creatinine on admission.
    Lesson 2.19.3: SBP < 115 mmHg.
    Lesson 2.19.4: “Cold” low‐output HF.
    Lesson 2.19.5: Positive troponin (especially > 1 ng/ml, regardless of the presence of ischemia).
    Lesson 2.19.6: The mortality and rehospitalization rates at 60 and 90 days are 8-10% and 30%, respectively.
    Lesson 2.19.7: The mortality and rehospitalization rates at 1 year are 30% and 50%, respectively.
  Mini Chapter 2.20: Notes about pharmacological treatment used in treatment of AHF
    Lesson 2.20.1: Furosemide dosing: Loop diuretics are the mainstay of acute HF therapy.
    Lesson 2.20.2: Laboratory monitoring is necessary to detect adverse metabolic effects
    Lesson 2.20.3: Hypokalemia, Hypomagnesemia, Hypocalcemia.
    Lesson 2.20.4: Diuretic resistance
    Lesson 2.20.5: Several mechanisms are implicated
    Lesson 2.20.6: Acute, intrinsic kidney injury unresponsive to the diuretic (e.g., ATN).
    Lesson 2.20.7: Activation of the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system.
    Lesson 2.20.8: Post‐diuretic rebound effect, i.e., tubular reabsorption of sodium in between doses.
    Lesson 2.20.9: Management
    Lesson 2.20.10: If there is a moderate response to the maximal single dose of furosemide
    Lesson 2.20.11: Vasodilators should be used with caution in patients with significant mitral or aortic stenosis.
    Lesson 2.20.12: Intravenous nitroglycerin (NTG)
    Lesson 2.20.13: Intravenous nitroprusside
    Lesson 2.20.14: Nesiritide
    Lesson 2.20.15: Serelaxin (investigational)
    Lesson 2.20.16: Dobutamine
    Lesson 2.20.17: Mechanism of action: Dobutamine has marked β1‐agonist, and less marked β2‐ and α1‐agonist effect.
    Lesson 2.20.18: Milrinone
    Lesson 2.20.19: Dose: Bolus: 25-75 µg/kg over 10–20 min .. Maintenance: 0.375-0.75 µg/kg/min.
    Lesson 2.20.20: Levosimendan
    Lesson 2.20.21: Mechanism of action
    Lesson 2.20.22: Open of ATP-sensitive potassium channels → vasodilation.
    Lesson 2.20.23: Dopamine
    Lesson 2.20.24: Mechanism of action: Endogenous catecholamine, acting on both dopaminergic and adrenergic neurons.
    Lesson 2.20.25: Low dose (3-5 µg/kg/min.) stimulates dopaminergic receptors → renal and mesenteric vasodilation.
    Lesson 2.20.26: Large dose (> 10 µg/kg/min) stimulates alpha-adrenergic receptors.
    Lesson 2.20.27: Norepinephrine
    Lesson 2.20.28: Mechanism of action: have inotropic and vasoconstrictive effects.
    Lesson 2.20.29: Stimulates α1 and α2 receptors → Vasoconstriction → increases peripheral vascular resistance.
    Lesson 2.20.30: Acts on beta-1 adrenergic receptors → ↑ heart rate and cardiac output.
    Lesson 2.20.31: Dose: 0.2-1.0 µg/kg/min
    Lesson 2.20.32: Epinephrine (adrenaline)
    Lesson 2.20.33: Dose: 0.05-0.5 µg/kg/min
  Mini Chapter 2.21: RA pressure, pulmonary capillary pressure, and cardiac output, if
    Lesson 2.21.1: There is progressive renal failure with diuretic resistance.
    Lesson 2.21.2: A mixed shock is suspected (cardiogenic and septic).
    Lesson 2.21.3: Definition and prognosis
    Lesson 2.21.4: Classification
    Lesson 2.21.5: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 2.21.6: Monitoring and investigations
    Lesson 2.21.7: A 12-lead ECG should be immediately performed followed by continuous ECG monitoring.
    Lesson 2.21.8: Insertion of an arterial line and central venous catheter is recommended in all CS patients.
    Lesson 2.21.9: Management of LV‐related cardiogenic shock
    Lesson 2.21.10: In ACS-related cardiogenic shock
    Lesson 2.21.11: Inotropic support
    Lesson 2.21.12: Causes of right sided HF
    Lesson 2.21.13: Acute pulmonary hypertension e.g pulmonary embolism, acute lung injury or ARDS.
    Lesson 2.21.14: Chronic pulmonary hypertension (groups 1–5)
    Lesson 2.21.15: RV morphological features
    Lesson 2.21.16: Afterload sensitivity
    Lesson 2.21.17: Ventricular interdependence
    Lesson 2.21.18: Echocardiographic features of RV dysfunction
  Mini Chapter 2.22: Two echo indices are valuable for the assessment of RV systolic function
    Lesson 2.22.1: Diagnosis of the underlying mechanism of RV failure
    Lesson 2.22.2: PA pressure > 50, or PVR ↑: Pulmonary hypertension, possibly from left HF.
    Lesson 2.22.3: PA pressure normal or < 50, and PVR normal
    Lesson 2.22.4: Treatment of acute, isolated, or predominant RV failure
    Lesson 2.22.5: Preload management
    Lesson 2.22.6: Maintain sinus rhythm and AV synchrony
    Lesson 2.22.7: Heart transplant remains the ultimate treatment for refractory RV failure.

Chapter 3: Mechanical circulatory support (MCS)
  Mini Chapter 3.1: Mechanical circulatory support (MCS) may be used as   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 3.2: Overview of IABP   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 3.3: Triggering and timing   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 3.4: Troubleshooting and lack of appropriate augmentation   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 3.5: Indications for IABP   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 3.6: Contraindications
    Lesson 3.6.1: Moderate or severe AI.
    Lesson 3.6.2: Abdominal aortic aneurysm.
    Lesson 3.6.3: Aortic stents or grafts.
    Lesson 3.6.4: HOCM (since intracavitary obstruction increases with afterload reduction).
    Lesson 3.6.5: Insertion complications (pseudo aneurysm formation, bleeding, vascular injury).
    Lesson 3.6.6: Aortic damage.
    Lesson 3.6.7: Systemic embolization.
    Lesson 3.6.8: Limb ischaemia.
    Lesson 3.6.9: Compartment syndrome.
    Lesson 3.6.10: Haematological effects (haemolysis and thrombocytopenia).
    Lesson 3.6.11: Malpositioning causing arterial occlusion
    Lesson 3.6.12: Too low → renal and mesenteric ischaemia.
    Lesson 3.6.13: Timing errors worsening myocardial oxygen supply- demand ratio.
    Lesson 3.6.14: Balloon rupture (if blood enters balloon lumen may clot and cause difficulty in removing).
    Lesson 3.6.15: Infection
    Lesson 3.6.16: Daily chest X‐rays are performed to check the catheter position.
  Mini Chapter 3.7: Weaning
    Lesson 3.7.1: Stable from the ischemic standpoint.
    Lesson 3.7.2: When the patient is ready to be weaned off, there is two ways for weaning
    Lesson 3.7.3: During the weaning process, assess for
    Lesson 3.7.4: Chest pain or ischaemic ECG changes
    Lesson 3.7.5: Pulmonary oedema
    Lesson 3.7.6: Confusion, tachycardia or hypotension
    Lesson 3.7.7: Reduced cardiac output and elevated filling pressures.
  Mini Chapter 3.8: Causes of lack of appropriate pressure augmentation   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 3.9: Complications   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 3.10: Contraindications
    Lesson 3.10.1: Moderate or severe AS or AI, as the Impella catheter may worsen the aortic pathology.
    Lesson 3.10.2: Implanted mechanical aortic valve.
    Lesson 3.10.3: Presence of LV thrombus.
    Lesson 3.10.4: Inability to anticoagulate patients.
    Lesson 3.10.5: Severe peripheral arterial disease is a relative contraindication.
  Mini Chapter 3.11: Hemodynamic differences between IABP and Impella
    Lesson 3.11.1: A membrane oxygenator can be added to the TandemHeart circuit to provide respiratory support.

  Mini Chapter 3.21: Types of ventricular assist devices   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 3.22: There are several pump designs   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 3.23: Continuous-flow LVADs have a number of major advantages over previous pulsatile technology
    Lesson 3.23.1: Continuous-flow pumps eliminate the need for a blood pumping chamber and volume compensation.
    Lesson 3.23.2: A lighter, smaller pump is better suited for patients with a smaller body size.
    Lesson 3.23.3: Simple designs include only 1 internal moving part, the rotor, and no internal valves.
    Lesson 3.23.4: They are silent in operation.
    Lesson 3.23.5: The benefits of the smaller percutaneous lead: reduced infection risk and greater patient comfort.
  Mini Chapter 3.24: The following LVAD parameters are displayed   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

  Mini Chapter 3.27: Contraindications for LVADs
    Lesson 3.27.1: Absolute
    Lesson 3.27.2: Coumarin intolerance.
    Lesson 3.27.3: Absence of trained caregivers (i.e living alone is LVAD contraindication).
    Lesson 3.27.4: Severe psychiatric disorders or non-adherence to the staff instructions.
    Lesson 3.27.5: Severe motor deficit or cognitive deficit related after stroke.
    Lesson 3.27.6: Neoplastic disease with unfavorable prognosis.
    Lesson 3.27.7: Vascular malformation of the small bowel that predisposes to bleeding.
    Lesson 3.27.8: Severe pulmonary obstructive disease.
    Lesson 3.27.9: Severe hepatic dysfunction.
    Lesson 3.27.10: Ventricular arrhythmias.
    Lesson 3.27.11: Active infection.
    Lesson 3.27.12: Hematologic changes (platelets < 50,000/mm3 and thrombophilia).
    Lesson 3.27.13: Relative
    Lesson 3.27.14: Difficult-to-control diabetes
    Lesson 3.27.15: Partial motor deficit after stroke
    Lesson 3.27.16: Severe malnutrition
    Lesson 3.27.17: Significative peripheral artery disease

  Mini Chapter 3.46: Indications
    Lesson 3.46.1: Advanced HF.
    Lesson 3.46.2: No other therapeutic option, except for LVAD as BTT.
  Mini Chapter 3.47: Contraindications
    Lesson 3.47.1: Severe peripheral arterial or cerebrovascular disease
    Lesson 3.47.2: Systemic disease with multiorgan involvement.
    Lesson 3.47.3: Other serious comorbidity with poor prognosis.
    Lesson 3.47.4: Current alcohol or drug abuse.
    Lesson 3.47.5: Insufficient social supports to achieve compliant care in the outpatient setting.

  Mini Chapter 3.51: Complications
    Lesson 3.51.1: The major types of rejection are
    Lesson 3.51.2: Mixed rejection where EMB displays both ACR and AMR, occurs in 8% of biopsies.
    Lesson 3.51.3: Treatment of rejection
    Lesson 3.51.4: A bolus dose of steroids and ATG are administered in patients with symptomatic rejection.
    Lesson 3.51.5: Intravenous heparin, as coronary microvascular thrombosis occurs in severe rejection.
    Lesson 3.51.6: For patients with AMR: Plasma exchange and I.V immunoglobulin are frequently administered.
    Lesson 3.51.7: Renal dysfunction, mainly from calcineurin inhibitors.

  Mini Chapter 4.75: Prognosis
    Lesson 4.75.1: The worst prognosis is seen with the following three cardiomyopathies: (HAD)
    Lesson 4.75.2: Some DCMs may be reversible (1)
    Lesson 4.75.3: DCM associated with inflammatory/immune response: Myocarditis, Peripartum cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 4.75.4: Besides GDMT, several additional predictors of reverse LV remodelling have been identified
    Lesson 4.75.5: Female sex, a non-ischaemic HF aetiology and the absence of digoxin use (IMPROVE-HF study).
    Lesson 4.75.6: Higher baseline LVEF.
    Lesson 4.75.7: Lower LV end-diastolic diameter.
    Lesson 4.75.8: Lower extent of LGE on CMR (indicative of lower interstitial replacement fibrosis).
    Lesson 4.75.9: TTN mutation (Higher rate of LV reverse remodeling in up to 70%).
    Lesson 4.75.10: Poor prognostic factors
    Lesson 4.75.11: Clinical: Male sex, advanced age (> 60 years), Black race, NYHA class (III-IV).
    Lesson 4.75.12: ECG: Presence of LBBB.
    Lesson 4.75.13: Biomarkers: Higher natriuretic peptide levels.
    Lesson 4.75.14: Biopsy-proven, Virus-negative post-myocarditis DCM.
    Lesson 4.75.15: Acute giant-cell and eosinophilic myocarditis
    Lesson 4.75.16: Cardiac sarcoidosis.
  Mini Chapter 4.76: Definition   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.77: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.78: Diagnosis
    Lesson 4.78.1: The following parameters suggest Arrhythmia-induced (vs Arrhythmia-aggravated) cardiomyopathy
    Lesson 4.78.2: Shorter intrinsic QRS duration.
    Lesson 4.78.3: Smaller LV end-diastolic diameter.
    Lesson 4.78.4: Absence of LGE on CMR (Presence of LGE suggests underlying structural heart disease).
    Lesson 4.78.5: LVEF improvement/normalization (reverse remodelling) following suppression of the arrhythmia.

  Mini Chapter 4.95: Definition   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.96: Pathology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.97: Diagnosis
    Lesson 4.97.1: VT of LBBB morphology with superior axis.
    Lesson 4.97.2: RV angiography: Regional RV akinesis, dyskinesis or aneurysm.
    Lesson 4.97.3: Family history
    Lesson 4.97.4: ARVC confirmed clinically or pathologically at autopsy in first degree relative.
    Lesson 4.97.5: Identification of pathogenic gene mutation associated with ARVC.
    Lesson 4.97.6: Pathogenic or likely pathogenic variants can be identified in up to 60% of patients with ARVC.
    Lesson 4.97.7: Indication
    Lesson 4.97.8: Must be offered to all patients with a suspected AC.
  Mini Chapter 4.98: Differential Diagnosis (ARVC phenocopies)   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.99: Treatment
    Lesson 4.99.1: HF treatment (ACEIs, diuretics) is used in patients who develop RV or biventricular dysfunction.
    Lesson 4.99.2: AAD therapy (amiodarone and flecainide) should be considered in case of recurrent symptomstic VT.
    Lesson 4.99.3: ICD implantation is indicated if
    Lesson 4.99.4: Arrhythmic syncope in patients with definitive ARVC.
    Lesson 4.99.5: RV and LV dysfunction (RV FAC ≤ 17% or RV EF ≤ 35%, LVEF ≤ 35%).

  Mini Chapter 4.107: Causes of RCM   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.108: Features of RCM
    Lesson 4.108.1: Morphological Features
    Lesson 4.108.2: LA and RA are markedly dilated and functional TR and MR, sometimes severe.
    Lesson 4.108.3: Imaging features
    Lesson 4.108.4: Tissue Doppler shows reduced early diastolic myocardial velocity (e′) leading to an elevated E/e′.
    Lesson 4.108.5: Hemodynamic features
    Lesson 4.108.6: The thickening frequently involves both ventricles, not just the LV.
  Mini Chapter 4.109: Genetic testing
    Lesson 4.109.1: When inherited, RCM most commonly presents as an autosomal dominant disorder.
  Mini Chapter 4.110: Management   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.111: Prognosis   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.112: Definition   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.113: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 4.113.1: It is usually seen at an age over 40-50.
    Lesson 4.113.2: It may be primary or secondary to bone-marrow plasma cell disorders e.g., multiple myeloma.
    Lesson 4.113.3: It is associated with amyloid renal failure and nephrotic syndrome.
  Mini Chapter 4.114: Diagnosis
    Lesson 4.114.1: Monoclonal protein screen
    Lesson 4.114.2: Cardiac scintigraphy with Tc-PYP followed by SPECT
  Mini Chapter 4.115: Treatment
    Lesson 4.115.1: Treatment of TTR-CA
    Lesson 4.115.2: Management of HF symptoms
    Lesson 4.115.3: Management of AF
  Mini Chapter 4.116: Prognosis
    Lesson 4.116.1: It is a multisystem disorder affecting particularly the heart, kidney, and brain.
    Lesson 4.116.2: Types: Two Anderson–Fabry phenotypes can be distinguished
    Lesson 4.116.3: Diagnosis
  Mini Chapter 4.117: Extracardiac red flags
    Lesson 4.117.1: No male-to-male transmission in pedigree
    Lesson 4.117.2: Renal involvement (dialysis, renal transplantation) or LVH in relatives
    Lesson 4.117.3: Albuminuria
    Lesson 4.117.4: Neuropathic pain
    Lesson 4.117.5: Angiokeratomas
    Lesson 4.117.6: Hypohidrosis, heat/cold and exercise intolerance
    Lesson 4.117.7: Cornea verticillata
    Lesson 4.117.8: Hearing loss (either progressive or sudden), tinnitus, vertigo
    Lesson 4.117.9: GI symptoms (nausea, vomiting, non-specific abdominal pain, constipation,
  Mini Chapter 4.118: Cardiac red flags
    Lesson 4.118.1: Short PQ interval in young patients
    Lesson 4.118.2: Atrioventricular blocks in adult patients
    Lesson 4.118.3: Bradycardia
    Lesson 4.118.4: Chronotropic incompetence
    Lesson 4.118.5: Hypertrophy of papillary muscles
    Lesson 4.118.6: Mitral and aortic valve thickening with mild-to-moderate
    Lesson 4.118.7: Reduced global longitudinal strain
    Lesson 4.118.8: Basal-inferolateral late gadolinium enhancement
    Lesson 4.118.9: Low native T1 (caution with ‘pseudonormalization’ in areas
    Lesson 4.118.10: High focal/global T2
    Lesson 4.118.11: Elevated high-sensitivity troponin
    Lesson 4.118.12: Elevated NT-proBNP
    Lesson 4.118.13: Global subendocardial LGE in amyloidosis.
    Lesson 4.118.14: Patchy mid‐wall enhancement in HCM.
    Lesson 4.118.15: Patchy mid‐wall or subepicardial enhancement in Fabry disease.
    Lesson 4.118.16: Diagnosis
    Lesson 4.118.17: Gene testing is recommended for diagnosis when phenotypic features are present.
    Lesson 4.118.18: Management
    Lesson 4.118.19: Pulmonary valvuloplasty may be considered in children and infants with severe RVOT obstruction.
    Lesson 4.118.20: Surgical myectomy and orthotopic heart transplantation may be considered.
    Lesson 4.118.21: There is no relationship between the extent of neurological involvement and cardiac phenotype.
    Lesson 4.118.22: Mitochondrial iron storage is the pathologic hallmark of the disease.
    Lesson 4.118.23: Short PR interval and increased ECG voltages may represent useful diagnostic clues for GSDs.

  Mini Chapter 4.139: Definition   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.140: Aetiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.141: Features of HCM
    Lesson 4.141.1: Asymmetry
    Lesson 4.141.2: Pattern of LVH in HCM
    Lesson 4.141.3: Sigmoid HCM (40-50%): septal protuberance basal concave septum
    Lesson 4.141.4: Reverse Curve HCM (30-40%): convex septum, concentric LV cavity.
    Lesson 4.141.5: Apical HCM (10%): apical hypertrophy “Ace of spades”.
    Lesson 4.141.6: Neutral HCM (10%): straight septum.
    Lesson 4.141.7: LVOT obstruction
    Lesson 4.141.8: The marked septal hypertrophy may also contribute to RVOT obstruction, particularly in children.
    Lesson 4.141.9: Causes of MR in HOCM
    Lesson 4.141.10: Mitral valve abnormalities aggravate SAM and LVOT obstruction
    Lesson 4.141.11: Extreme elongation and prolapse of the posterior mitral leaflet (~9% of operated HOCM).
    Lesson 4.141.12: Papillary muscle insertion directly onto the anterior leaflet.
    Lesson 4.141.13: Chordal rupture.
  Mini Chapter 4.142: Less common forms of HCM   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 4.143: Pathophysiology of heart failure in HCM
    Lesson 4.143.1: HCM due to sarcomere gene mutations
    Lesson 4.143.2: HCM due to storage disorders
  Mini Chapter 4.144: Natural history and mortality
    Lesson 4.144.1: HCM due to sarcomere gene mutations
    Lesson 4.144.2: HCM due to storage disorders
  Mini Chapter 4.145: Diagnosis
    Lesson 4.145.1: Symptoms
    Lesson 4.145.2: Most patients are asymptomatic and have a normal lifespan.
    Lesson 4.145.3: Dyspnea and HF may result from LVOT obstruction and/or LV diastolic dysfunction.
    Lesson 4.145.4: Syncope: Two types of syncope must be distinguished
    Lesson 4.145.5: A number of noncardiac symptoms act as pointers for specific diagnoses.

  Mini Chapter 4.161: Clinical features favouring hypertension only
    Lesson 4.161.1: Normal 12 lead ECG or isolated increased voltage without repolarisation abnormality.
    Lesson 4.161.2: Regression of LVH over 6-12 months after tight systolic blood pressure control (< 130 mmHg).
  Mini Chapter 4.162: Clinical features favouring hypertrophic cardiomyopathy
    Lesson 4.162.1: Family history of HCM.
    Lesson 4.162.2: RV hypertrophy.
    Lesson 4.162.3: LGE at the RV insertion points or localized to segments of maximum LV thickening on CMR.
    Lesson 4.162.4: Maximum LV wall thickness ≥ 15 mm (Caucasian); ≥ 20 mm (black).
    Lesson 4.162.5: Severe diastolic dysfunction.
    Lesson 4.162.6: Marked repolarisation abnormalities, conduction disease or Q-waves on 12 lead ECG.

    Lesson 4.167.1: Complications
    Lesson 4.167.2: AV block: 2% in surgical myectomy and 7–20% in alcoholic septal ablation.
    Lesson 4.167.3: Bundle branch block (LBBB in septal myectomy and RBBB in alcohol septal ablation),
    Lesson 4.167.4: Residual LVOT gradients (more in alcoholic septal ablation).
    Lesson 4.167.5: AF; or
    Lesson 4.167.6: AV sequential ventricular pacing

    Lesson 4.173.1: There is an increased risk of SCD in younger patients.
    Lesson 4.173.2: Sudden cardiac death is very rare below the age of 6 years.
    Lesson 4.173.3: Family history of SCD does not appear to be an independent risk factor for SCD in childhood HCM.
    Lesson 4.173.4: There is no evidence that the frequency, duration, or rate of NSVT influences the risk of SCD.
    Lesson 4.173.5: NSVT occurring during or immediately following exercise is very rare, but may be associated with a
    Lesson 4.173.6: 2020 AHA/ACC HCM guidelines consider it as a major independent SCD risk factor. However, ESC

Chapter 5: Chronic coronary syndrome
  Mini Chapter 5.1: Main features of CCS
    Lesson 5.1.1: Natural history: Stable symptomatic or asymptomatic phases which may be interrupted by ACS.
    Lesson 5.1.2: Clinical presentations
    Lesson 5.1.3: Asymptomatic
    Lesson 5.1.4: Ischaemic cardiomyopathy.
  Mini Chapter 5.2: Epidemiology
    Lesson 5.2.1: From 5-7% in women aged 45-64 years to 10-12% in women aged 65-84 and
    Lesson 5.2.2: From 4-7% in men aged 45-64 years to 12-14% in men aged 65-84.
  Mini Chapter 5.3: Prognosis   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 5.4: Angina caused by fixed coronary obstruction

  Mini Chapter 5.6: Risk assessment and diagnosis
    Lesson 5.6.1: Step 1: Symptoms and signs
  Mini Chapter 5.7: Meets the following three characteristics
    Lesson 5.7.1: Constricting discomfort in the front of the chest or in the
    Lesson 5.7.2: Precipitated by physical exertion;
    Lesson 5.7.3: Relieved by rest or nitrates within 5 min.
    Lesson 5.7.4: Unstable angina may present in one of three ways
    Lesson 5.7.5: Step 2: Comorbidities and other causes of symptoms
    Lesson 5.7.6: Step 3: Basic testing
  Mini Chapter 5.8: Basic biochemistry testing   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 5.9: The following blood tests are recommended in all patients
    Lesson 5.9.1: Full blood count (including haemoglobin);
    Lesson 5.9.2: Creatinine measurement and estimation of renal function;
    Lesson 5.9.3: A lipid profile (including LDL-C).
  Mini Chapter 5.10: Resting ECG   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

    Lesson 5.14.1: Exclusion of alternative causes of angina;
    Lesson 5.14.2: Identification of regional wall motion abnormalities suggestive of CAD;
    Lesson 5.14.3: Measurement of LVEF for risk stratification; and
    Lesson 5.14.4: Evaluation of diastolic function.
    Lesson 5.14.5: Step 4: Assessment of pre-test probability and clinical likelihood of CAD

    Lesson 5.16.1: Step 6: Assessment of event risk
  Mini Chapter 5.17: CCS
    Lesson 5.17.1: Normal stress test (given adequate stress): 1 year
    Lesson 5.17.2: Normal coronary angiogram: 2 years
    Lesson 5.17.3: CCTA with no stenosis or plaque: 2 years
  Mini Chapter 5.18: Treatment
    Lesson 5.18.1: Event prevention: Anitplatelet drugs - Lipid lowering agents - RAAS blockers.
    Lesson 5.18.2: Smoking
    Lesson 5.18.3: Increase consumption of fruits and vegetables (≥ 200 g each per day).
    Lesson 5.18.4: 35-45 g of fibre per day, preferably from wholegrains.
    Lesson 5.18.5: Moderate consumption of nuts (30 g per day, unsalted).
    Lesson 5.18.6: 12 servings of fish per week (one to be oily fish).
    Lesson 5.18.7: Limited lean meat, low-fat dairy products, and liquid vegetable oils.
    Lesson 5.18.8: Saturated fats to account for < 10% of total energy intake; replace with polyunsaturated fats.
    Lesson 5.18.9: ≤ 5-6 g of salt per day.
    Lesson 5.18.10: If alcohol is consumed, limiting intake to ≤ 100 g/week or < 15 g/day is recommended.
    Lesson 5.18.11: Avoid energy-dense foods such as sugar-sweetened soft drinks.
  Mini Chapter 5.19: Physical activity
    Lesson 5.19.1: Sexual activity
    Lesson 5.19.2: Weight management
    Lesson 5.19.3: Lipid management
    Lesson 5.19.4: The goals of treatment are LDL-C < 55 mg/dL and > 50% LDL-C reduction.
    Lesson 5.19.5: Arterial Hypertension Management.
    Lesson 5.19.6: Diabetes and other disorders
  Mini Chapter 5.20: B-blockers
    Lesson 5.20.1: B-blockers act directly on the heart to reduce heart rate, contractility, and ectopic activity.
    Lesson 5.20.2: Calcium channel blockers
    Lesson 5.20.3: Except for nifedipine, DHPs are not contraindicated in bradycardia or HF (1).
    Lesson 5.20.4: Examples: amlodipine, felodipine, and nifedipine XL.
    Lesson 5.20.5: Doses: amlodipine 2.5-10 mg Qday (same for felodipine); nifedipine XL 30-90 mg Qday.
    Lesson 5.20.6: Nitrates
    Lesson 5.20.7: Ivabradine
    Lesson 5.20.8: Nicorandil
    Lesson 5.20.9: Long-term use of oral nicorandil may stabilize coronary plaque in patients with stable angina.
    Lesson 5.20.10: Occasional side-effects include: oral, intestinal and perianal ulceration.
  Mini Chapter 5.21: Ranolazine
    Lesson 5.21.1: Ranolazine clearance is reduced by renal and hepatic impairment.
    Lesson 5.21.2: Trimetazidine
    Lesson 5.21.3: It has similar anti-anginal efficacy to propranolol in doses of 20 mg twice daily.
    Lesson 5.21.4: In diabetic persons, trimetazidine improved HbA1c and glycaemia.
  Mini Chapter 5.22: General considerations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

    Lesson 6.2.3: Angiographic findings consistent with a procedural flow-limiting complication such as coronary

Chapter 7: ST elevation myocardial infarction
  Mini Chapter 7.1: Epidemiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 7.2: Emergency care
    Lesson 7.2.1: A 12-lead ECG should be obtained as soon as possible at the point of FMC
    Lesson 7.2.2: Typically, ST-elevation measured at the J point, should be found in two contiguous leads and be
    Lesson 7.2.3: ≥ 0.25 mV in men < 40 years, ≥ 0.2 mV in men > 40 years, or ≥ 0.15 mV in women in leads V2-V3.
    Lesson 7.2.4: ≥ 0.1 mV in other leads (in the absence of LVH or LBBB).
    Lesson 7.2.5: Isolated posterior MI
    Lesson 7.2.6: Left main coronary obstruction or multivessel disease
  Mini Chapter 7.3: ECG monitoring   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

  Mini Chapter 7.37: It is recommended that ACS patients adopt a healthy lifestyle, including
    Lesson 7.37.1: Stopping all smoking of tobacco (1)
    Lesson 7.37.2: Healthy diet (Mediterranean style)
    Lesson 7.37.3: Alcohol restriction (max. 100 g/week; same limit for men and women).
    Lesson 7.37.4: Regular aerobic physical activity and resistance exercise
    Lesson 7.37.5: Reduced sedentary time
  Mini Chapter 7.38: Lipid-lowering drugs   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 7.39: RAAS system inhibitors   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 7.40: Beta-blockers   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 7.41: Proton pump inhibitors   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 7.42: Anti-inflammatory drugs
    Lesson 7.42.1: Differential diagnosis
    Lesson 7.42.2: Arrhythmias (inappropriate bradycardia, advanced AV block, VT).
    Lesson 7.42.3: Treatment of RV shock (beside emergent reperfusion)
    Lesson 7.42.4: Hypoxemia should be aggressively treated as hypoxemia increases PVR and RV afterload.
    Lesson 7.42.5: Inhaled NO has shown to reduce RA pressure and PVR, and increase stroke volume, in RV MI.
    Lesson 7.42.6: Severe mitral regurgitation (MR)
    Lesson 7.42.7: Mechanism
    Lesson 7.42.8: Posterior leaflet tethering – A degree of ischemic MR is seen in ~30% of acute MI.
    Lesson 7.42.9: Treatment
    Lesson 7.42.10: Ventricular septal rupture (VSR): [~1% of MIs (only 0.2% of reperfused MIs)].
    Lesson 7.42.11: Free wall rupture
    Lesson 7.42.12: Dynamic LVOT obstruction: [1–2% of MIs and up to 12% of anterior MIs in women].
    Lesson 7.42.13: Escalate β‐blockers, NTG, and readminister anticoagulants.
    Lesson 7.42.14: VF and sudden death: There are three types of VF (mostly characterized in fibrinolytic trials)
    Lesson 7.42.15: Sustained VT: There are two types of VT
    Lesson 7.42.16: Non‐sustained VT (NSVT)
    Lesson 7.42.17: Early NSVT (< 48 hours) is not associated with any impairment of short‐ or long‐term survival.
    Lesson 7.42.18: Late NSVT is associated with impaired long‐term survival.
    Lesson 7.42.19: Accelerated idioventricular rhythm = slow wide ventricular rhythm at a rate of 60–100 (120) bpm.
  Mini Chapter 7.43: Treatment
    Lesson 7.43.1: The sinus nodal artery originates from the proximal RCA (60%) or the LCx (40%).
    Lesson 7.43.2: The right bundle mainly has a single arterial supply from the LAD (first septal branch).
    Lesson 7.43.3: The left anterior fascicle has a single arterial supply from the LAD (first septal branch).
    Lesson 7.43.4: Dyskinesis without an aneurysm is much more common than a true aneurysm.
    Lesson 7.43.5: An aneurysm leads to increased preload and afterload, and a double mortality for the same EF.
    Lesson 7.43.6: On imaging, the distinction between a true LV aneurysm and a pseudoaneurysm is
    Lesson 7.43.7: MRI may be used in equivocal cases and shows loss of epicardial fat across the pseudoaneurysm.
    Lesson 7.43.8: Treatment
  Mini Chapter 7.44: Atrial fibrillation   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

Chapter 8: Non ST elevation ACS
  Mini Chapter 8.1: Definition
    Lesson 8.1.1: Rest angina.
    Lesson 8.1.2: Angina occurring within 2 weeks after a myocardial infarction (post‐infarction angina).
  Mini Chapter 8.2: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 8.3: The pathogenesis of NSTE-ACS involves four processes operating singly or in various combinations
    Lesson 8.3.1: Cardiac causes of secondary NSTE-ACS include: severe hypertension, acute HF, tachyarrhythmias.
    Lesson 8.3.2: Non‐cardiac causes of secondary NSTE-ACS include: GI bleed, severe anemia, hypoxia, sepsis.
  Mini Chapter 8.4: Diagnosis
    Lesson 8.4.1: Clinical presentation and ECG
    Lesson 8.4.2: Patients with acute chest pain and persistent (> 20 min) ST-elevation. This is termed (STEMI).
    Lesson 8.4.3: Biomarkers
  Mini Chapter 8.5: Compared with standard cardiac troponin assays, hs-cTn assays
    Lesson 8.5.1: Have higher NPV for AMI.
    Lesson 8.5.2: Reduce the ‘troponin-blind’ interval leading to earlier detection of AMI.
    Lesson 8.5.3: Are associated with a 2-fold increase in the detection of type 2 MI.
    Lesson 8.5.4: Elevations beyond 5-fold the upper reference limit have high (> 90%) PPV for acute type 1 MI.
    Lesson 8.5.5: Elevations up to 3-fold the upper reference limit have only limited (50-60%) PPV for AMI.
    Lesson 8.5.6: It is common to detect circulating levels of cardiac troponin in healthy individuals.
    Lesson 8.5.7: Rapid ‘rule-in’ and ‘rule-out’ algorithms
    Lesson 8.5.8: Non-invasive imaging
    Lesson 8.5.9: Functional evaluation
    Lesson 8.5.10: Stress echocardiography has demonstrated superior prognostic value over exercise ECG.
    Lesson 8.5.11: SPECT is useful for the risk stratification of patients with acute chest pain suggestive of ACS.
    Lesson 8.5.12: Anatomical evaluation
    Lesson 8.5.13: CCTA allows visualization of the coronary arteries, and a normal scan excludes CAD.
  Mini Chapter 8.6: Diagnosis and risk stratification   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 8.7: Imaging   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

    Lesson 8.10.2: Age ≥ 75 years
    Lesson 8.10.3: Liver cirrhosis with portal hypertension

    Lesson 8.10.5: Severe or end-stage CKD (eGFR < 30 mL/min)
    Lesson 8.10.6: Hb 11-12.9 g/dL for men or 11-11.9 g/dL for women
    Lesson 8.10.7: Spontaneous bleeding requiring hospitalization - Spontaneous bleeding requiring hospitalization
    Lesson 8.10.8: Presence of a brain arteriovenous malformation - Chronic use of oral NSAIDS or steroids
    Lesson 8.10.9: Previous spontaneous intracranial haemorrhage - Any ischaemic stroke at any time not meeting the
    Lesson 8.10.10: Previous traumatic intracranial haemorrhage
    Lesson 8.10.11: Moderate or severe ischaemic stroke (NIHSS score
    Lesson 8.10.12: Major surgery or major trauma within 30 days
    Lesson 8.10.13: Active malignancy ( 1 ) (excluding non-melanoma

    Lesson 8.10.15: Baseline thrombocytopenia (plt before PCI < 100 x
    Lesson 8.10.16: Anticipated use of long-term OAC (2)

    Lesson 9.17.1: Loading dose (LD) of 150-300 mg orally or 75-250 mg i.v. if oral ingestion is not possible,
    Lesson 9.17.2: No specific dose adjustment in CKD patients.
    Lesson 9.17.3: LD of 300-600 mg orally, followed by a MD of 75 mg o.d.
    Lesson 9.17.4: At the time of fibrinolysis: initial dose of 300 mg (75 mg for patients > 75 years of age).
    Lesson 9.17.5: LD of 60 mg orally, followed by a MD of 10 mg o.d. (reduced to 5 mg o.d. if BW < 60 kg).
    Lesson 9.17.6: In patients aged ≥ 75 years, prasugrel should be used with caution, but a dose of 5 mg o.d.
    Lesson 9.17.7: Prior stroke is a contraindication for prasugrel.
    Lesson 9.17.8: LD of 180 mg orally, followed by a MD of 90 mg b.i.d.
    Lesson 9.17.9: No specific adjustment in CKD patients.
    Lesson 9.17.10: Bolus of 30 mcg/kg i.v. followed by 4 mcg/kg/min infusion for at least 2 h or the duration

    Lesson 9.17.12: Ticagrelor (LD 180 mg) should be administered at the time of PCI to minimize the potential
    Lesson 9.17.13: Double bolus of 180 mcg/kg i.v. (given at a 10-min interval) followed by an infusion of 2.0
    Lesson 9.17.14: For CrCl 30-50 mL/min: first LD, 180 mcg/kg i.v. bolus (max 22.6 mg); maintenance
    Lesson 9.17.15: Contraindicated in patients with end-stage renal disease and with prior ICH, ischaemic

  Mini Chapter 9.18: Anticoagulant drugs (for use before and during PCI)
    Lesson 9.18.1: Initial treatment: i.v. bolus 70-100 U/kg then infusion titrated to achieve aPTT of 60-80s.
    Lesson 9.18.2: During PCI: 70–100 U/kg i.v. bolus or according to ACT in case of UFH pre-treatment.
    Lesson 9.18.3: Initial treatment: for treatment of ACS 1 mg/kg b.i.d. subcutaneously for a minimum of 2
    Lesson 9.18.4: In patients whose CrCl < 30 mL/min (by Cockcroft–Gault equation), the dosage should be
    Lesson 9.18.5: During PCI: for patients managed with PCI, if the last dose of enoxaparin was given less

    Lesson 9.18.7: During PPCI: 0.75 mg/kg i.v. bolus followed by 1.75 mg/kg/h for 4 h after the procedure.
    Lesson 9.18.8: In patients whose CrCl < 30 mL/min (by Cockcroft–Gault equation), maintenance infusion
    Lesson 9.18.9: During PCI: A single bolus of UFH is recommended.
    Lesson 9.18.10: Avoid if CrCl < 20 mL/min.

    Lesson 9.19.5: Haemoglobin < 11 g/dL

    Lesson 9.19.7: Baseline thrombocytopenia (plt < 100 x 10 /L)
    Lesson 9.19.8: Chronic use of oral NSAIDS or steroids
    Lesson 9.19.9: Chronic bleeding diathesis
    Lesson 9.19.10: Any ischaemic stroke at any time not meeting the
    Lesson 9.19.11: Liver cirrhosis with portal hypertension
    Lesson 9.19.12: Active malignancy ( 3 ) (excluding non-melanoma skin
    Lesson 9.19.13: Previous spontaneous intracranial haemorrhage
    Lesson 9.19.14: Previous traumatic intracranial haemorrhage within the
    Lesson 9.19.15: Presence of a brain arteriovenous malformation
    Lesson 9.19.16: Moderate or severe ischaemic stroke (1) within the past 6
    Lesson 9.19.17: Major surgery or major trauma within 30 days prior to PCI
    Lesson 9.19.18: Non-deferrable major surgery on DAPT
    Lesson 9.19.19: Before PCI
    Lesson 9.19.20: In VKA-treated patients, no parenteral anticoagulation is needed if the INR is > 2.5.
    Lesson 9.19.21: After PCI
    Lesson 9.19.22: Default strategy
    Lesson 9.19.23: Followed by Dual Antithrombotic with OAC and SAPT (preferably clopidogrel) for up to 12 months.
    Lesson 9.19.24: Dose of OAC
    Lesson 9.19.25: NOAC should be taken at the recommended dose for stroke prevention.
    Lesson 9.19.26: Regarding duration of TAT and DAT
    Lesson 9.19.27: In patients with multiple HBR factors: DAT may be shortened to 6 months.
  Mini Chapter 9.20: During PCI, a UFH bolus is recommended in any of the following circumstances

    Lesson 9.20.2: Radial artery approach as default vascular access

    Lesson 11.18.4: Complications

  Mini Chapter 11.21: Mechanisms of mitral regurgitation   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 11.22: There are four major mechanisms of mitral regurgitation (Carpentier’s classification)
    Lesson 11.22.1: Type I: Normal leaflet motion.
    Lesson 11.22.2: Type II: Excessive leaflet motion
    Lesson 11.22.3: Type IIIa: Restricted leaflet motion during diastole and systole (i.e restricted opening)
    Lesson 11.22.4: Type IIIb: Restricted leaflet motion during systole (i.e restricted closure)
  Mini Chapter 11.23: Three additional mechanisms contribute to MR
    Lesson 11.23.1: Annular dilatation, which reduces leaflet coaptation.
  Mini Chapter 11.24: Causes of mitral regurgitation   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 11.25: Causes of acute MR
    Lesson 11.25.1: Papillary muscle rupture or acute functional MR occurring in the context of an acute MI.
    Lesson 11.25.2: Chordae tendinae rupture. Causes: idiopathic, mitral valve prolapse, endocarditis, trauma.
    Lesson 11.25.3: Acute functional MR secondary to an acute ventricular process (MI, myocarditis).
    Lesson 11.25.4: Primary MR: [Primary lesion of one or more components of the mitral valve apparatus]
    Lesson 11.25.5: Mitral Valve Prolapse (MVP)
    Lesson 11.25.6: There are two forms of MVP
    Lesson 11.25.7: MVP occurs in 1-2% of the population with a familial trend. Female‐to‐male ratio= 2:1.
    Lesson 11.25.8: MVP may be associated with tricuspid prolapse (in ~40%) and aortic valve prolapse (in ~10%).
    Lesson 11.25.9: 15% of MVP cases progress to severe MR at a mean follow‐up duration of 15 years.
    Lesson 11.25.10: Complications in serious MVP subgroup: (1) severe MR; (2) endocarditis; (3) VT/sudden death.
    Lesson 11.25.11: Secondary (Functional) MR
    Lesson 11.25.12: Other causes of MR
    Lesson 11.25.13: Systolic anterior motion of the anterior leaflet and chordae in HOCM.
    Lesson 11.25.14: Causes of transient severe MR

  Mini Chapter 11.36: Aetiology
    Lesson 11.36.1: Age‐related calcific degeneration (most common cause)
    Lesson 11.36.2: Bicuspid aortic valve (BAV)
    Lesson 11.36.3: BAV is the most common congenital heart disease (~1.3% of the population).
    Lesson 11.36.4: Patients with BAV have a high risk of ascending aortic dilatation or dissection.
    Lesson 11.36.5: Patients with BAV should undergo, at least once, a screening with CT to assess the whole aorta.

  Mini Chapter 11.40: Other features suggestive of severe AS
    Lesson 11.40.1: Dimensionless index
    Lesson 11.40.2: Peak LVOT velocity is < 1 m/s.
    Lesson 11.40.3: LVOT velocity/aortic valve velocity is ≤ 0.25.
  Mini Chapter 11.41: Pitfalls
    Lesson 11.41.1: Like any velocity assessment, it is angle‐dependent.
  Mini Chapter 11.42: Additional diagnostic and prognostic parameters
    Lesson 11.42.1: Catheterization
  Mini Chapter 11.43: Categories of Aortic Stenosis
    Lesson 11.43.1: Low‐gradient AS with AVA ≤ 1 cm2 and low EF < 50%
    Lesson 11.43.2: In case of mean gradient < 30 mmHg, AVA ≤ 1cm2, and EF < 50% (esp. < 40%), the patient may have
    Lesson 11.43.3: Truly severe AS with a low gradient resulting from the low cardiac output, or
    Lesson 11.43.4: Low‐gradient AS with AVA ≤ 1 cm2 but Normal EF (Paradoxical low‐flow/Low‐gradient Severe AS)
    Lesson 11.43.5: Severe preload reduction (e.g., hypovolemia)
    Lesson 11.43.6: Associated mitral valve disease that reduces the flow across the aortic valve.
    Lesson 11.43.7: 3rd step: Measure the stroke volume index and valvuloarterial impedance.
    Lesson 11.43.8: Typical symptoms without explanation.
    Lesson 11.43.9: Elderly patient (> 70 years).
    Lesson 11.43.10: LVH (additional history of Hypertension to be considered).
    Lesson 11.43.11: Reduced LV longitudinal function without other explanation.
    Lesson 11.43.12: Mean gradient 30-40 mmHg (1)
    Lesson 11.43.13: AVA ≤ 0.8 cm2
    Lesson 11.43.14: Low flow (SVi < 35 ml/m2) confirmed by techniques other than standard
    Lesson 11.43.15: Calcium Score by MSCT
    Lesson 11.43.16: Severe AS very likely: Men ≥ 3000, Women ≥ 1600
    Lesson 11.43.17: Severe AS likely: Men ≥ 2000, Women ≥ 1200
    Lesson 11.43.18: Severe AS unlikely: Men <1600, Women < 800
  Mini Chapter 11.44: Notes
    Lesson 11.44.1: Diagnosis of subvalvular or supravalvular AS
    Lesson 11.44.2: Subvalvular AS
    Lesson 11.44.3: It is commonly associated with VSD.
    Lesson 11.44.4: Supravalvular AS
    Lesson 11.44.5: Aortic valve thickening and regurgitation may develop.
    Lesson 11.44.6: This condition is frequently associated with peripheral pulmonary artery stenosis.
    Lesson 11.44.7: Surgical therapy is indicated if: Symptomatic, LVH, or Asymptomatic with mean gradient > 50 mmHg.

  Mini Chapter 11.105: Complications
    Lesson 11.105.1: Bioprosthetic valve degeneration depends on
    Lesson 11.105.2: PV thrombosis is a complex multifactorial phenomenon, occurs due to
    Lesson 11.105.3: Hemodynamic Factors: e.g., Low cardiac output, prosthetic hemodynamic profile, or hyperviscosity.
    Lesson 11.105.4: Hemostatic Factors: e.g., hypercoagulable state, significant tissue injury.
    Lesson 11.105.5: It presents with HF, hemodynamic compromise and loss of the prosthetic click on exam.
    Lesson 11.105.6: Causes: (all these processes may also be seen with a bioprosthetic valve, at a lower frequency)
    Lesson 11.105.7: Thrombosis (more common than pannus formation).
    Lesson 11.105.8: Early postoperative prosthetic obstruction may be due to severe annular calcification.
    Lesson 11.105.9: A paraprosthetic leak is observed with both mechanical and bioprosthetic valves.
    Lesson 11.105.10: EOAPr= CSALVOT x VTILVOT / VTIPr
    Lesson 11.105.11: They often do well in the intermediate term but have an impaired long‐term survival and prognosis.
    Lesson 11.105.12: PPM is severe or associated with moderate or severe valve stenosis.
    Lesson 11.105.13: Mean transprosthetic gradient is high (30-35 mmHg).
    Lesson 11.105.14: If patient develops heart failure symptoms or LV systolic dysfunction.
    Lesson 11.105.15: CABG: ~1–5% - Redo CABG: ~10%
    Lesson 11.105.16: MVR: ~6%
    Lesson 11.105.17: MVR in patients > 70 years: 14% - MVR + CABG: 11% (2) - MV repair: ~1–3%
    Lesson 11.105.18: AVR: ~3–4% (up to 8% in patients with low EF) - AVR+CABG: 6% - AVR in octogenarians: ~6–9%
    Lesson 11.105.19: Multiple valve replacement: 9%
    Lesson 11.105.20: Redo valve replacement

    Lesson 12.22.1: Empirical antimicrobial therapy: The initial choice of empirical treatment depends on
    Lesson 12.22.2: Previous antibiotic therapy.
    Lesson 12.22.3: IE in a native valve or a prosthesis (and if so, when surgery was performed [early vs. late PVE]).
    Lesson 12.22.4: The place of the infection (community, nosocomial, or non-nosocomial healthcare-associated IE).
    Lesson 12.22.5: Local epidemiology, especially for antibiotic resistance and culture-negative pathogens.
    Lesson 12.22.6: Duration of antimicrobial therapy
    Lesson 12.22.7: Drug treatment of PVE should last longer (≥ 6 weeks) than that of NVE (2-6 weeks).
    Lesson 12.22.8: Different strategies between NVE and PVE
    Lesson 12.22.9: NVE and late PVE regimens should cover staphylococci, streptococci, and enterococci.
    Lesson 12.22.10: Coagulase negative staph. should be empirically covered in PVE but not in NVE.
    Lesson 12.22.11: In staphylococcal PVE, the regimen should include rifampin whenever the strain is susceptible.
    Lesson 12.22.12: Notes regarding specific antibiotics

    Lesson 12.38.6: Antimicrobial therapy
    Lesson 12.38.7: Surgery
    Lesson 12.38.8: Tricuspid valve repair is more frequently performed than replacement.
    Lesson 12.38.9: Indications for surgical treatment of right-sided IE
    Lesson 12.38.10: RV dysfunction secondary to acute severe TR non-responsive to diuretics.
    Lesson 12.38.11: Persistent vegetation with respiratory insufficiency requiring ventilatory support
    Lesson 12.38.12: Large residual tricuspid vegetations (> 20 mm) after recurrent septic pulmonary
    Lesson 12.38.13: Patients with simultaneous involvement of left-heart structures.
    Lesson 12.38.14: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 12.38.15: Diagnosis
    Lesson 12.38.16: As in NVE, diagnosis of PVE is based mainly on the results of echocardiography and blood cultures.
    Lesson 12.38.17: Prognosis
    Lesson 12.38.18: Endocarditis following TAVI
    Lesson 12.38.19: Predisposing factors: younger age, male gender, renal dysfunction, and significant residual AR.
    Lesson 12.38.20: Enterococci and S. aureus are the two most common microorganisms in IE post-TAVI.
    Lesson 12.38.21: Nuclear imaging or CT have been useful to diagnose IE post-TAVI.
    Lesson 12.38.22: Endocarditis following transcatheter pulmonary valve implantation (TPVI)
    Lesson 12.38.23: S. aureus and oral group streptococci are the most common microorganisms causing IE post-TPVI.
    Lesson 12.38.24: Pathophysiology: CIED-related IE occurs by two mechanisms
    Lesson 12.38.25: Prophylaxis
    Lesson 12.38.26: A chest X-ray or CT should be performed to evaluate the presence of pulmonary complications.
    Lesson 12.38.27: Management
  Mini Chapter 12.39: System extraction   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 12.40: Antibiotics duration   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 12.41: Reimplantation
    Lesson 12.41.1: The mitral valve was more often affected (62%) than the aortic valve (24%).

Chapter 13: Rheumatic fever
  Mini Chapter 13.1: Pathogenesis
    Lesson 13.1.1: Aschoff bodies describes the granulomatous nodules found in rheumatic heart fever.
  Mini Chapter 13.2: Diagnosis   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 13.3: Evidence of recent streptococcal infection
    Lesson 13.3.1: Raised or rising streptococci antibodies
    Lesson 13.3.2: Positive throat swab
    Lesson 13.3.3: Positive rapid group A streptococcal antigen test
  Mini Chapter 13.4: Major criteria   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 13.5: Minor criteria
    Lesson 13.5.1: 2 major criteria
    Lesson 13.5.2: 1 major with 2 minor criteria
    Lesson 13.5.3: Manifestations
    Lesson 13.5.4: Arthritis
    Lesson 13.5.5: The arthritis of rheumatic fever responds promptly to NSAIDs within 48 hrs.
    Lesson 13.5.6: Radiographs may show features of a joint effusion, but no other abnormalities are noted.
    Lesson 13.5.7: Carditis
    Lesson 13.5.8: Sydenham Chorea
    Lesson 13.5.9: Chorea may be the only presenting manifestation of rheumatic fever. It is more common in females.
    Lesson 13.5.10: It usually lasts for 8 to 15 weeks (may last for 1 week to 2 years).
    Lesson 13.5.11: Chorea does not occur simultaneously with arthritis but may coexist with carditis.
    Lesson 13.5.12: Subcutaneous Nodules
    Lesson 13.5.13: Erythema Marginatum
    Lesson 13.5.14: Erythema marginatum is suggestive of coexisting carditis.
    Lesson 13.5.15: Investigations
    Lesson 13.5.16: Recommended for All Cases
    Lesson 13.5.17: White blood cell count
    Lesson 13.5.18: Throat swab for GAS culture before giving antibiotics.
    Lesson 13.5.19: Blood culture, if febrile
    Lesson 13.5.20: ECG: the most common finding is PR prolongation and sinus tachycardia.
    Lesson 13.5.21: Chest radiograph
    Lesson 13.5.22: Echocardiogram
    Lesson 13.5.23: Tests for Alternative Diagnoses, Depending on Clinical Features
    Lesson 13.5.24: Repeated blood cultures with temperature spikes if infective endocarditis is suspected
    Lesson 13.5.25: Joint aspirate for possible septic arthritis (microscopy and culture)
    Lesson 13.5.26: Copper, ceruloplasmin, antinuclear antibody, and drug screen for choreiform movements
    Lesson 13.5.27: Serology and autoimmune markers for arboviral, autoimmune, or reactive arthritis
    Lesson 13.5.28: Peripheral blood smear for sickle cell disease.
  Mini Chapter 13.6: Treatment   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 13.7: The aim of treatment of a proven attack of rheumatic fever is to
    Lesson 13.7.1: Eradicate the GAS from the pharynx.
    Lesson 13.7.2: Provide symptomatic relief.
    Lesson 13.7.3: Commence secondary prophylaxis.

    Lesson 14.7.1: VT/flutter

    Lesson 14.8.3: Pre-excited AF
    Lesson 14.8.4: Polymorphic VT
    Lesson 14.8.5: Torsade de pointes
    Lesson 14.8.6: Ventricular fibrillation
  Mini Chapter 14.9: Approach to Narrow QRS complex tachycardias
    Lesson 14.9.1: Inadequate dose/delivery
    Lesson 14.9.2: High septal VT
    Lesson 14.9.3: Sinus tachycardia
    Lesson 14.9.4: Automated focal AT
    Lesson 14.9.5: Junctional ectopic tachycardia
    Lesson 14.9.6: Sinus nodal reentry
    Lesson 14.9.7: Triggered focal AT
    Lesson 14.9.8: Micro-re-enterant focal AT
  Mini Chapter 14.10: Approach to wide QRS complex tachycardias
    Lesson 14.10.1: DD of a wide QRS complex tachycardia includes
    Lesson 14.10.2: Hyperkalemia;
    Lesson 14.10.3: Drug toxicity (class I antiarrhythmic agents, tricyclics);
    Lesson 14.10.4: Ventricular pacemaker tracking an atrial arrhythmia (lack of mode switch), or
    Lesson 14.10.5: Pacemaker‐ mediated tachycardia.
    Lesson 14.10.6: Features characteristic of VT versus SVT with aberrancy
    Lesson 14.10.7: QRS morphology inconsistent with a typical RBBB or LBBB.
    Lesson 14.10.8: Onset of R‐to‐nadir of S > 100 ms in any precordial lead.
    Lesson 14.10.9: QRS complexes outnumber P waves (with AV association or AV dissociation).
    Lesson 14.10.10: Deep Q wave in V4–V6, is particularly suggestive of VT.
    Lesson 14.10.11: A tachycardia that starts with a PVC and has a morphology similar to this PVC is VT.
    Lesson 14.10.12: In VT, the QRS complex is wide in its initial portion and has a slow initial upslope or downslope.
  Mini Chapter 14.11: Characters supportive of SVT   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 14.12: AF with pre-excitation is diagnosed when
    Lesson 14.12.1: AF has VT morphological features; or
    Lesson 14.12.2: AF is wide and polymorphic (QRS varies in height and width), bizarre looking, or
    Lesson 14.12.3: AF is very fast (> 200 bpm).
    Lesson 14.12.4: Absence of RS in precordial leads
    Lesson 14.12.5: RS >100 ms in any lead (1)
    Lesson 14.12.6: Initial R wave
    Lesson 14.12.7: Initial R or Q wave > 40 ms
    Lesson 14.12.8: Presence of a notch of a predominantly
    Lesson 14.12.9: Lead V1: Monophasic R, Rsr’, biphasic qR
    Lesson 14.12.10: Lead V6: R:S ratio < 1 (rS, QS patterns)
    Lesson 14.12.11: Lead V1: Broad R wave, slurred or notched-down
    Lesson 14.12.12: Lead V6: Q or QS wave
  Mini Chapter 14.13: Any of the following criteria favors VT
    Lesson 14.13.1: R taller then R’
    Lesson 14.13.2: Initial R > 40 msec
    Lesson 14.13.3: Slurred or notched S in V1
    Lesson 14.13.4: Beginning of Q or QS in V6
  Mini Chapter 14.14: Acute management in the absence of an established diagnosis
    Lesson 14.14.1: Regular Narrow QRS (120 ms) tachycardias
    Lesson 14.14.2: Regular Wide QRS (>120 ms) tachycardias
  Mini Chapter 14.15: Hemodynamically unstable patients

    Lesson 15.4.1: Orthodromic (including PJRT): anterograde conduction occurs via the AV node, with retrograde
    Lesson 15.4.2: Rate usually 200-300 bpm

    Lesson 15.4.4: QRS < 120 ms unless pre-existing bundle branch block, or rate-related aberrant conduction
    Lesson 15.4.5: QRS alternans: phasic variation in QRS amplitude associated with AVNRT and AVRT, (distinguished
    Lesson 15.4.6: Wide QRS complexes due to abnormal ventricular depolarisation via accessory pathway

    Lesson 16.11.1: 20-30% of ischemic strokes
    Lesson 16.11.2: Cardioembolic
    Lesson 16.11.3: 10% of cryptogenic strokes
    Lesson 16.11.4: AF is present in 4% of patients with HF NYHA I
    Lesson 16.11.5: > 60% of patients have impaired QoL
    Lesson 16.11.6: 17% have disabling symptoms.
    Lesson 16.11.7: Related to comorbid vascular atheroma.

    Lesson 16.11.9: Brain white matter lesions, inflammation
    Lesson 16.11.10: Hypoperfusion, Microembolism

  Mini Chapter 16.18: Contraindications/precautions/comments
    Lesson 16.18.1: Should not be used in ischemic heart disease and/or significant structural heart disease.
    Lesson 16.18.2: May induce hypotension, AFL with 1:1 conduction (in 3.5-5 % of patients)
    Lesson 16.18.3: Flecainide may induce mild QRS complex widening
    Lesson 16.18.4: Don’t use for pharmacological cardioversion of AFL.

    Lesson 16.19.2: May cause arterial hypotension, QT prolongation, QRS widening or non-sustained VT.
  Mini Chapter 16.20: Contraindications/precautions/comments
    Lesson 16.20.1: May cause hypotension, bradycardia/AV block, QT prolongation.
  Mini Chapter 16.21: Contraindications/precautions/comments

    Lesson 16.21.2: Should not be used in patients with prolonged QT, severe LVH or low LVEF.
    Lesson 16.21.3: Should be used in the setting of a CCU as it may cause QT prolongation, polymorphic VT (torsade de
    Lesson 16.21.4: ECG monitoring for at least 4 hrs after administration to detect a proarrhythmic event.

Chapter 17: Ventricular Arrhythmia
  Mini Chapter 17.1: Definitions and Classification   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 17.2: Ventricular arrhythmia subtypes
    Lesson 17.2.1: Unifocal or monomorphic PVCs: PVCs with a single QRS morphology.
    Lesson 17.2.2: Multifocal, multiform, or polymorphic PVCs: PVCs with different QRS morphologies.
    Lesson 17.2.3: Short-coupled PVC: A PVC that interrupts the T-wave of the preceding conducted beat.
  Mini Chapter 17.3: VT can be classified according to duration
    Lesson 17.3.1: Non-sustained VT (NSVT): Run of consecutive ventricular beats persisting for 3 beats to 30 s.
    Lesson 17.3.2: Sustained VT: Continuous VT for at least 30 s, or which requires an intervention for termination.
  Mini Chapter 17.4: VT can be classified according to morphology
    Lesson 17.4.1: Monomorphic VT (MVT): Same QRS morphology from beat to beat.
    Lesson 17.4.2: Polymorphic VT (PVT): is a VT that has two or more QRS morphologies.
    Lesson 17.4.3: Sudden Cardiac death
    Lesson 17.4.4: Syncope
    Lesson 17.4.5: Pathogenic variant: genetic variants most likely to cause an associated disease.
    Lesson 17.4.6: Site of origin of the PVC: (PVC looks away, i.e., looks negative, from where it originates)
    Lesson 17.4.7: Right vs. left
    Lesson 17.4.8: RV origin → LBBB morphology
    Lesson 17.4.9: LV origin → RBBB morphology
    Lesson 17.4.10: Anterior vs. inferior
    Lesson 17.4.11: Anterior origin → QRS (+) in the inferior leads (vertical axis).
    Lesson 17.4.12: Inferior origin → QRS (–) in the inferior leads (left axis).
    Lesson 17.4.13: Basal vs. apical
    Lesson 17.4.14: Basal origin → QRS is upright in all precordial leads, and (–) in I-aVL if lateral origin.
    Lesson 17.4.15: Apical origin → QRS is negative in all precordial leads, as it looks away from them, esp. V3-V6.

    Lesson 17.24.2: The following parameters suggest PVC-induced cardiomyopathy (vs PVC-aggravated Cardiomyopathy)
