Chapter 18: Bradyarrhythmia
  Mini Chapter 18.1: Classification   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 18.2: Sinus Node Disorders   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 18.3: AV junction disorders   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 18.4: Intraventricular conduction disturbance
    Lesson 18.4.1: QR in aVR with slurring in R, and negative T wave
    Lesson 18.4.2: Partial RBBB: QRS width < 120 ms. ECG features include
  Mini Chapter 18.5: LBBB
    Lesson 18.5.1: Advanced LBBB: QRS ≥ 120 ms. ECG features include
    Lesson 18.5.2: QS or rS in V1 with small ‘r’ with ST slightly elevated and positive asymmetrical T wave.
    Lesson 18.5.3: Exclusive R wave in I and aVL often with a negative asymmetrical T wave, slight ST depression, and
    Lesson 18.5.4: Usually, QS in aVR with positive T wave.
    Lesson 18.5.5: Generally, the ST segment is slightly opposed to the QRS polarity.
    Lesson 18.5.6: Partial LBBB: QRS < 120 ms. ECG features include
    Lesson 18.5.7: Leftwards QRS axis between -45 and -75.
    Lesson 18.5.8: S until V6 with IDT in V6 less than IDT in aVL, and with IDT in aVL ≥ 50 ms.
    Lesson 18.5.9: QRS duration < 120 ms. However, in isolated cases the QRS may not surpass 100 ms.
    Lesson 18.5.10: In advanced cases, mid-terminal slurring is present in I and aVL.
  Mini Chapter 18.6: Left posterior fascicular block (Inferoposterior hemiblock)
    Lesson 18.6.1: QRS axis greatly deviated to the right (between +90 and +140).
    Lesson 18.6.2: QRS width < 120 ms.
    Lesson 18.6.3: IDT ≥ 50 ms in aVF and V6, with IDT < 50 ms in aVL.
    Lesson 18.6.4: Mid-terminal slurring in II, III, and aVF in advanced cases (in the absence of partial RBBB).
  Mini Chapter 18.7: Bifascicular block implies a block in two of the three conduction fascicles, such as
    Lesson 18.7.1: RBBB with alternating LAFB and LPFB.
  Mini Chapter 18.8: Outside the pause that follows an obvious PAC or PVC, a pause on a rhythm strip may be secondary to   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 18.9: Location of the block in each type of AV block
    Lesson 18.9.1: First‐degree AV block is usually a nodal block, particularly if QRS is narrow.

  Mini Chapter 18.20: Causes
    Lesson 18.20.1: Idiopathic degenerative, age‐related sinus node disease (most common cause).
    Lesson 18.20.2: Acute ischemia may lead to transient SA block.
    Lesson 18.20.3: Drugs.
    Lesson 18.20.4: Hyper‐ or hypokalemia, hypothyroidism, obstructive jaundice.
    Lesson 18.20.5: High vagal tone (athlete, acute gastric illness).

  Mini Chapter 18.22: Causes
    Lesson 18.22.1: Calcific valvular disease.
    Lesson 18.22.2: Drugs (β‐blockers, calcium antagonists, digoxin, and antiarrhythmic drugs).
    Lesson 18.22.3: Electrolytes (hypo‐ or hyperkalemia, hypermagnesemia).
    Lesson 18.22.4: Benign vagotonic AV nodal block during sleep (particularly athletes) or during vasovagal syncope
    Lesson 18.22.5: Sarcoidosis and other autoimmune diseases (lupus, ankylosing spondylitis)
    Lesson 18.22.6: Lyme disease
    Lesson 18.22.7: Myocarditis and Chagas disease

Chapter 19: Cardic Pacing
  Mini Chapter 19.1: Indications   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 19.2: Sinus node dysfunction with
    Lesson 19.2.1: Associated symptoms (can be clearly attributed to bradyarrhythmia)
    Lesson 19.2.2: Symptomatic chronotropic incompetence.
    Lesson 19.2.3: Symptomatic patients with the bradycardia-tachycardia.
  Mini Chapter 19.3: AV block
    Lesson 19.3.1: Mobitz II AV block even if it is asymptomatic.
    Lesson 19.3.2: High‐grade or third‐degree AV block, even if it is asymptomatic.
  Mini Chapter 19.4: Bundle branch blocks
    Lesson 19.4.1: Alternating BBB with or without symptoms.
  Mini Chapter 19.5: Reflex Syncope with
    Lesson 19.5.1: Symptomatic pause(s) > 3 s or asymptomatic pause(s) > 6 s due to sinus arrest or AVB; or
    Lesson 19.5.2: Cardioinhibitory carotid sinus syndrome (1); or
    Lesson 19.5.3: Asystolic syncope during tilt testing (2)
  Mini Chapter 19.6: Other Conditions
    Lesson 19.6.1: Refractory dilated cardiomyopathy with QRS ≥ 130 ms (CRT).
    Lesson 19.6.2: Drug refractory HOCM with contraindication to or refusing septal reduction therapy.
    Lesson 19.6.3: High risk patients with congenital long QT syndrome.
    Lesson 19.6.4: Sustained VT terminated by pacing.
    Lesson 19.6.5: After heart transplantation for persistent symptomatic bradycardia or chronotropic incompetence.
    Lesson 19.6.6: After TAVI if
    Lesson 19.6.7: Complete or high-degree AVB that persists for 24 - 48 h after TAVI
    Lesson 19.6.8: New-onset alternating BBB after TAVI
    Lesson 19.6.9: Patients with pre-existing RBBB who develop any further conduction disturbance after TAVI (3) .
    Lesson 19.6.10: Congenital AV block if one of the following risk factors is present
    Lesson 19.6.11: Symptoms
    Lesson 19.6.12: Pauses > 3 the cycle length of the ventricular escape rhythm
    Lesson 19.6.13: Broad QRS escape rhythm
    Lesson 19.6.14: Prolonged QT interval
    Lesson 19.6.15: Complex ventricular ectopy
    Lesson 19.6.16: Mean daytime heart rate < 50 b.p.m.
  Mini Chapter 19.7: Types of cardiac rhythm devices
    Lesson 19.7.1: 2nd letter = chamber sensed: A, V, or D
    Lesson 19.7.2: 5th letter = Capability of multisite pacing: A, V, D, O.
  Mini Chapter 19.8: Mode of Pacing   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 19.9: Asynchronous Pacing (VOO, AOO, DOO)
    Lesson 19.9.1: Pacing without sensing.
    Lesson 19.9.2: Not manufactured yet.
    Lesson 19.9.3: Single Chamber demand Pacing (VVI, AAI)
    Lesson 19.9.4: Single sensing and pacing lead in RA (AAI) or RV (VVI).
    Lesson 19.9.5: VVI pacing may lead to a pacemaker syndrome.
    Lesson 19.9.6: Dual Chamber Pacing and Sensing (DDD): = AAI + VAT + VVI

    Lesson 19.17.1: Inappropriate programming (low sensitivity).
    Lesson 19.17.2: Electromagnetic interference.
    Lesson 19.17.3: Battery depletion.
    Lesson 19.17.4: Oversensing
  Mini Chapter 19.18: Causes of oversensing
    Lesson 19.18.1: Lead fracture or insulation break creating false signals.
    Lesson 19.18.2: Inappropriate programming.
    Lesson 19.18.3: Oversensing of a tall T wave and considering it QRS.
    Lesson 19.18.4: Failure to capture
  Mini Chapter 19.19: Causes of failure to capture
    Lesson 19.19.1: Lead fracture or insulation break.
    Lesson 19.19.2: Inappropriate programming.
    Lesson 19.19.3: Battery depletion.
    Lesson 19.19.4: Specific Pacemaker abnormalities
  Mini Chapter 19.20: Pacemaker syndrome
    Lesson 19.20.1: Physical examination reveals cannon "A" wave.
    Lesson 19.20.2: The definitive treatment is conversion to dual chamber pacing.
    Lesson 19.20.3: Pacemaker‐mediated tachycardia (PMT)
    Lesson 19.20.4: Also known as endless-loop tachycardia or pacemaker circus movement tachycardia.
    Lesson 19.20.5: PMT is a complication of DDD pacing.
    Lesson 19.20.6: PMT may be
    Lesson 19.20.7: Rapid tracking of atrial fibrillation or flutter.
    Lesson 19.20.8: Rapid ventricular triggering from electromagnetic interference.
  Mini Chapter 19.21: Crosstalk
    Lesson 19.21.1: To prevent the occurrence of crosstalk, two approaches have been corporated in the pacemaker
    Lesson 19.21.2: The ventricular blanking period at the time of the atrial impulse stimulus.
  Mini Chapter 19.22: Fusion and pseudofusion
    Lesson 19.22.1: The atrial rate is slower than the mode‐switch rate.

    Lesson 19.23.2: If pacing spikes are present

    Lesson 19.24.4: Higher cost
    Lesson 19.24.5: More transvenous hardware
    Lesson 19.24.6: Risk associated with the additional lead (e.g. ventricular perforation)
    Lesson 19.24.7: More complex programming

Chapter 20: Syncope
  Mini Chapter 20.1: Definition of syncope   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 20.2: Classification of syncope
    Lesson 20.2.1: Vasovagal syncope
    Lesson 20.2.2: Situational syncope
    Lesson 20.2.3: Carotid sinus syndrome
    Lesson 20.2.4: Post-exertional syncope
    Lesson 20.2.5: Postural orthostatic tachycardia syndrome (POTS)
    Lesson 20.2.6: Ventricular tachycardia secondary to structural heart disease, or primary electrical disease.
  Mini Chapter 20.3: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 20.4: Initial Evaluation
    Lesson 20.4.1: The initial evaluation should answer key questions
    Lesson 20.4.2: Unconsciousness often lasts longer than 5 min.
    Lesson 20.4.3: Postictal confusion or paralysis
    Lesson 20.4.4: Prolonged tonic-clonic movements.
    Lesson 20.4.5: Tongue biting strongly suggests seizure.
  Mini Chapter 20.5: Initial syncope evaluation include
    Lesson 20.5.1: Additional evaluation
    Lesson 20.5.2: Immediate ECG monitoring when there is a suspicion of arrhythmic syncope.
    Lesson 20.5.3: Head-up tilt testing when there is suspicion of syncope due to OH or reflex syncope.
  Mini Chapter 20.6: Clinical clues
    Lesson 20.6.1: Position during syncope
    Lesson 20.6.2: Supine → reflex syncope is unlikely
    Lesson 20.6.3: Sitting or standing position → any cause is possible.
    Lesson 20.6.4: Within a few minutes of standing → orthostatic hypotension
    Lesson 20.6.5: Prolonged sitting or standing → vasovagal
    Lesson 20.6.6: Situation during syncope
    Lesson 20.6.7: Post-exertion → vasovagal
    Lesson 20.6.8: Postprandial → orthostatic hypotension
    Lesson 20.6.9: Sudden fear, pain, unpleasant sight, hot environment → vasovagal
    Lesson 20.6.10: Strain situation (micturition) → reflex syncope
    Lesson 20.6.11: Head turning, shaving, tight collar → carotid sinus syndrome.
    Lesson 20.6.12: Prodromes (abdominal discomfort, malaise, palpitations, nausea, blurry vision)
    Lesson 20.6.13: Yes and > 5 seconds → reflex syncope.
    Lesson 20.6.14: How consciousness is regained after syncope?
    Lesson 20.6.15: Promptly → cardiac syncope
    Lesson 20.6.16: Prolonged fatigue or nausea after syncope → reflex syncope
    Lesson 20.6.17: Confusion → seizure
    Lesson 20.6.18: Color during syncope
    Lesson 20.6.19: Pale, diaphoresis → reflex syncope, orthostatic syncope
    Lesson 20.6.20: Blue → arrhythmia, seizure
    Lesson 20.6.21: Duration
    Lesson 20.6.22: > 5 minutes → seizure, hypoglycemia; not syncope (except occasionally aortic stenosis)
    Lesson 20.6.23: Underlying heart disease, chest pain → cardiac syncope
    Lesson 20.6.24: Multiple neuropsychiatric or blood pressure medications → reflex or orthostatic syncope
    Lesson 20.6.25: The presence or the lack of injury does not help differentiate cardiac from reflex syncope.
  Mini Chapter 20.7: ECG or rhythm monitor findings suggestive of cardiac syncope
    Lesson 20.7.1: Bradyarrhythmia-related syncope is established with any of the following
    Lesson 20.7.2: Mobitz II or complete AV block.
    Lesson 20.7.3: Alternating LBBB or RBBB (on the same ECG or on ECGs obtained on separate occasions)
    Lesson 20.7.4: Bradyarrhythmia-related syncope is suggested with
    Lesson 20.7.5: Isolated RBBB or LBBB (VT also possible, depending on the underlying cardiac disease)
    Lesson 20.7.6: Mobitz type I AV block
    Lesson 20.7.7: Tachyarrhythmia-related syncope is established with: Sustained VT or fast SVT (> 160 bpm)
    Lesson 20.7.8: Underlying heart disease and VT are suggested with
    Lesson 20.7.9: Q waves
    Lesson 20.7.10: LBBB, RBBB, QRS > 110 ms
    Lesson 20.7.11: LVH, RVH, large R wave in V1
    Lesson 20.7.12: Acute ST–T abnormalities
  Mini Chapter 20.8: Reflex syncope and OH   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 20.9: Cardiac syncope   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 20.10: Arrhythmic syncope is highly probable when the ECG shows
    Lesson 20.10.1: Persistent sinus bradycardia < 40 b.p.m. or sinus pauses > 3 s in awake state and in absence of
    Lesson 20.10.2: Mobitz II second- and third-degree AV block.
    Lesson 20.10.3: Alternating left and right BBB.
    Lesson 20.10.4: VT or rapid paroxysmal SVT.
    Lesson 20.10.5: Non-sustained episodes of polymorphic VT and long or short QT interval; or
    Lesson 20.10.6: Pacemaker or ICD malfunction with cardiac pauses.
  Mini Chapter 20.11: Syncopal events
    Lesson 20.11.1: New onset of chest discomfort, breathlessness, abdominal pain, or headache
    Lesson 20.11.2: Syncope during exertion or when supine
    Lesson 20.11.3: Sudden onset palpitation followed by syncope
    Lesson 20.11.4: Minor (high risk only if associated with structural heart disease or abnormal ECG)
    Lesson 20.11.5: No warning symptoms or short (< 10s) prodrome
    Lesson 20.11.6: Family history of SCD at young age
    Lesson 20.11.7: Syncope in the sitting position
  Mini Chapter 20.12: Past medical history
    Lesson 20.12.1: Severe structural or coronary artey disease (heart failure, Low EF or previous MI)
  Mini Chapter 20.13: Physical Examination
    Lesson 20.13.1: Unexplained systolic blood pressure in the ED < 90 mmHg
    Lesson 20.13.2: Suggestion of GI bleed on rectal examinations
    Lesson 20.13.3: Persistent bradycardia (< 40 b.p.m) in awake state and in absence of physical training
    Lesson 20.13.4: Undiagnosed systolic murmur
  Mini Chapter 20.14: ECG
    Lesson 20.14.1: ECG changes consistent with acute ischemia
    Lesson 20.14.2: Mobitz II second- and third- degree AV block
    Lesson 20.14.3: Slow AF (< 40 b.p.m)
    Lesson 20.14.4: Persistent sinus bradycardia (< 40 b.p.m) or
    Lesson 20.14.5: Bundle branch block, intraventricular conduction
    Lesson 20.14.6: Sustained and non-sustained VT
    Lesson 20.14.7: Dysfunction of pacemaker or ICD
    Lesson 20.14.8: Type 1 burgada pattern
    Lesson 20.14.9: QTc > 460 ms in repeated ECGs indicating LQTs.
    Lesson 20.14.10: Risk Stratification
    Lesson 20.14.11: Mobitz I second degree AV block and first-degree
    Lesson 20.14.12: Asymptomatic inappropriate mild sinus
    Lesson 20.14.13: Paroxysmal SVT or AF
    Lesson 20.14.14: Pre-exicted QRS complex
    Lesson 20.14.15: Short QTc interval (≤ 340 ms)
    Lesson 20.14.16: Atypical Burgada patterns
    Lesson 20.14.17: Negative T wave in right precordial leads, epsilon
    Lesson 20.14.18: Criteria for hospital admission
  Mini Chapter 20.15: Additional advice and clinical perspectives
    Lesson 20.15.1: In the ED, presyncope should be managed as syncope; as it carries the same prognosis.
    Lesson 20.15.2: Diagnostic radiology and laboratory tests such as chest X-ray, brain CT, routine blood hematology,
  Mini Chapter 20.16: Additional Diagnostic tests
    Lesson 20.16.1: Carotid sinus massage
    Lesson 20.16.2: An abnormal response to carotid sinus massage is defined as any of the following
    Lesson 20.16.3: Vasodepressor response: SBP decreases by ≥ 50 mmHg and/or
    Lesson 20.16.4: Cardioinhibitory response: pause ≥ 3 sec (sinus pause or AV block).
  Mini Chapter 20.17: Indications   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

    Lesson 20.18.1: Active standing
  Mini Chapter 20.19: Indications   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 20.20: Diagnostic criteria
    Lesson 20.20.1: Tilt testing
    Lesson 20.20.2: Types of response to tilt table testing
    Lesson 20.20.3: Normal response: heart rate increases by 10-15 bpm and DBP increases by > 10 mmHg.
    Lesson 20.20.4: Vasodepressor response: abrupt, rapid hypotension without significant drop in heart rate (< 10%).
    Lesson 20.20.5: Cardioinhibitory response: hypotension with a drop in heart rate to < 40 bpm or a pause > 3 sec.

    Lesson 20.32.2: Include patients with a high likelihood of arrhythmic events.
    Lesson 20.32.3: The absence of arrhythmia during syncope excludes arrhythmic syncope.
    Lesson 20.32.4: Video recording in suspected syncope
    Lesson 20.32.5: Exercise testing: There is no data supporting routine exercise testing in patients with syncope.
  Mini Chapter 20.33: Indications   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 20.34: Diagnostic criteria
    Lesson 20.34.1: Coronary angiography
  Mini Chapter 20.35: Treatment
    Lesson 20.35.1: Discontinuation/reduction of hypotensive therapy targeting a systolic BP of 140 mmHg.
    Lesson 20.35.2: Pharmacological treatment

Chapter 21: Pericardial diseases
  Mini Chapter 21.1: In all cases of suspected pericardial disease, a first diagnostic evaluation is recommended with
    Lesson 21.1.1: Auscultation
    Lesson 21.1.2: Transthoracic echocardiography
    Lesson 21.1.3: Chest X-ray
  Mini Chapter 21.2: Major factors include   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 21.3: Aetiology
    Lesson 21.3.1: The four most common causes are
    Lesson 21.3.2: Other common causes of pericarditis, occurring in specific contexts
  Mini Chapter 21.4: Infectious causes
    Lesson 21.4.1: Parasitic (very rare): Echinococcus spp, Toxoplasma spp
  Mini Chapter 21.5: Non-infectious causes
    Lesson 21.5.1: Neoplastic
    Lesson 21.5.2: Metabolic: Uraemia, myxoedema, anorexia nervosa, other rare.
  Mini Chapter 21.6: Traumatic
    Lesson 21.6.1: Early onset (rare)
    Lesson 21.6.2: Direct injury (penetrating thoracic injury, esophageal perforation).
    Lesson 21.6.3: Indirect injury (non-penetrating thoracic injury, radiation injury).
    Lesson 21.6.4: Delayed onset: Pericardial injury syndromes (common; such as postmyocardial infarction syndrome,
  Mini Chapter 21.7: Drug-related
    Lesson 21.7.1: Lupus-like syndrome (procainamide, hydralazine, methyldopa, isoniazid, phenytoin).
    Lesson 21.7.2: Penicillins as a hypersensitivity pericarditis with eosinophilia.
  Mini Chapter 21.8: Others
    Lesson 21.8.1: Common: Amyloidosis, Aortic dissection, PAH and HF.
    Lesson 21.8.2: Uncommon: congenital partial and complete absence of the pericardium.
  Mini Chapter 21.9: Predictors of poor prognosis
    Lesson 21.9.1: High fever > 38 C.
    Lesson 21.9.2: Subacute course (symptoms over several days without a clear-cut acute onset).
    Lesson 21.9.3: Large pericardial effusion (i.e. diastolic echo-free space > 20 mm).
    Lesson 21.9.4: Cardiac tamponade.
    Lesson 21.9.5: Failure to respond within 7 days to NSAIDs.
    Lesson 21.9.6: Myopericarditis.
    Lesson 21.9.7: Immunodepression
    Lesson 21.9.8: Trauma
    Lesson 21.9.9: Oral anticoagulant therapy.
  Mini Chapter 21.10: Types   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 21.11: Additional supporting findings
    Lesson 21.11.1: Elevation of markers of inflammation (i.e CRP, ESR and WBCs)
    Lesson 21.11.2: Evidence of pericardial inflammation by an imaging technique (CT, CMR)
  Mini Chapter 21.12: Diagnosis   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 21.13: The diagnosis of pericarditis requires two of the following four features
    Lesson 21.13.1: The rub is due to the friction of the inflamed visceral and parietal pericardial layers.
    Lesson 21.13.2: Low QRS voltage and QRS alternans
    Lesson 21.13.3: Most often, No effusion is present (“dry” pericarditis).
  Mini Chapter 21.14: Additional supporting findings
    Lesson 21.14.1: Evidence of pericardial inflammation by an imaging technique (CT, MRI).
  Mini Chapter 21.15: Management   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 21.16: Prognosis
    Lesson 21.16.1: Pericarditis is a self‐limiting disease with no complication or recurrence in > 70 % of patients.
    Lesson 21.16.2: Low (<1%) for idiopathic and presumed viral pericarditis;
    Lesson 21.16.3: Intermediate (2 –5%) for autoimmune, immunemediated and neoplastic aetiologies; and
    Lesson 21.16.4: High (20–30%) for bacterial aetiologies, especially with TB and purulent pericarditis.
    Lesson 21.16.5: One‐third of patients have pleuropericardial involvement during these recurrences.

    Lesson 21.20.2: Increasing in size, pericardiocentesis is warranted because of the risk of tamponade.

  Mini Chapter 22.15: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 22.15.1: Four types of ASDs occur in the atrial septum
  Mini Chapter 22.16: Natural history
    Lesson 22.16.1: Spontaneous closure does not occur in ostium primum or sinus venosus type.
  Mini Chapter 22.17: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.17.1: Auscultation
    Lesson 22.17.2: A widely split and fixed S2 results from RBBB which results in delayed pulmonary valve closure.
    Lesson 22.17.3: ECG: P-Pulmonale, RBBB (as the dilated RV prolongs the time required for RV depolarization).
  Mini Chapter 22.18: Diagnostic work-up
    Lesson 22.18.1: Echocardiography is the first-line diagnostic technique, providing diagnosis and quantification.
    Lesson 22.18.2: Other key information to be provided includes PAP and tricuspid regurgitation.
    Lesson 22.18.3: Exercise testing should be performed in patients with PAH to exclude desaturation.
  Mini Chapter 22.19: Management of ASD
    Lesson 22.19.1: Indications of closure: ASD closure is indicated in
    Lesson 22.19.2: If CHF does not respond to medical management.
    Lesson 22.19.3: Presence of significant left-to-right shunt (significant RV enlargement) regardless of symptoms.
    Lesson 22.19.4: Patients with suspicion of paradoxical embolism in absence of PAH and LV disease.
    Lesson 22.19.5: Mode of closure
  Mini Chapter 22.20: Follow-up recommendations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.21: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 22.21.1: Types of VSD
  Mini Chapter 22.22: Natural history
    Lesson 22.22.1: Inlet and infundibular VSDs do not become smaller or close spontaneously.
    Lesson 22.22.2: PVOD may begin to develop as early as 6 to 12 months of age in patients with a large VSD.
  Mini Chapter 22.23: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.23.1: Manifestations
    Lesson 22.23.2: Small VSD: patients are asymptomatic, with normal growth and development.
    Lesson 22.23.3: If PVOD occurs: cyanosis and a decreased level of activity may result.
    Lesson 22.23.4: Auscultation
    Lesson 22.23.5: Small VSD: regurgitant systolic murmur (holosystolic or less) maximally audible at the LLSB.
    Lesson 22.23.6: If pulmonary hypertension occurs: S2 may split narrowly, and the intensity of the P2 increases.
  Mini Chapter 22.24: Diagnostic work-up
    Lesson 22.24.1: Exercise testing should be performed in patients with PAH to exclude desaturation.
  Mini Chapter 22.25: Management of VSD   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

    Lesson 22.26.1: Complications
    Lesson 22.26.2: The incidence of neurologic complications is directly related to the circulatory arrest time.
    Lesson 22.26.3: Follow up
    Lesson 22.26.4: After surgical closure without residual abnormality, 5-year intervals may be reasonable.
  Mini Chapter 22.27: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 22.27.1: The failure of normal development of this tissue may be either complete or partial
    Lesson 22.27.2: The magnitude of the left-to-right shunt in complete AV canal is determined by the level of PVR.
  Mini Chapter 22.28: Natural history   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

    Lesson 22.29.4: Partial AV canal: the same as secundum ASD.

  Mini Chapter 22.37: Complications
    Lesson 22.37.1: Development or progression of A-V valve regurgitation (commonest late problem), requiring repair.
    Lesson 22.37.2: Sinus node dysfunction resulting in bradyarrhythmias may occur.
    Lesson 22.37.3: Postoperative arrhythmias (usually supraventricular).
  Mini Chapter 22.38: Follow up
    Lesson 22.38.1: Lifelong regular follow-up of all patients with an AVSD, operated and unoperated, is recommended.
  Mini Chapter 22.39: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.40: Clinical Picture   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.41: Natural history
    Lesson 22.41.1: CHF or recurrent pneumonia develops if the shunt is large.
    Lesson 22.41.2: Although rare, an aneurysm of PDA may develop and possibly rupture in adult life.
    Lesson 22.41.3: Coronary AV fistula: the murmur is audible over the precordium, not at the ULSB.
    Lesson 22.41.4: Venous hum: an innocent condition that disappears when the patient is supine.
    Lesson 22.41.5: Murmurs of collaterals in patients with COA or TOF: audible in the intercostal spaces.
  Mini Chapter 22.42: Diagnostic work-up
    Lesson 22.42.1: CCT can further evaluate the anatomy where required.
    Lesson 22.42.2: Exercise testing should be performed in patients with PAH to exclude desaturation of lower limbs.
  Mini Chapter 22.43: Management of PDA
    Lesson 22.43.1: Transcatheter occlusion of PDA has become a standard of care (Success rate > 95%).
  Mini Chapter 22.44: Follow-up recommendations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

    Lesson 22.47.3: Echocardiography: isolated PAPVC can be missed even with careful echocardiography.

    Lesson 22.53.1: Objective decrease in exercise capacity.
    Lesson 22.53.2: Decreasing RV function and/or progression of TR to at least moderate.
    Lesson 22.53.3: RVSP > 80 mmHg.
    Lesson 22.53.4: R-L shunting via an ASD or VSD
    Lesson 22.53.5: Symptoms related to PS.
    Lesson 22.53.6: Decreasing RV function and/or progressive TR to at least moderate.
    Lesson 22.53.7: R-L shunting via an ASD or VSD.
  Mini Chapter 22.54: Follow-up recommendations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.55: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 22.55.1: LVOT Obstruction can occur at the valvular, subvalvular or supravalvular levels

  Mini Chapter 22.58: Diagnostic work-up
    Lesson 22.58.1: Mild: Mean Doppler < 25 mm Hg [or peak-to-peak gradient <30 mmHg].
    Lesson 22.58.2: Moderate: Mean Doppler 25-40 mmHg [or peak-to-peak gradient 30-50 mmHg].
    Lesson 22.58.3: Severe: Mean Doppler > 40 mm Hg [or peak-to-peak gradient > 50 mmHg]

    Lesson 22.61.1: Very severe AS defined by a Vmax > 5.5 m/s.
    Lesson 22.61.2: Severe valve calcification and a rate of Vmax progression ≥ 0.3 m/s/year.
    Lesson 22.61.3: Markedly elevated BNP levels (> 3-fold ageand sex-corrected normal range) confirmed by
    Lesson 22.61.4: Severe PH (systolic PAP at rest > 60 mmHg confirmed by invasive measurement) without
  Mini Chapter 22.62: Concomitant aortic valve surgery at the time of other cardiac/ascending aorta surgery   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.63: Supravalvular aortic stenosis
    Lesson 22.63.1: Symptoms attributable to obstruction (exertional dyspnoea, angina, syncope).
    Lesson 22.63.2: LV systolic dysfunction (EF < 50% without other explanation).
    Lesson 22.63.3: Surgery required for significant CAD or valvular disease.
  Mini Chapter 22.64: Subaortic stenosis
    Lesson 22.64.1: Mean gradient < 40 mmHg but LVEF < 50%.
    Lesson 22.64.2: AR is severe and LVESD > 50 mm (or 25 mm/m2 BSA) and/or EF < 50%.
    Lesson 22.64.3: Mean Doppler gradient is ≥ 40 mmHg and marked LVH present.
    Lesson 22.64.4: Mean Doppler gradient is ≥ 40 mmHg and there is a fall in blood pressure below baseline
    Lesson 22.64.5: Mean Doppler gradient is ≥ 40 mmHg, LV is normal (EF > 50% and no LVH), exercise testing
    Lesson 22.64.6: Progression of AR is documented, and AR becomes more than mild (to prevent further
  Mini Chapter 22.65: Follow-up recommendations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.66: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.67: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.67.1: Symptomatic Infants
    Lesson 22.67.2: Auscultation: A loud S3 gallop and week and thready pulses, without heart murmur, are common.
    Lesson 22.67.3: ECG: A normal or rightward QRS axis and RVH or RBBB, rather than LVH.
    Lesson 22.67.4: Early death from CHF and renal failure is possible.
    Lesson 22.67.5: Asymptomatic Children
    Lesson 22.67.6: Manifestations: usually asymptomatic except for rare complaints of leg pain after exercise.
    Lesson 22.67.7: ECG: Leftward QRS axis and LVH, but it may be normal (in 20% of patients).
  Mini Chapter 22.68: Diagnostic work-up
    Lesson 22.68.1: In the presence of extensive collateral arteries, gradients are often underestimated.
  Mini Chapter 22.69: Management of coarctation and re-coarctation of the aorta   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.70: Indications of intervention
    Lesson 22.70.1: Associated significant aortic valve stenosis or regurgitation (BAV).
    Lesson 22.70.2: Aneurysm of the ascending aorta with a diameter > 50 mm or rapid progression of diameter.
    Lesson 22.70.3: Aneurysm and false aneurysms at the previous CoA site.
    Lesson 22.70.4: Symptomatic or large aneurysms of the circle of Willis.
    Lesson 22.70.5: For neonates, infants and small children with native coarctation
    Lesson 22.70.6: Balloon angioplasty is not a good choice because of its long-term risk of aneurysm.
    Lesson 22.70.7: For small children with recurrent coarctation
    Lesson 22.70.8: For older children, adolescents and adults
  Mini Chapter 22.71: Follow-up recommendations
    Lesson 22.71.1: Recommended imaging intervals are commonly every 3-5 years but also depend on baseline pathology.
  Mini Chapter 22.72: Complications, Sequalae
    Lesson 22.72.1: Complications of surgery
    Lesson 22.72.2: Recurrent laryngeal nerve injury, chylothorax, bleeding, and infection.
  Mini Chapter 22.73: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.74: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.74.1: ECG may show RVH.
  Mini Chapter 22.75: Diagnostic Workup
    Lesson 22.75.1: Cardiac CT or MRI is more frequently used to clarify the anatomy before surgery.
    Lesson 22.75.2: Medical
    Lesson 22.75.3: PGE1 infusion, intubation, and oxygen administration.
    Lesson 22.75.4: Surgical
    Lesson 22.75.5: TOF occurs in 5% to 10% of all CHDs. This is the most common cyanotic heart defect.
    Lesson 22.75.6: TOF populations can be subdivided into
    Lesson 22.75.7: Nonsyndromic patients (which represent the vast majority).
  Mini Chapter 22.76: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 22.76.1: VSD is a perimembranous defect with extension into the infundibular septum.
    Lesson 22.76.2: Associated Anomalies
  Mini Chapter 22.77: Natural history
    Lesson 22.77.1: In TOF, Hypoxic spells may develop in infants.
  Mini Chapter 22.78: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.78.1: Symptoms
    Lesson 22.78.2: Patients with TOF with mild PS (acyanotic TOF) may be asymptomatic.
    Lesson 22.78.3: Auscultation
    Lesson 22.78.4: ECG: RAD and RVH. In the acyanotic form, the QRS axis is normal.
    Lesson 22.78.5: Chest radiographs
    Lesson 22.78.6: In acyanotic TOF, chest radiographs are indistinguishable from those of a small to moderate VSD.
    Lesson 22.78.7: Pathophysiology of hypoxic spell: (↑ PS or ↓ SVR → ↑ R-L shunt)
    Lesson 22.78.8: Treatment of hypoxic spell
    Lesson 22.78.9: Pick up the infant and hold in a knee-chest position.
    Lesson 22.78.10: If not fully responsive to the above measures, the following may be tried
  Mini Chapter 22.79: Diagnostic work-up
    Lesson 22.79.1: Echocardiography usually makes the diagnosis and quantitate the severity of TOF
    Lesson 22.79.2: Doppler studies estimate the pressure gradient across the RVOT obstruction.
    Lesson 22.79.3: Strain measurements are helpful in quantifying the degree of electromechanical dyssychrony.
    Lesson 22.79.4: Associated anomalies such as ASD and persistence of the left SVC can be imaged.
    Lesson 22.79.5: CPET assists timing of reintervention and provides prognostic information.
  Mini Chapter 22.80: Management
    Lesson 22.80.1: Medical
    Lesson 22.80.2: Hypoxic spells should be recognized and treated appropriately (as described before).
    Lesson 22.80.3: Total repair surgery
    Lesson 22.80.4: Symptomatic or cyanotic infants with favorable anatomy: at any time after 3 to 4 months of age.
    Lesson 22.80.5: Asymptomatic and minimally cyanotic children: between 3 and 24 months.
    Lesson 22.80.6: Mildly cyanotic infants who have had previous shunt surgery: at 1-2 years of age.
  Mini Chapter 22.81: Indications for EP testing and ICDs   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.82: Follow up recommendation   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.83: Common complications in adulthood are
    Lesson 22.83.1: Intervention after TOF repair
  Mini Chapter 22.84: RVOTO when one of the following criteria is present
    Lesson 22.84.1: Decrease in objective exercise capacity.
    Lesson 22.84.2: Progressive RV dilation to RVESVi ≥ 80 mL/m2, and/or RVEDVi ≥ 160 mL/m2, and/or
    Lesson 22.84.3: Progressive RV systolic dysfunction.
    Lesson 22.84.4: RVOTO with RVSP > 80 mmHg.
  Mini Chapter 22.85: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.86: Natural history
    Lesson 22.86.1: Associated ASD or accessory conduction pathways (leading to SVT) may be the presenting feature.
  Mini Chapter 22.87: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.87.1: Manifestations
    Lesson 22.87.2: In milder cases, dyspnea, fatigue, and cyanosis on exertion may be present in childhood.
    Lesson 22.87.3: ECG: RBBB and RAH. WPW preexcitation, SVT, and first-degree AV block are occasionally present.
    Lesson 22.87.4: Chest radiographs may show extreme cardiomegaly, involving principally the RA, and decreased PVMs.
  Mini Chapter 22.88: Diagnostic work-up   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.89: Management
    Lesson 22.89.1: Medical
    Lesson 22.89.2: Asymptomatic children with mild Ebstein’s anomaly require only regular observation.
    Lesson 22.89.3: Surgical
    Lesson 22.89.4: Indications of surgery
    Lesson 22.89.5: Critically ill neonates who show symptoms within the first week of life.
    Lesson 22.89.6: RVOT obstruction by redundant tricuspid valve.
    Lesson 22.89.7: Severe activity limitation (i.e., NYHA class III or IV).
    Lesson 22.89.8: History of paradoxical embolus.
    Lesson 22.89.9: Repeated, life-threatening arrhythmias in patients with associated WPW syndrome.
    Lesson 22.89.10: Procedure
    Lesson 22.89.11: If good RV size and function: TV reconstruction with ASD closure.
    Lesson 22.89.12: If poor RV size or function: staged Fontan operation (with ASD enlargement)
    Lesson 22.89.13: If deeply cyanotic neonates: initial MBT is needed before BDG.
    Lesson 22.89.14: During surgery, interruption of accessory pathway is recommended if recurrent SVT.
  Mini Chapter 22.90: Indications for surgery   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.91: Indications for catheter intervention   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.92: In adults with Ebstein, isolated device closure of ASD/PFO   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.93: Follow-up recommendations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.94: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.95: Natural history   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.96: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.96.1: Manifestations
    Lesson 22.96.2: Cyanosis immediately after birth.
    Lesson 22.96.3: ECG: BVH (70% of patients); RVH or LVH is less common.
  Mini Chapter 22.97: Diagnostic work-up
    Lesson 22.97.1: Echocardiography: The following three findings are diagnostic
    Lesson 22.97.2: A large VSD is imaged directly under the truncal valve, similar to that seen in TOF.
    Lesson 22.97.3: The pulmonary valve cannot be imaged; only one semilunar valve (i.e., truncal valve) is imaged.
    Lesson 22.97.4: CT or MRI may be necessary to evaluate arch anatomy or PA anatomy.
  Mini Chapter 22.98: Management
    Lesson 22.98.1: Medical
    Lesson 22.98.2: Vigorous decongestive measures with diuretics and ACEIs are required before surgery.
    Lesson 22.98.3: Pay attention to the following when DiGeorge syndrome is suspected or confirmed
    Lesson 22.98.4: Serum Ca and Mg levels should be obtained.
    Lesson 22.98.5: Only irradiated blood product should be used.
    Lesson 22.98.6: Surgical
  Mini Chapter 22.99: Follow-up recommendations
    Lesson 22.99.1: Truncal valve insufficiency may develop or progress.
    Lesson 22.99.2: A small conduit needs to be replaced at 2-3 years of age.
    Lesson 22.99.3: Calcification of the valve in the conduit may occur within 1 to 5 years.
    Lesson 22.99.4: Ventricular arrhythmias may develop because of right ventriculotomy.
  Mini Chapter 22.100: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 22.100.1: Depending on the drainage site of the pulmonary veins, the defects may be divided into
    Lesson 22.100.2: The level of systemic arterial oxygen saturation is proportional to the amount of PBF.
  Mini Chapter 22.101: Natural history   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.102: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.102.1: Patients without PV obstruction
    Lesson 22.102.2: Symptoms: Growth retardation, mild cyanosis, and signs of CHF.
    Lesson 22.102.3: ECG: RAD, RVH (rsR’ pattern in V1), and occasional RAH.
    Lesson 22.102.4: Patients with PV obstruction
    Lesson 22.102.5: Symptoms: Marked cyanosis and respiratory distress are present in the neonate.
    Lesson 22.102.6: ECG: RAD and RVH.
    Lesson 22.102.7: Chest radiographs: plethoric and congested lung fields with a relatively small heart shadow.
    Lesson 22.102.8: Patients with the infracardiac type rarely survive more than a few weeks without surgery.
  Mini Chapter 22.103: Diagnostic Workup
    Lesson 22.103.1: Echocardiography
  Mini Chapter 22.104: Management
    Lesson 22.104.1: In obstructed TAPVC: emergent surgery, with surgical mortality rate of 20%.
    Lesson 22.104.2: In unobstructed TAPVC: elective surgery before 6 months of age, with a mortality rate of 5-10%.
  Mini Chapter 22.105: Follow-Up   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.106: Follow-up is needed for possible late development of complication, which include
    Lesson 22.106.1: Postoperative arrhythmias (usually atrial).
  Mini Chapter 22.107: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.108: Natural history   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.109: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.109.1: ECG: RAD and RVH. BVH may be present in infants with large VSD, PDA, or PS.
  Mini Chapter 22.110: Management   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.111: Stabilization before surgery
    Lesson 22.111.1: PGE1 infusion should be started to improve arterial oxygen saturation by reopening the ductus.
  Mini Chapter 22.112: Surgical approaches   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.113: Strategy
    Lesson 22.113.1: Simple TGA
    Lesson 22.113.2: TGA + VSD
    Lesson 22.113.3: TGA + LVOTO
    Lesson 22.113.4: TGA + VSD + PS: (1) BT shunt then Rastellli or (2) Nikaidoh operation or (3) REV operation
    Lesson 22.113.5: Complications: long-term complications are infrequent. The most common complications are
    Lesson 22.113.6: Coronary artery occlusion.
    Lesson 22.113.7: Neo-aortic root dilatation, resulting in AR.
    Lesson 22.113.8: Acute angle of the aortic arch, which may lead to functional obstruction and hypertension.
    Lesson 22.113.9: Complications: The most common complications are
    Lesson 22.113.10: Systemic RV dysfunction and failure.
    Lesson 22.113.11: Secondary progressive TR (systemic AV valve).
    Lesson 22.113.12: Ventricular tachyarrhythmias
    Lesson 22.113.13: Baffle stenosis, either superior baffle (most common) or inferior baffle obstruction.
    Lesson 22.113.14: Death due to heart failure or sudden death, probably caused by arrhythmia.
  Mini Chapter 22.114: Baffle leak or stenosis   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.115: Pulmonary venous-atrium obstruction   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.116: Systemic (tricuspid) AV valve regurgitation
    Lesson 22.116.1: Complications: Common complications are
    Lesson 22.116.2: Stenosis or regurgitation of the valved conduit between the RV and the PA.
    Lesson 22.116.3: LVOT obstruction (i.e., obstruction of the flow from the LV to the aorta).
    Lesson 22.116.4: Residual VSD.
    Lesson 22.116.5: LV dysfunction.
    Lesson 22.116.6: Arrhythmias, both ventricular and supraventricular.
    Lesson 22.116.7: Endocarditis of the valved conduit.
    Lesson 22.116.8: Death, either sudden (arrhythmia) or due to heart failure.
  Mini Chapter 22.117: Follow-Up   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.118: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.119: Natural history   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.120: Clinical Picture   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.121: Diagnostic work-up   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.122: Management
    Lesson 22.122.1: Surgical
    Lesson 22.122.2: Initial treatment: this will depend on the mode of presentation and the associated lesions
    Lesson 22.122.3: Neonates with arch hypoplasia/coarctation require resuscitation and urgent surgical repair.
    Lesson 22.122.4: Patients with pulmonary stenosis or atresia require initial palliation with a BT shunt.
    Lesson 22.122.5: Definitive Procedures: There are two major approaches to surgical management of L-TGA
  Mini Chapter 22.123: Follow-up recommendations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.124: Late complications are
    Lesson 22.124.1: Systemic RV dysfunction and failure.
    Lesson 22.124.2: Progressive TR (systemic AV valve).
    Lesson 22.124.3: LVOT Obstruction.
    Lesson 22.124.4: VTs (extremely rare).
    Lesson 22.124.5: Absent RVOT (e.g., pulmonary atresia, truncus arteriosus).
    Lesson 22.124.6: Unsuitable RVOT (e.g., D-TGA + VSD + PS or complicated DORV or L-TGA +PS).
    Lesson 22.124.7: Iatrogenic: e.g., Ross operation.
    Lesson 22.124.8: There is no ideal conduit. Limited durability implicates early reoperation.
    Lesson 22.124.9: Freedom from reoperation for conduit failure at 20 years was reported at 32% and 40%.
  Mini Chapter 22.125: Diagnostic work-up   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.126: Indications for intervention
    Lesson 22.126.1: Decrease in objective exercise capacity (CPET).
    Lesson 22.126.2: Progressive RV dilation to RVESVi ≥ 80 mL/m2, and/or RVEDVi ≥ 160 mL/m2, and/or
    Lesson 22.126.3: Progressive RV systolic dysfunction.
    Lesson 22.126.4: RVSP > 80 mmHg
  Mini Chapter 22.127: Follow-up recommendations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.128: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 22.128.1: In doubly committed VSD or Non committed VSD: mild cyanosis is present and the PBF is increased.
  Mini Chapter 22.129: Natural history   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.130: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.130.1: Subaortic VSD without PS
    Lesson 22.130.2: Physical findings resemble those of a large VSD (i.e CHF and failure to thrive).
    Lesson 22.130.3: Chest radiographs show cardiomegaly with increased PVMs and prominent MPA.
    Lesson 22.130.4: Subaortic VSD with PS (Fallot type)
    Lesson 22.130.5: Physical findings resemble those of TOF.
    Lesson 22.130.6: ECG shows RAD, RAH, and RVH or RBBB.
    Lesson 22.130.7: Chest radiographs show normal heart size (with upturned apex) and decreased PVMs.
    Lesson 22.130.8: Subpulmonary VSD (Taussig-Bing malformation)
    Lesson 22.130.9: Physical findings resemble those of TGA with severe cyanosis in newborn infants.
    Lesson 22.130.10: ECG shows RAD, RAH, and RVH. First-degree AV block is frequently present.
    Lesson 22.130.11: Chest radiographs show cardiomegaly with increased pulmonary vascular markings and prominent MPA.
  Mini Chapter 22.131: Diagnostic work-up
    Lesson 22.131.1: The size and position of the VSD should be determined in relation to the great arteries
    Lesson 22.131.2: Typical subpulmonary or subaortic VSD can be demonstrated by parasternal long-axis.
    Lesson 22.131.3: Doubly committed VSD is recognized in the parasternal or the apical long-axis view.
    Lesson 22.131.4: Non-committed VSDs are best seen in the apical four-chamber view.
  Mini Chapter 22.132: Management
    Lesson 22.132.1: Surgical
    Lesson 22.132.2: For non-committed or multiple VSDs (with large PBF and CHF)
    Lesson 22.132.3: Palliative procedure: a PA banding is occasionally performed.
    Lesson 22.132.4: For subaortic VSD and PS (Fallot type)
    Lesson 22.132.5: Palliative procedure: a B-T shunt or RVOT stent may be indicated to increase pulmonary blood flow.
    Lesson 22.132.6: For subpulmonary VSD (Taussig-Bing malformation)
  Mini Chapter 22.133: Follow-up recommendations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.134: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.135: Natural history
    Lesson 22.135.1: In patients with associated PS: cyanosis increases if PS worsens.
    Lesson 22.135.2: Progressive AV valve regurgitation is poorly tolerated.
    Lesson 22.135.3: Complete heart block develops in about 12% of patients.
    Lesson 22.135.4: The cause of death can be CHF, arrhythmias, or sudden death.
  Mini Chapter 22.136: Univentricular heart includes the following malformations
    Lesson 22.136.1: Tricuspid atresia
    Lesson 22.136.2: Double-inlet left ventricle (DILV)
    Lesson 22.136.3: Pulmonary atresia with intact septum
    Lesson 22.136.4: Unbalanced AVSD (dominant left)
    Lesson 22.136.5: Transposition/VSD with small RV
    Lesson 22.136.6: Hypoplastic left heart syndrome
    Lesson 22.136.7: DORV with mitral atresia
    Lesson 22.136.8: Unbalanced AVSD (dominant right)
  Mini Chapter 22.137: Diagnostic work-up   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.138: Fundamental TTE parameters/issues/items in the diagnosis of UVHs are
    Lesson 22.138.1: Abdominal and atrial situs.
    Lesson 22.138.2: Position of the heart in the chest and position of the apex.
    Lesson 22.138.3: Veno-atrial, AV, and ventriculo-arterial connections.
    Lesson 22.138.4: AV valve function should be evaluated, with special focus on regurgitation.
    Lesson 22.138.5: Ventricular function/hypertrophy.
    Lesson 22.138.6: ASD/VSD type, size, number, location.
    Lesson 22.138.7: Ascending aorta, aortic arch, and descending aorta; detect/ exclude coarctation.
    Lesson 22.138.8: PAs common trunk, branches, and sources of pulmonary blood supply.
    Lesson 22.138.9: Visualization of shunts (BlalockTaussig, Waterston, etc.).
  Mini Chapter 22.139: Management   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.140: Idea
    Lesson 22.140.1: Ideal candidates for Fontan are those who have normal LV function and low PVR
    Lesson 22.140.2: Normal LV function results from prevention of excessive volume or pressure loading of the LV by
    Lesson 22.140.3: Preventing excessive volume load by using a relatively small systemic-to-pulmonary shunt.
    Lesson 22.140.4: Avoiding ventricular hypertrophy (e.g., by relieving the LVOT obstruction)
    Lesson 22.140.5: Low pulmonary vascular resistance may result from
  Mini Chapter 22.141: Strategy
    Lesson 22.141.1: Medical
    Lesson 22.141.2: Surgical
    Lesson 22.141.3: Second-stage surgical palliative procedures
  Mini Chapter 22.142: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.143: Natural history   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.144: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.144.1: Manifestations: Severe cyanosis, tachypnea, and poor feeding are usual.
  Mini Chapter 22.145: Diagnostic Workup   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.146: Management   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.147: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.148: Natural history   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.149: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.149.1: Manifestations: Severe and progressive cyanosis is present from birth.
  Mini Chapter 22.150: Diagnostic Workup   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.151: Management   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.152: The aim of treatment is to
    Lesson 22.152.1: Secure adequate pulmonary blood flow.
    Lesson 22.152.2: Medical
  Mini Chapter 22.153: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.154: Natural history   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.155: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.155.1: Auscultation: S2 is loud and single. Heart murmur is usually absent.
    Lesson 22.155.2: ECG: RVH (because V5 and V6 electrodes are placed over the dilated RV).
  Mini Chapter 22.156: Diagnostic Workup
    Lesson 22.156.1: Medical
    Lesson 22.156.2: The patient should be intubated and ventilated, and metabolic acidosis should be corrected.
    Lesson 22.156.3: An intravenous infusion of PGE1 may produce temporary improvement.
    Lesson 22.156.4: Balloon atrial septostomy may help decompress the LA and temporarily improve oxygenation.
    Lesson 22.156.5: Stage I: One of the following procedures is done in preparation for a future Fontan operation.
    Lesson 22.156.6: Stage II (at 3 to 6 mo): Bidirectional Glenn (BDG) or hemi-Fontan operation
    Lesson 22.156.7: Perform cardiac catheterization (to find and occlude venous collaterals) or
    Lesson 22.156.8: Proceed with Fontan operation
    Lesson 22.156.9: Stage III (Fontan operation) within 1-2 yrs after stage II operation.
  Mini Chapter 22.157: Complications of the Fontan-Operation
    Lesson 22.157.1: Early Complications: may include the following
    Lesson 22.157.2: Low cardiac output, heart failure, or both are early postoperative complications.
    Lesson 22.157.3: Late Complications
    Lesson 22.157.4: Thromboembolism can occur in up to 10% of the patients after Fontan operation.
  Mini Chapter 22.158: Diagnostic work-up   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.159: Medical treatment
    Lesson 22.159.1: Electrical cardioversion is the mainstay of treatment, as drug therapy is often ineffective.
  Mini Chapter 22.160: Follow-up recommendations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.161: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.162: Risk stratification
    Lesson 22.162.1: Myocardial fibrosis has been demonstrated, suggesting myocardial ischaemia may play a role.
    Lesson 22.162.2: Level of exercise (e.g., competitive sports).
  Mini Chapter 22.163: Diagnostic work-up
    Lesson 22.163.1: Presentation: Chest pain, exertional syncope and SCD may be the first manifestation of AOCA.
  Mini Chapter 22.164: Management   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.165: Surgery should be considered in asymptomatic patients with
    Lesson 22.165.1: AAOCA (right or left) and evidence of myocardial ischemia.
    Lesson 22.165.2: AAOLCA and no evidence of myocardial ischemia but a high-risk anatomy.
  Mini Chapter 22.166: Surgery may be considered for   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.167: Pathophysiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.168: Diagnosis
    Lesson 22.168.1: Presentation
    Lesson 22.168.2: CTA or CMR may assist in more definitive diagnosis and provide additional information.
  Mini Chapter 22.169: Management
    Lesson 22.169.1: It is usually diagnosed incidentally during coronary angiography or noninvasive cardiac imaging.
    Lesson 22.169.2: The majority of CAFs are congenital, but may be iatrogenic.
    Lesson 22.169.3: Clinical sequelae include cardiac chamber dilatation and dyspnea, as well as symptoms of ischemia.
  Mini Chapter 22.170: Management
    Lesson 22.170.1: Indications for CAF Closure: Symptomatic medium or large CAF with
    Lesson 22.170.2: Evidence of ischemia in the feeder artery territory.
    Lesson 22.170.3: Arrhythmia thought to be related to CAF.
    Lesson 22.170.4: Endarteritis.
    Lesson 22.170.5: Vessel rupture.
    Lesson 22.170.6: Cardiac chamber enlargement.
    Lesson 22.170.7: Technique of Closure
    Lesson 22.170.8: Procedural Complications
    Lesson 22.170.9: For larger fistulas, there is a risk for device embolization.
  Mini Chapter 22.171: References and suggested readings

Chapter 23: Pulmonary Embolism
  Mini Chapter 23.1: Epidemiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 23.2: Predisposing factors
    Lesson 23.2.1: Fracture of lower limb
    Lesson 23.2.2: Hip or knee replacement
    Lesson 23.2.3: Major trauma
    Lesson 23.2.4: Hospitalization for HF or AF/flutter (within previous 3 months)
    Lesson 23.2.5: Myocardial infarction (within previous 3 months)
    Lesson 23.2.6: Spinal cord injury
    Lesson 23.2.7: Arthroscopic knee surgery
    Lesson 23.2.8: Autoimmune diseases
    Lesson 23.2.9: Blood transfusion
    Lesson 23.2.10: Central venous lines
    Lesson 23.2.11: Intravenous catheters and leads
    Lesson 23.2.12: Chemotherapy
    Lesson 23.2.13: Congestive heart failure or respiratory failure
    Lesson 23.2.14: Erythropoiesis-stimulating agents
    Lesson 23.2.15: Hormone replacement therapy (depends on formulation)
    Lesson 23.2.16: In vitro fertilization
    Lesson 23.2.17: Oral contraceptive therapy
    Lesson 23.2.18: Post-partum period
    Lesson 23.2.19: Infection (specifically pneumonia, urinary tract infection, and HIV)
    Lesson 23.2.20: Inflammatory bowel disease
    Lesson 23.2.21: Cancer (highest risk in metastatic disease)
    Lesson 23.2.22: Paralytic stroke
    Lesson 23.2.23: Superficial vein thrombosis
    Lesson 23.2.24: Thrombophilia
    Lesson 23.2.25: Bed rest > 3 days

    Lesson 24.5.6: Flattening of the interventricular

    Lesson 24.5.9: RA area (end-systole) > 18 cm2

    Lesson 24.17.1: Psychosocial support.
    Lesson 24.17.2: Immunization against SARS-CoV-2, influenza, and Streptococcus pneumoniae
    Lesson 24.17.3: Diuretics if there is signs of RV failure and fluid retention
    Lesson 24.17.4: Long-term oxygen therapy if PaO2 is < 60 mmHg (8 kPa) on at least two occasions.
    Lesson 24.17.5: Correction of iron status in the presence of iron-deficiency anemia.

    Lesson 24.19.2: Endothelin 1 pathway: Endothelin‐1 receptor antagonists (ERA): bosentan, ambrisentan, macitentan.
    Lesson 24.19.3: Prostacyclin (PGI2) pathway
    Lesson 24.19.4: Prostacyclin (PGI2) analogues: e.g., Epoprostenol and Treprostinil, and Iloprost.
    Lesson 24.19.5: PGI2 receptor (IP) agonists: e.g., selexipag.
    Lesson 24.19.6: Nitric oxide pathway
    Lesson 24.19.7: Phosphodiesterase‐5 inhibitors: e.g., Sildenafil and Tadalafil.
    Lesson 24.19.8: Stimulator of guanylate cyclase: e.g., Riociguat.
    Lesson 24.19.9: Nitrates increase cGMP in the systemic but not pulmonary arteries and are not effective in PAH.

  Mini Chapter 24.30: To reduce the risk of morbidity/mortality events
    Lesson 24.30.1: Addition of macitentan to PDE5is or oral/inhaled prostacyclin analogues is recommended
    Lesson 24.30.2: The addition of selexipag to ERAsc and/or PDE5is is recommended.
    Lesson 24.30.3: The addition of oral treprostinil to ERA or PDE5i/riociguat monotherapy is recommended.
    Lesson 24.30.4: The addition of bosentan to sildenafil is not recommended
  Mini Chapter 24.31: To improve exercise capacity
    Lesson 24.31.1: The addition of sildenafil to epoprostenol is recommended.
    Lesson 24.31.2: The addition of inhaled treprostinil to sildenafil or bosentan monotherapy should be
    Lesson 24.31.3: The addition of riociguat to bosentan should be considered.
    Lesson 24.31.4: The addition of tadalafil to bosentan may be considered.
  Mini Chapter 24.32: Evidence from studies with safety of combination therapy as the primary outcome measure

    Lesson 24.32.2: The addition of ambrisentan to sildenafil may be considered.
    Lesson 24.32.3: The addition of bosentan to sildenafil may be considered.
    Lesson 24.32.4: The addition of sildenafil to bosentan may be considered.
    Lesson 24.32.5: Other sequential double- or triple-combination therapies may be considered to improve
    Lesson 24.32.6: Lung transplant in patients with PAH

    Lesson 25.2.2: Cough, shortness of breath, or difficult or painful swallowing in large TAAs.

    Lesson 25.8.6: Complications
    Lesson 25.8.7: Aortic insufficiency (AI): Mechanisms include
    Lesson 25.8.8: Dissection dilates the sinotubular junction, preventing leaflet coaptation;
    Lesson 25.8.9: Dissection flap prolapses through the aortic orifice and prevents leaflet coaptation.
    Lesson 25.8.10: AI may also be pre‐existent, secondary to a bicuspid aortic valve.
    Lesson 25.8.11: Aortic rupture into the pericardium, leading to tamponade.
    Lesson 25.8.12: Laboratory testing
  Mini Chapter 25.9: To detect signs of
    Lesson 25.9.1: Diagnostic imaging
    Lesson 25.9.2: CT angiogram.
    Lesson 25.9.3: MR angiogram.
    Lesson 25.9.4: The true lumen is compressed and crescentic, while the false lumen is oval;
    Lesson 25.9.5: The false lumen has “smoke” and no flow, or less flow, on Doppler imaging.

  Mini Chapter 25.49: Takayasu arteritis (Pulseless disease)
    Lesson 25.49.1: Diminished or absent pulses with limb claudications (in 84-96%), vascular bruits (80-94%).
    Lesson 25.49.2: Hypertension (33-83%) usually reflecting renal artery stenosis.
    Lesson 25.49.3: Congestive heart failure associated with hypertension and aortic regurgitation.
    Lesson 25.49.4: Takayasu retinopathy (37%).
    Lesson 25.49.5: Pulmonary artery involvement (14-100%).
    Lesson 25.49.6: Role of imaging
    Lesson 25.49.7: Angiography remains the gold standard for confirming the diagnosis and planning the treatment.
    Lesson 25.49.8: Treatment
    Lesson 25.49.9: Leiosarcomas and fibrosarcomas originate from the media or adventitia of the aortic wall.
    Lesson 25.49.10: Diagnosis and workup

    Lesson 27.32.1: Adopt a more plant- and less animal-based food pattern.
    Lesson 27.32.2: < 5 g total salt intake per day
    Lesson 27.32.3: 30-45 g of fibre of per day, preferably from wholegrains
    Lesson 27.32.4: ≥ 200 g of fruit per day (≥ 2-3 servings)
    Lesson 27.32.5: ≥ 200 g of vegetables per day (≥ 2-3 servings)
    Lesson 27.32.6: Fish is recommended 1-2 times per week, in particular fatty fish.
    Lesson 27.32.7: 30 g unsalted nuts per day
    Lesson 27.32.8: Consumption of alcohol should be limited to a maximum of 100 g per week.
    Lesson 27.32.9: Sugar-sweetened beverages, such as soft drinks and fruit juices, must be discouraged.

    Lesson 27.49.5: Blood pressure elevation by a variety of mechanisms.

    Lesson 27.51.1: Intravascular volume is increased.
  Mini Chapter 27.52: The mechanism by which obesity leads to insulin resistance includes
    Lesson 27.52.1: Reduced adeponectin production by adipocytes.
  Mini Chapter 27.53: Treatment
    Lesson 27.53.1: Locaserin, a serotonin 5-HT2C receptor antagonist that results in modest weight loss.
    Lesson 27.53.2: Phentermine/topiramate, a GABA receptor modulators and norepinephrine releasing agent.
  Mini Chapter 27.54: Types of bariatric surgery
    Lesson 27.54.1: Sleeve Gastrectomy is performed by removing approximately 80% of the stomach.

  Mini Chapter 27.58: Phases of cardiac rehabilitation
    Lesson 27.58.1: Phase 3: refers to non-ECG-monitored programs, provided by health clubs and fitness facilities.
    Lesson 27.58.2: Exercise training
    Lesson 27.58.3: The value of physical fitness
    Lesson 27.58.4: Exercise therapy for coronary artery disease
    Lesson 27.58.5: Clinical effects
    Lesson 27.58.6: Reduction of total mortality (27%)
    Lesson 27.58.7: Reduction of cardiac mortality (31%)
    Lesson 27.58.8: Improvement of exercise capacity (12 to 32%)
    Lesson 27.58.9: Improvement of psychological, functioning and social adaptation
    Lesson 27.58.10: No proven effect on return to work.
    Lesson 27.58.11: Initiation of therapy
    Lesson 27.58.12: Patients with contraindication to exercise are excluded.
    Lesson 27.58.13: Mechanisms of improvement
    Lesson 27.58.14: Reduction of LDL-C and increase the HDL-C levels.
    Lesson 27.58.15: Reduction of the incidence of type 2 DM.
    Lesson 27.58.16: Exercise therapy for chronic heart failure
    Lesson 27.58.17: Reduction of total mortality (35%)
    Lesson 27.58.18: Reduction of hospitalization (28%)
    Lesson 27.58.19: Improvement of symptoms and peak oxygen consumption (20%)
    Lesson 27.58.20: Small but significant improvement in cardiac size.
    Lesson 27.58.21: Increase in ejection fraction after prolonged (> 5 years) exercise training.
    Lesson 27.58.22: Small improvement of cardiac performance.
    Lesson 27.58.23: Reduction (25 to 32%) in circulating levels of angiotensin Il and aldosterone.
    Lesson 27.58.24: Improvement of exercise performance and ventilation dynamics.
    Lesson 27.58.25: Exercise therapy after cardiac surgery
    Lesson 27.58.26: Exercise therapy after cardiac surgery was proven to increase maximal oxygen consumption.
    Lesson 27.58.27: Exercise therapy in arrhythmia
    Lesson 27.58.28: Exercise therapy in congenital heart disease
    Lesson 27.58.29: Dietary counseling
    Lesson 27.58.30: Smoking cessation
    Lesson 27.58.31: Psychosocial support
    Lesson 27.58.32: The pathophysiological mechanisms underlying this relationship include
    Lesson 27.58.33: Sexual problems
    Lesson 27.58.34: Sexual dysfunction is highly prevalent in patients with coronary artery disease or hypertension.

    Lesson 28.18.6: LV geometry can be classified using
    Lesson 28.18.7: Relative Wall Thickness (RWT)= 2 x LV posterior wall thickness/LVIDD. Normal value: ≤ 0.42
    Lesson 28.18.8: LV Mass Index (LVMI)= LV mass/BSA. Normal values: ≤ 115 (male) and ≤ 95 (female)
    Lesson 28.18.9: LV geometry can be classified into 4 patterns
    Lesson 28.18.10: Normal: RWT ≤ 0.42, normal LVMI
    Lesson 28.18.11: Eccentric hypertrophy: RWT ≤ 0.42, increased LVMI
    Lesson 28.18.12: Concentric remodelling: RWT > 0.42, normal LVMI
    Lesson 28.18.13: Concentric hypertrophy: RWT > 0.42, increased LVMI

    Lesson 28.19.7: In patients with grade 1 hypertension and at high risk or with evidence of HMOD, prompt initiation
    Lesson 28.19.8: Lifestyle changes are recommended.
    Lesson 28.19.9: Blood pressure treatment targets
    Lesson 28.19.10: It is recommended that SBP should be targeted to a BP range of 130-139 mmHg.
    Lesson 28.19.11: Close monitoring of adverse effects is recommended.

    Lesson 28.19.13: Lifestyle interventions

  Mini Chapter 28.38: In people with diabetes receiving BP-lowering drugs it is recommended
    Lesson 28.38.1: To target SBP to 130 mmHg and < 130 mmHg if tolerated, but not <120 mmHg.
    Lesson 28.38.2: In older people (aged ≥ 65 years aged), to target to an SBP range of 130–139 mmHg.
    Lesson 28.38.3: To target the DBP to < 80 mmHg, but not < 70 mmHg.
    Lesson 28.38.4: Hypertension in CKD
  Mini Chapter 28.39: In patients with diabetic or non-diabetic CKD
    Lesson 28.39.1: It is recommended to lower SBP to a range of 130-139 mmHg.
    Lesson 28.39.2: Individualized treatment should be considered according to its tolerability and impact on renal
    Lesson 28.39.3: Hypertension in CAD
  Mini Chapter 28.40: In patients with CAD receiving BP-lowering drugs, it is recommended
    Lesson 28.40.1: To target SBP to ≤ 130 mmHg if tolerated, but not < 120 mmHg.
    Lesson 28.40.2: In older patients (aged ≥ 65 years), to target to an SBP range of 130-140 mmHg.
    Lesson 28.40.3: To target DBP to < 80 mmHg, but not < 70 mmHg.
    Lesson 28.40.4: Hypertensive patients with heart failure or LVH
  Mini Chapter 28.41: In all patients with LVH
    Lesson 28.41.1: It is recommended to treat with an RAS blocker in combination with a CCB or diuretic.
    Lesson 28.41.2: SBP should be lowered to a range of 120-130 mmHg.
    Lesson 28.41.3: Hypertension patients with acute stroke and cerebrovascular disease
  Mini Chapter 28.42: In patients with acute intracerebral hemorrhage
    Lesson 28.42.1: Immediate BP lowering is not recommended for patients with SBP < 220 mmHg.
    Lesson 28.42.2: In patients with SBP ≥ 220 mmHg, careful acute BP lowering with i.v. therapy to < 180 mmHg
    Lesson 28.42.3: In patients with acute ischemic stroke who are eligible for i.v. thrombolysis, BP should be
    Lesson 28.42.4: In patients with markedly elevated BP who do not receive fibrinolysis, drug therapy may be
    Lesson 28.42.5: Immediately for TIA.
    Lesson 28.42.6: After several days in ischaemic stroke.

    Lesson 28.42.9: Perioperative management of hypertension

    Lesson 29.5.5: 70: 100 mg/dl: Lifestyle advice

    Lesson 29.5.8: > 100 mg/dl: Lifestyle intervention and concomitant drug intervention

    Lesson 29.5.10: > 70 mg/dl: Lifestyle intervention and concomitant drug intervention

    Lesson 29.6.2: > 55 mg/dl: Lifestyle intervention and concomitant drug intervention
    Lesson 29.6.3: 55:70 mg/dl: Lifestyle intervention, consider adding drug if uncontrolled
    Lesson 29.6.4: Very-high risk in primary or secondary prevention: A therapeutic regimen that achieves ≥
    Lesson 29.6.5: High risk: A therapeutic regimen that achieves ≥ 50% LDL-C reduction from baseline and an
    Lesson 29.6.6: Moderate risk: A therapeutic regimen that achieves LDL < 100 mg/dL (< 2.6 mmol/L).
    Lesson 29.6.7: Low risk: A therapeutic regimen that achieves LDL < 116 mg/dL (< 3.0 mmol/L).

    Lesson 29.10.10: In children and adolescents, an LDL-C goal of < 115 mg/dL is recommended.
  Mini Chapter 29.11: It is recommended that a diagnosis of FH is considered in
    Lesson 29.11.1: Patients with CHD aged < 55 years for men and < 60 years for women,
    Lesson 29.11.2: People with severely elevated LDL-C [in adults > 190 mg/dL, in children > 150 mg/dL], and
    Lesson 29.11.3: People with relatives with premature fatal or non-fatal CVD,
    Lesson 29.11.4: People with relatives who have tendon xanthomas,
    Lesson 29.11.5: First-degree relatives of FH patients.

Chapter 30: Cardiodiabetes
  Mini Chapter 30.1: Diagnosis of diabetes
    Lesson 30.1.1: HbA1C ≥ 6.5% or
    Lesson 30.1.2: Fasting plasma glucose (FPG) ≥ 126 mg/dl (1) or
    Lesson 30.1.3: Symptoms of hyperglycemia plus non fasting plasma glucose of ≥ 200 mg/dl.
    Lesson 30.1.4: The diagnosis of Pre-diabetes depends on the detection of any of the following (WHO criteria)
    Lesson 30.1.5: HbA1C 6-6.4% (1) or
    Lesson 30.1.6: Fasting plasma glucose of 110-125 mg/dl (Impaired fasting glucose [IFG]) (2) or
    Lesson 30.1.7: 2-hr plasma glucose of 140-200 mg/dl with FPG < 126 mg/dL (Impaired glucose tolerance [IGT]).
    Lesson 30.1.8: Two tests of either FPG or random glucose levels in the diabetes range.
  Mini Chapter 30.2: Screening for diabetes   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 30.3: Classifying diabetes
    Lesson 30.3.1: Type 1 diabetes
    Lesson 30.3.2: Type 2 diabetes
    Lesson 30.3.3: Monogenic diabetes
  Mini Chapter 30.4: CV manifestations of diabetes
    Lesson 30.4.1: Heart failure
    Lesson 30.4.2: Increasing fasting glucose by 1 mmol/L increases the risk of heart failure by 14%.
  Mini Chapter 30.5: Possible contributing mechanisms include
    Lesson 30.5.1: Collagen accumulation leading to decreased myocardial compliance.
    Lesson 30.5.2: Altered energy substrate supply and utilization
    Lesson 30.5.3: Oxidative stress
    Lesson 30.5.4: Abnormal myocardial calcium handling
    Lesson 30.5.5: Endothelial dysfunction
    Lesson 30.5.6: Deposition of intramyocardial fat (myocardial steatosis)
    Lesson 30.5.7: Cardiac autonomic neuropathy
    Lesson 30.5.8: Gene abnormalities
    Lesson 30.5.9: Thrombosis and coagulation
    Lesson 30.5.10: Higher prevalence of other complications such as diabetic nephropathy
    Lesson 30.5.11: Decreased perception of myocardial ischaemia
    Lesson 30.5.12: Resting tachycardia due to parasympathetic denervation which increases myocardial O2 demands
    Lesson 30.5.13: Abnormal coronary blood flow regulation
    Lesson 30.5.14: Increased prevalence of QT prolongation and QT dispersion
  Mini Chapter 30.6: CV risk assessment in patients with diabetes and prediabetes
    Lesson 30.6.1: Proteinuria (UACR > 300 mg/g; stage A3) or
    Lesson 30.6.2: Very high CV risk: Patients with T2DM with
    Lesson 30.6.3: Clinically established ASCVD or
    Lesson 30.6.4: Severe TOD or
    Lesson 30.6.5: 10-year CVD risk ≥ 20% using SCORE2-Diabetes.
    Lesson 30.6.6: Lifestyle management
  Mini Chapter 30.7: Weight reduction   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

  Mini Chapter 30.10: Insulin
    Lesson 30.10.1: Hypoglycemia
    Lesson 30.10.2: Can be classified according to source (analogue,
    Lesson 30.10.3: Weight gain
    Lesson 30.10.4: Lipodystrophy
    Lesson 30.10.5: Avoid in breast feeding and pregnancy.
    Lesson 30.10.6: Lactic acidosis
  Mini Chapter 30.11: Physiologic actions
    Lesson 30.11.1: ↑ insulin sensitivity
    Lesson 30.11.2: First-line drug in the management of T2DM
    Lesson 30.11.3: Does not cause hypoglycemia or wight gain.
    Lesson 30.11.4: Cannot be used if eGFR of < 30 ml/min.
    Lesson 30.11.5: Stop during episodes of tissue hypoxia (e.g MI)
    Lesson 30.11.6: ↓ hepatic gluconeogenesis
    Lesson 30.11.7: Stop before IV contrast (e.g Angiography) and 2
    Lesson 30.11.8: ↓ absorption of carbohydrates
    Lesson 30.11.9: Metformin reduces intestinal absorption of vit B12
    Lesson 30.11.10: Fluid retention
    Lesson 30.11.11: Contraindicated
    Lesson 30.11.12: In patients who fail to show response to
    Lesson 30.11.13: DPP-4 inhibitors require dose adjustment in
    Lesson 30.11.14: Nausea and vomiting
    Lesson 30.11.15: Urinary tract infection
    Lesson 30.11.16: Typically result in weight loss
    Lesson 30.11.17: Low incidence of hypoglycemia
  Mini Chapter 30.12: CV effects of glucose-lowering drugs
    Lesson 30.12.1: Blood Pressure Control
    Lesson 30.12.2: GLP1-RAs have shown evidence of a slight, but significant, BP decrease, partly due to weight loss.
    Lesson 30.12.3: SGLT2 inhibitors induced a larger BP decrease without heart rate changes.

    Lesson 30.21.1: Multifactorial approaches
    Lesson 30.21.2: Combined reduction in HbA1c, SBP, and lipids decreases CV events by 75%.
    Lesson 30.21.3: Target SBP 130 mmHg for most adults, < 130 mmHg if tolerated, but not < 120
    Lesson 30.21.4: Less-stringent targets, SBP 130 - 139 in older patients (aged > 65 years)
  Mini Chapter 30.22: Glycaemic control
    Lesson 30.22.1: HbA1c target for most adults is < 7.0% (< 53 mmol/mol)

    Lesson 30.22.3: Less-stringent HbA1c goals of < 8% (64 mmol/mol) or ≤ 9% (75 mmol/mol) may be
  Mini Chapter 30.23: Lipid profile
    Lesson 30.23.1: In patients with DM at very high CV risk,a target LDL-C to < 55 mg/dL (< 1.4
    Lesson 30.23.2: In patients with DM at high risk, target LDL-C to < 70 mg/dL (<1.8 mmol/L)
    Lesson 30.23.3: In patients with DM at moderate CV risk, aim for an LDL-C target of < 100 mg/dL

  Mini Chapter 31.39: Two major mechanisms underlie perioperative MI
    Lesson 31.39.1: Atrial fibrillation
    Lesson 31.39.2: Peri-operative stroke
    Lesson 31.39.3: Peri-operative stroke is mainly ischaemic or cardioembolic, and AF is often the leading condition.
    Lesson 31.39.4: The associated mortality ranges from 18- 26%.
    Lesson 31.39.5: In an attempt to attenuate the risk of peri-operative stroke
    Lesson 31.39.6: Adequate selection of the anaesthetic technique (regional vs. neuraxial vs. general anaesthesia),
    Lesson 31.39.7: Prevention and treatment of AF,
    Lesson 31.39.8: Euglycaemic control (avoiding both hyperglycaemia and hypoglycaemia), and
    Lesson 31.39.9: Meticulous peri-operative control of BP.

Chapter 32: Cardio obstetrics
  Mini Chapter 32.1: Epidemiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 32.2: Physiological adaptations to pregnancy
    Lesson 32.2.1: During the first trimester (from conception to 13 weeks and 6 days of gestation)
    Lesson 32.2.2: 50% increase in renal flow and glomerular filtration rates by the end of the first trimester.
    Lesson 32.2.3: During the third trimester (from 28 weeks and 0 days of gestation through delivery)
    Lesson 32.2.4: Heart rate peaks in the late third trimester, with a 20% to 25% increase relative to baseline.
    Lesson 32.2.5: During active labor, SBP and DBP can increase an additional 15-25% and 10-15% respectively.
    Lesson 32.2.6: Cardiac output is increased by 15% in early labor and 25% during the active phase.
  Mini Chapter 32.3: Pre-pregnancy counselling
    Lesson 32.3.1: Predictors of maternal CV events
    Lesson 32.3.2: Prior cardiac event (heart failure, transient ischaemic attack, stroke, arrhythmia).
    Lesson 32.3.3: NYHA class III/IV.
    Lesson 32.3.4: Left heart obstruction (moderate to severe).
    Lesson 32.3.5: Reduced systemic ventricular systolic function (EF < 40%).
    Lesson 32.3.6: Reduced subpulmonary ventricular function (TAPSE < 16 mm).
    Lesson 32.3.7: Systemic atrioventricular valve regurgitation (moderate to severe).
    Lesson 32.3.8: Pulmonary atrioventricular valve regurgitation (moderate to severe).
    Lesson 32.3.9: Pulmonary arterial hypertension.
    Lesson 32.3.10: Cardiac medication before pregnancy.
    Lesson 32.3.11: Cyanosis (O2 saturation < 90%)
    Lesson 32.3.12: Smoking history
    Lesson 32.3.13: Predictors of neonatal events
    Lesson 32.3.14: NYHA class III/IV or cyanosis during baseline pre-natal visit
    Lesson 32.3.15: Maternal left heart obstruction
    Lesson 32.3.16: Smoking during pregnancy
    Lesson 32.3.17: Low maternal oxygen saturation (< 90%)
    Lesson 32.3.18: Multiple gestations
    Lesson 32.3.19: Use of anticoagulants throughout pregnancy
    Lesson 32.3.20: Cardiac medication before pregnancy
    Lesson 32.3.21: ‘At birth’ cyanotic heart disease
    Lesson 32.3.22: Mechanical valve prosthesis
    Lesson 32.3.23: Maternal cardiac event during pregnancy
    Lesson 32.3.24: Maternal decline in cardiac output during pregnancy
    Lesson 32.3.25: Abnormal uteroplacental Doppler flow.
  Mini Chapter 32.4: Diagnosis
    Lesson 32.4.1: Small or mild (pulmonary stenosis, PDA, MVP)
    Lesson 32.4.2: Successfully repaired simple lesions (ASD or VSD, PDA)
    Lesson 32.4.3: Atrial or ventricular ectopic beats, isolated
  Mini Chapter 32.5: Diagnosis
    Lesson 32.5.1: Unoperated ASD or VSD.
    Lesson 32.5.2: Repaired tetralogy of Fallot.
    Lesson 32.5.3: Most arrhythmias (supraventricular arrhythmias).
    Lesson 32.5.4: Turner syndrome without aortic dilatation.
  Mini Chapter 32.6: Diagnosis
    Lesson 32.6.1: Mild left ventricular impairment (EF > 45%)
    Lesson 32.6.2: Hypertrophic cardiomyopathy
    Lesson 32.6.3: Native or tissue valve disease not considered WHO I or IV (mild MS, moderate AS)
    Lesson 32.6.4: Marfan or other HTAD syndrome without aortic dilatation.

  Mini Chapter 32.38: Cardiovascular drugs and pregnancy
    Lesson 32.38.1: ACE‐I, ARB, aldosterone antagonists: contraindicated during pregnancy.
    Lesson 32.38.2: Statins, ezetimibe: contraindicated.
    Lesson 32.38.3: Aspirin is safe.
    Lesson 32.38.4: Clopidogrel: probably safe, but limited data.
    Lesson 32.38.5: Adenosine: safe
    Lesson 32.38.6: Digoxin: safe.
    Lesson 32.38.7: Diuretics: may be used to treat HF. Avoid the aggressive use, as they may reduce placental flow.
    Lesson 32.38.8: Amiodarone: crosses the placenta and may cause neonatal goiter. May be used if necessary.
    Lesson 32.38.9: Sotalol, flecainide: relatively safe.
    Lesson 32.38.10: Hydralazine, methyldopa: safe.
    Lesson 32.38.11: Nitrates: may be used in HF, but preferably avoided.
  Mini Chapter 32.39: References and suggested readings
