  Mini Chapter 33.35: Benihn primary tumor
    Lesson 33.35.1: Myxoma is the most common primary cardiac tumor in adults.
    Lesson 33.35.2: Typically have a narrow stalk connected to the fossa ovalis. Approximately 75% of cardiac
    Lesson 33.35.3: Cardiac myxomas may occasionally be found on the posterior wall of the left atrium.
    Lesson 33.35.4: Symptoms result from intracardiac obstruction, systemic embolization, or constitutional
    Lesson 33.35.5: Myxomas are vascular; blood vessels may be seen on color flow imaging and enhanced mildly
    Lesson 33.35.6: Once the likely diagnosis of cardiac myxoma is made based on echocardiography, resection
    Lesson 33.35.7: The operative mortality is reported to be < 5%.
    Lesson 33.35.8: When atrial myxomas arise in unusual locations and multiple tumors are present, think
    Lesson 33.35.9: The tumor are small (<20 mm), round or oval, with well-demarcated borders and a
    Lesson 33.35.10: Nearly half have small stalks, and those with stalks are mobile.
    Lesson 33.35.11: Can occur on any valve as single lesion (90%), but most commonly involve the aortic valve,
    Lesson 33.35.12: Most often attach to the arterial side of semilunar valves and the atrial surface of the
    Lesson 33.35.13: The distinction between papillary fibroelastomas and vegetations can be difficult by
    Lesson 33.35.14: Surgical resection is indicated in patients who have had embolic events, complications that
    Lesson 33.35.15: Recurrence after surgical resection is rare, but well described in the literature.
    Lesson 33.35.16: Found in the ventricular walls or on the atrioventricular valves. Can be diagnosed before
    Lesson 33.35.17: The microscopic hallmark is the Spider cell (large cell containing central cytoplasmic mass
    Lesson 33.35.18: At least 80% of patients with rhabdomyomas have tuberous sclerosis (autosomaldominant).
    Lesson 33.35.19: ≥ 50% regress spontaneously after infancy. Therefore, in the absence of symptoms, surgery
    Lesson 33.35.20: Benign connective tissue tumors derived from fibroblasts that occur predominantly in
    Lesson 33.35.21: Fibromas are the second most common type of primary cardiac tumors occurring in
    Lesson 33.35.22: Typically large tumors, usually occur within the ventricular myocardium, most commonly in
    Lesson 33.35.23: About 70% of patients are symptomatic. The most common manifestations are congestive
    Lesson 33.35.24: Resection is usually recommended in asymptomatic patients (due to the risk of fatal
    Lesson 33.35.25: Sudden death has been reported to occur in approximately 15% of patients, typically in
    Lesson 33.35.26: Usualy asymptomatic.
    Lesson 33.35.27: LV > RA. Subondocerdial > subepicardial > intramyocardial
    Lesson 33.35.28: Subepicardial tumor can get very large.
    Lesson 33.35.29: Any age usually asymptomatic.
    Lesson 33.35.30: May be associated with GI hemangiomas. Any chamber or wall. Usually, intramural.
    Lesson 33.35.31: Heterogenous on CT. Iso/hyper on T1, hyper on T2.
    Lesson 33.35.32: Enhancement, sometimes with central core. Heterogenous LGE. Isointense to
    Lesson 33.35.33: DD: Teratoma (Hypointense on FPP. Seen in neonates. Both are mixed solid/cystic on CT.
  Mini Chapter 33.36: Malignant tumors
    Lesson 33.36.1: The common presentation is right-sided heart failure or cardiac tamponade as well as
    Lesson 33.36.2: 3:1 male-to-female ratio (unlike other sarcomas, which have a 1:1 ratio).
    Lesson 33.36.3: Very poor prognosis. Usually large or have metastasized at the time of diagnosis. Not
    Lesson 33.36.4: Typically present in their 30s, a decade younger than with other types of sarcomas.
    Lesson 33.36.5: Often occurs in the left atrium, may originate from the posterior wall of LA and involve the
    Lesson 33.36.6: Poor prognosis, with a mean survival after surgery of < 7 months.
    Lesson 33.36.7: Radical surgical resection is the treatment of choice.
    Lesson 33.36.8: The preferential LA location and the frequently myxoid appearance of leiomyosarcomas
    Lesson 33.36.9: Rare form of non-Hodgkin’s lymphoma often restricted to right-sided heart chambers and/or
    Lesson 33.36.10: Approximately 50% of patients with primary cardiac lymphoma will have large pericardial
    Lesson 33.36.11: Rapidly evolves and has been considered an oncologic emergency requiring rapid tissue
    Lesson 33.36.12: Usually asymptomatic.
    Lesson 33.36.13: Anomalous outpouching of partietal pericardium.
    Lesson 33.36.14: Right > left cardiophernic angle.
    Lesson 33.36.15: DDx: bronchogenic cyst (around carina). Pericardial fat pad. Lobulated pleural effusion.
  Mini Chapter 33.37: Normal Anatomical variants

    Lesson 33.37.2: Eustachian valve is a remnant of the embryologic valve responsible for directing IVC blood

    Lesson 33.37.4: A prominent muscle ridge is formed between the LAA and the atrial insertion of the left
    Lesson 33.37.5: Thin filamentous structures attached to the leaflet margin of the aortic valve of elderly

Chapter 35: Cardiac involvement in systemic diseases
  Mini Chapter 35.1: Definition   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 35.2: Mechanisms   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 35.3: There are four mechanisms of shock   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 35.4: Right heart catheterization establishes the mechanism by assessing the three determinants of shock
    Lesson 35.4.1: Right‐ and left‐sided filling pressures (CVP, PCWP). The normal CVP is <8–12mmHg.
    Lesson 35.4.2: Cardiac output (CO). The normal cardiac index is 2.2–4.0 liters/min/m2.
    Lesson 35.4.3: Systemic vascular resistance (SVR). Normal SVR is 700–1500 dyn.s.cm–5.
  Mini Chapter 35.5: Some shock states may be mixed
    Lesson 35.5.1: Reduced cardiac output, anemia, or hypoxemia reduces O2 delivery.
  Mini Chapter 35.6: Shock Management
    Lesson 35.6.1: Urine output > 0.5 ml/kg/h, with a stable creatinine.
    Lesson 35.6.2: Absence of acidosis. Gastric PH or serum lactate may be monitored as a marker of perfusion.
    Lesson 35.6.3: Mixed venous O2 saturation (SvO2) ≥ 65–70%.
    Lesson 35.6.4: Heart rate < 120 bpm.
    Lesson 35.6.5: Warm skin with capillary refill ≤ 2 seconds.
    Lesson 35.6.6: Cardiac history.
    Lesson 35.6.7: Fever and potential sources of infection.
    Lesson 35.6.8: ECG and chest X‐ray are quickly performed.
    Lesson 35.6.9: Intravenous fluid boluses
    Lesson 35.6.10: At this point, along with these initial measures
    Lesson 35.6.11: Echocardiography is performed.
    Lesson 35.6.12: Provide adequate oxygenation (arterial O2 saturation >90–95%), and adequate hemoglobin level
    Lesson 35.6.13: Perform a quick workup in parallel to the previous steps
    Lesson 35.6.14: Bedside echocardiography is performed
    Lesson 35.6.15: Echocardiography helps determine
    Lesson 35.6.16: In cardiogenic shock, look for a cause and treat it
    Lesson 35.6.17: PCI+ IABP ± mechanical circulatory support (e.g., Impella) in MI.
    Lesson 35.6.18: Surgical correction of an acute valvular regurgitation or a mechanical complication of MI.
    Lesson 35.6.19: Cardioversion of a fast tachyarrhythmia (>150bpm).
    Lesson 35.6.20: Pacing for an inappropriately low heart rate, e.g., shock with a rate <60–70bpm.
  Mini Chapter 35.7: Fluid responsiveness
    Lesson 35.7.1: In spontaneously breathing patients
    Lesson 35.7.2: In mechanically ventilated patients
  Mini Chapter 35.8: Indications for transfusion   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 35.9: General indications for transfusion in critically ill or ACS patients   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 35.10: Cardiac effects of the growth hormone
    Lesson 35.10.1: Increased myocardial contractility.
    Lesson 35.10.2: Shift of myosin to the low ATPase activity V3 isoform.
    Lesson 35.10.3: Increased heart rate.
    Lesson 35.10.4: Induction of insulin resistance.
  Mini Chapter 35.11: Cardiovascular manifestations of acromegaly
    Lesson 35.11.1: Derangements in cardiac autonomic function as measured by heart rate recovery and variability.
  Mini Chapter 35.12: Hemodynamic effects of thyroid hormone
    Lesson 35.12.1: Augmentation of LV systolic and diastolic functions.
    Lesson 35.12.2: Increased plasma volume due to activation of renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system.
    Lesson 35.12.3: Increased red cell mass due to increased erythropoietin production.
  Mini Chapter 35.13: Cardiovascular manifestations
    Lesson 35.13.1: Palpitation due to increased heart rate and contractility.
    Lesson 35.13.2: Exertional dyspnea due to weakness of respiratory muscles.
    Lesson 35.13.3: Exercise intolerance due to weakness of skeletal muscles.
    Lesson 35.13.4: Myocardial ischemia due to coronary spasm or associated coronary artery disease in older patients.
    Lesson 35.13.5: Systolic hypertension is common and diastolic hypertension may also occur.
    Lesson 35.13.6: Takotsubo cardiomyopathy may be a presenting manifestation of severe thyrotoxicosis.
  Mini Chapter 35.14: Cardiovascular manifestations
    Lesson 35.14.1: Sinus bradycardia.
    Lesson 35.14.2: Serum Creatine kinase (CK) may be elevated consistent with a skeletal muscle origin.
    Lesson 35.14.3: ECG is characterized by sinus bradycardia, low voltage and prolonged QT interval.
    Lesson 35.14.4: Impaired LV diastolic function in severe or chronic disease.
    Lesson 35.14.5: Dilated cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 35.14.6: ECG abnormalities: prolongation of QT (hypocalcemia prolongs phase 2 of the action potential).
  Mini Chapter 35.15: Cardiovascular manifestations
    Lesson 35.15.1: Hypotension with postural accentuation.
    Lesson 35.15.2: Reduced LV end-diastolic and end-systolic dimensions.
    Lesson 35.15.3: ECG abnormalities: low voltage, sinus bradycardia, PR and QT prolongation and inverted T waves.
  Mini Chapter 35.16: Aetiology   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 35.17: Clinical picture
    Lesson 35.17.1: Non-specific weakness, fatigue, polyuria, or cramps may be associated due to hypokalaemia.
  Mini Chapter 35.18: Investigations
    Lesson 35.18.1: Imaging studies to detect adenoma include CT and MRI.
  Mini Chapter 35.19: Treatment
    Lesson 35.19.1: Laparoscopic adrenalectomy is indicated for unilateral aldosterone producing adenomas.
    Lesson 35.19.2: 11-β hydroxylase deficiency
    Lesson 35.19.3: 17-α hydroxylase deficiency
    Lesson 35.19.4: Corticosteroids are the mainstay of treatment.
  Mini Chapter 35.20: Aetiology
    Lesson 35.20.1: Corticotrophin-dependent: due to pituitary tumor (70-80%) or ectopic ACTH syndrome.
    Lesson 35.20.2: Corticotrophin-independent: usually due to unilateral cortisol producing adenoma or carcinoma.
  Mini Chapter 35.21: Clinical picture
    Lesson 35.21.1: Hypertension and hyperglycemia.
    Lesson 35.21.2: Weight gain, central obesity, facial plethora, and hirsutism.
    Lesson 35.21.3: Muscle wasting, osteoporosis, and easy bruising.
    Lesson 35.21.4: Menstrual irregularities.
  Mini Chapter 35.22: Investigations
    Lesson 35.22.1: Plasma ACTH
    Lesson 35.22.2: Suppression supports the diagnosis of pituitary tumor or hypothalamo-pituitary dysfunction.
    Lesson 35.22.3: Non-suppression is suggestive of ectopic ACTH production.
    Lesson 35.22.4: An increase in plasma ACTH suggests a pituitary tumor or hypothalamo-pituitary dysfunction.
    Lesson 35.22.5: Lack of response suggests ectopic ACTH production.
  Mini Chapter 35.23: Treatment
    Lesson 35.23.1: If a pituitary adenoma is responsible, it can be resected by transsphenoidal microsurgery.
    Lesson 35.23.2: If an adrenal tumor is responsible, it should be removed surgically.
    Lesson 35.23.3: Medical treatment for inoperable cases includes metyrapone, bromocryptine and ketoconazole.
  Mini Chapter 35.24: Clinical picture   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 35.25: Investigations
    Lesson 35.25.1: Initial screening test: 24 h urinary metanephrines.
    Lesson 35.25.2: If the urinary assays are borderline: Plasma catecholamines (norepinephrine plus epinephrine)
    Lesson 35.25.3: If the levels > 2000 pg/ml in the basal state, the presence of pheochromocytoma is highly likely.
    Lesson 35.25.4: If the levels are < 600 pg/ml, the diagnosis is very unlikely.
    Lesson 35.25.5: CT and MRI of the abdomen are used to localize the tumor after the diagnosis is established.
  Mini Chapter 35.26: Treatment
    Lesson 35.26.1: Pheochromocytomas should be resected. Endoscopic procedures are now the standard.
  Mini Chapter 35.27: Cardiovascular manifestations   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

    Lesson 35.29.2: Less common manifestations include diastolic dysfunction, SVT, myocarditis and pericarditis.

    Lesson 35.42.3: Aneurysms usually involving the aortic root and can lead to aortic regurgitation.

    Lesson 35.148.1: Unstable or refractory angina.
    Lesson 35.148.2: Uncontrolled hypertension.
    Lesson 35.148.3: Congestive heart failure (NYHA class III, IV).
    Lesson 35.148.4: Recent myocardial infarction (within 2 weeks).
    Lesson 35.148.5: High risk arrhythmias.
    Lesson 35.148.6: Obstructive hypertrophic cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 35.148.7: Moderate to severe valve disease.
  Mini Chapter 35.149: Treatment of ED in patients with CV disease
    Lesson 35.149.1: Drug therapy
    Lesson 35.149.2: Phosphodiesterase type 5 inhibitors: These agents act by blocking the degradation of cGMP by
    Lesson 35.149.3: Testosterone: indicated for men with hypogonadism. It is not associated with increased CV risk.
  Mini Chapter 35.150: Cardiovascular effects of HIV
    Lesson 35.150.1: Pericardial disease
    Lesson 35.150.2: HIV cardiomyopathy
    Lesson 35.150.3: Pulmonary hypertension
    Lesson 35.150.4: Coronary artery disease
    Lesson 35.150.5: Other cardiovascular affections in HIV infection
    Lesson 35.150.6: Prolonged QT interval particularly with co-infection with hepatitis C
    Lesson 35.150.7: Sudden cardiac death.
  Mini Chapter 35.151: Complications of ART   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

    Lesson 35.152.1: Myocardial ischemia
    Lesson 35.152.2: Causes: Cocaine induces myocardial ischemia through several mechanisms
    Lesson 35.152.3: Severe HTN and tachycardia increase myocardial O2 demands.
    Lesson 35.152.4: Chronic cocaine use promotes atherosclerosis.
    Lesson 35.152.5: Presentation
    Lesson 35.152.6: Diagnosis
    Lesson 35.152.7: Treatment of cocaine‐related MI or ischemia
    Lesson 35.152.8: Medical therapy
    Lesson 35.152.9: Role of invasive management and thrombolysis
    Lesson 35.152.10: Role of stress testing
    Lesson 35.152.11: LV dysfunction is seen in 7% of chronic cocaine users: LV dysfunction results from ischemia and/or
    Lesson 35.152.12: Aortic dissection.
    Lesson 35.152.13: Brugada pattern on ECG (RBBB with ST elevation in V1–V2).

    Lesson 36.45.2: Thyroid disease
    Lesson 36.45.3: P450 enzyme inhibitors, e.g.: amiodarone, ciprofloxacin

    Lesson 36.45.5: Inhibit platelet function: NSAIDs

  Mini Chapter 36.47: Management of high INR
    Lesson 36.47.1: Stop warfarin
    Lesson 36.47.2: Give i.v. vitamin K 5 mg
    Lesson 36.47.3: Prothrombin complex concentrate - if not available then FFP
    Lesson 36.47.4: Give i.v. vitamin K 1-3 mg
    Lesson 36.47.5: Repeat dose of vitamin K if INR still too high after 24 hrs
    Lesson 36.47.6: Restart warfarin when INR < 5.0
    Lesson 36.47.7: Give vitamin K 1-5 mg by mouth, using the i.v. preparation orally
    Lesson 36.47.8: Repeat dose of vitamin K if INR still too high after 24 hours
    Lesson 36.47.9: Give i.v vitamin K 1-3mg
    Lesson 36.47.10: Withhold 1 or 2 doses of warfarin
    Lesson 36.47.11: Reduce subsequent maintenance dose.

  Mini Chapter 36.74: Comparison to warfarin
    Lesson 36.74.1: ↓ ischemic stroke
    Lesson 36.74.2: Same ischemic
    Lesson 36.74.3: ↓ intracranial hemorrhage
    Lesson 36.74.4: ↓ intracranial
    Lesson 36.74.5: Same major bleeding
    Lesson 36.74.6: ↓ death (~0.5%/yr)
    Lesson 36.74.7: ↑ GI bleeding
    Lesson 36.74.8: ↓ death trend
    Lesson 36.74.9: Same ischemic stroke
    Lesson 36.74.10: ↓intracranial
    Lesson 36.74.11: ↓ major bleeding
    Lesson 36.74.12: ↓ death
    Lesson 36.74.13: No ↑ GI bleeding
    Lesson 36.74.14: ↑ ischemic stroke if GFR > 95
    Lesson 36.74.15: ↓death trend

    Lesson 36.76.1: DVT & PE
    Lesson 36.76.2: Prophylaxis: 5000 units SC q8-12hr, OR 7500 units SC q12hr
    Lesson 36.76.3: Treatment: 80 units/kg IV bolus, THEN continuous infusion of 18 units/kg/hr
    Lesson 36.76.4: Elective PCI: (Heparin should be discontinued immediately after PCI).
    Lesson 36.76.5: Without GPIIb/IIIa inhibitor: Initial IV bolus of 70-100 units/kg (target ACT 250-300 sec).
    Lesson 36.76.6: With GPIIb/IIIa inhibitor: Initial IV bolus of 50-70 units/kg (target ACT > 200 sec).

  Mini Chapter 36.101: Contraindications
    Lesson 36.101.1: Severe renal impairment (CrCl < 30 mL/min)
    Lesson 36.101.2: Body weight < 50 kg (venous thromboembolism prophylaxis only)
    Lesson 36.101.3: Active major bleeding
    Lesson 36.101.4: Bacterial endocarditis.

  Mini Chapter 36.102: Cautions
    Lesson 36.102.1: May cause prolonged anticoagulation in moderate renal impairment (CrCl 30-50 mL/min).
    Lesson 36.102.2: Discontinue if platelets <100,000/μL.
    Lesson 36.102.3: Not for IM administration.
    Lesson 36.102.4: Do not use interchangeably with heparin or LMWHs.
    Lesson 36.102.5: Increased risk of bleeding in patients < 50 kg; dosage reduction recommended.
  Mini Chapter 36.103: Mechanism of action   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

  Mini Chapter 36.105: Fibrinolytic agents (plasminogen activators), are divided into the following two categories
    Lesson 36.105.1: Non–fibrin-specific agents (e.g, streptokinase) catalyze systemic fibrinolysis.
    Lesson 36.105.2: 85%: 20 min.
    Lesson 36.105.3: 15%: 80 min.

  Mini Chapter 36.109: Contraindications   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.110: Absolute
    Lesson 36.110.1: Previous intracranial hemorrhage or stroke of unknown origin at anytime.
    Lesson 36.110.2: Ischemic stroke in the preceding 6 months
    Lesson 36.110.3: Central nervous system damage or neoplasm or arteriovenous malformation.
    Lesson 36.110.4: Recent major trauma/surgery/head injury (within the preceding month)
    Lesson 36.110.5: Gastrointestinal bleeding within the past month
    Lesson 36.110.6: Known bleeding disorder (excluding menses)
    Lesson 36.110.7: Aortic dissection
    Lesson 36.110.8: Non-compressible punctures in the past 24 hours (e.g liver biopsy, lumbar puncture).
  Mini Chapter 36.111: Relative
    Lesson 36.111.1: Transient ischemic attack in the preceding 6 months
    Lesson 36.111.2: Oral anticoagulant therapy
  Mini Chapter 36.112: Cautions
    Lesson 36.112.1: Reconstitute no more than 8 hr before use (does not contains antibacterial preservatives).
    Lesson 36.112.2: Avoid excessive agitation during dilution; mix by gently swirling and/or slow inversion.

  Mini Chapter 36.123: Indications
    Lesson 36.123.1: Stable Monomorphic or Polymorphic Ventricular Tachycardia
    Lesson 36.123.2: ACLS, Pulseless VF/VT
  Mini Chapter 36.124: Adverse effects
    Lesson 36.124.1: Thyroid toxicity
    Lesson 36.124.2: Lung toxicity
    Lesson 36.124.3: The lung toxicity is reversible only if diagnosed early, and sometimes improves with steroids.
    Lesson 36.124.4: Proarrhythmic effect
    Lesson 36.124.5: 2-5% incidence; includes significant heart block or sinus bradycardia.
    Lesson 36.124.6: Effects are prolonged when they occur because of long drug half-life.
    Lesson 36.124.7: Neuropathy, ataxia (dose‐dependent).
    Lesson 36.124.8: Blue skin discoloration.
    Lesson 36.124.9: Liver injury: increased liver AST or ALT levels (3-20%; as high as 40-50% in some studies)
    Lesson 36.124.10: Sleep disturbances (3-40%)
  Mini Chapter 36.125: Contraindications
    Lesson 36.125.1: Hypersensitivity.
    Lesson 36.125.2: Avoid during breastfeeding.
  Mini Chapter 36.126: Cautions
    Lesson 36.126.1: Avoid excessive exposure to sunlight; may cause photosensitivity.
    Lesson 36.126.2: Causes increased INR; use caution when initiating therapy in patients treated with warfarin.
  Mini Chapter 36.127: Drug interactions
    Lesson 36.127.1: Amiodarone increases the levels of the NOACs, particularly rivaroxaban.
  Mini Chapter 36.128: Mechanism of action   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

    Lesson 36.131.3: Dyspnea (12%)

    Lesson 36.132.2: Adenoscan: Contraindicated in bronchoconstrictive or bronchospastic lung disease (eg, asthma)

  Mini Chapter 36.136: Indications and Clinical evidence   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.137: Heart Failure   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.138: Adverse effects
    Lesson 36.138.1: Cardiac conduction effects: bradycardia, 1st-degree AV block, PVCs can occur during therapy (10%)
    Lesson 36.138.2: Hypertension or increased blood pressure (8.9%)
    Lesson 36.138.3: Atrial fibrillation (8.3%)
    Lesson 36.138.4: Heart block.
    Lesson 36.138.5: Headache: generally during the early treatment phase
    Lesson 36.138.6: Dizziness: possibly related to bradycardia
    Lesson 36.138.7: GI disturbances: nausea, vomiting, diarrhoea.
  Mini Chapter 36.139: Contraindications
    Lesson 36.139.1: Acute decompensated heart failure.
    Lesson 36.139.2: Clinically significant hypotension.
    Lesson 36.139.3: Clinically significant bradycardia
    Lesson 36.139.4: Severe hepatic impairment.
    Lesson 36.139.5: Severe renal dysfunction (no evidence on safety or pharmacokinetics for CrCl < 15 mL/min).
    Lesson 36.139.6: Pacemaker dependence (heart rate maintained exclusively by the pacemaker)
    Lesson 36.139.7: Concomitant use of strong CYP3A4 inhibitors.
    Lesson 36.139.8: Pregnancy or breastfeeding.
  Mini Chapter 36.140: Cautions
    Lesson 36.140.1: Increases the risk of AF; regularly monitor cardiac rhythm and discontinue drug if AF develops
  Mini Chapter 36.141: Monitoring
    Lesson 36.141.1: Obtain baseline BP and pulse before initiation and after each change in dose.
  Mini Chapter 36.142: Drug interactions   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.143: Mechanism of action
    Lesson 36.143.1: ACEIs competitively inhibit
  Mini Chapter 36.144: Indications & clinical evidence   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

  Mini Chapter 36.149: Adverse effects
    Lesson 36.149.1: Rash: switch ACE-I and if rash persists consider ARB.
  Mini Chapter 36.150: Contraindications
    Lesson 36.150.1: Aortic stenosis: may result in hypotension
    Lesson 36.150.2: Hypersensitivity to ACE-Is
    Lesson 36.150.3: Known bilateral renal artery stenosis
  Mini Chapter 36.151: Cautions
    Lesson 36.151.1: If urea, creatinine, or K+ does rise excessively
    Lesson 36.151.2: Patients on high-dose diuretics (i.e., furosemide > 80 mg daily)
    Lesson 36.151.3: Breast-feeding
  Mini Chapter 36.152: Monitoring
    Lesson 36.152.1: Obtain baseline BP and urea and electrolytes (U&Es) before initiation
    Lesson 36.152.2: Check BP and U&Es within two weeks of initiation or change of dose, then annually.
    Lesson 36.152.3: ACE-I dose should only be increased if
    Lesson 36.152.4: Systolic blood pressure > 90 mmHg
    Lesson 36.152.5: Serum creatinine increases by less than 20% (or eGFR falls by < 15%) on each dose titration
    Lesson 36.152.6: Potassium < 5.5 mmol/L.
  Mini Chapter 36.153: Drug interactions
    Lesson 36.153.1: Ciclosporin: increases risk of hyperkalaemia
    Lesson 36.153.2: Lithium: increases lithium levels
    Lesson 36.153.3: Potassium supplements: increased risk of severe hyperkalaemia.
    Lesson 36.153.4: Antacids may reduce the availability of ACE-I.
    Lesson 36.153.5: Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs may reduce the vasodilator effects of ACE-I.
  Mini Chapter 36.154: Pharmacological notes
    Lesson 36.154.1: The peak plasma drug concentrations are reached 1–4 h after ingestion.

    Lesson 36.167.3: Systolic blood pressure >90 mmHg

  Mini Chapter 36.169: Mechanism of action   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.170: Combination is an angiotensin receptor-neprilysin inhibitor (ARNI)
    Lesson 36.170.1: Enhancing diuresis, natriuresis and myocardial relaxation and anti-remodelling.
    Lesson 36.170.2: Inhibiting renin and aldosterone secretion.
  Mini Chapter 36.171: Indications & clinical evidence   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.172: Heart failure with reduced ejection fraction   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.173: Heart Failure with preserved Ejection Fraction   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.174: Adverse effects
    Lesson 36.174.1: Hyperkalemia (12%): ARNI is associated with less renal dysfunction and hyperkalemia than ACEI.
    Lesson 36.174.2: Cough (9%).
    Lesson 36.174.3: Dizziness (6%).
    Lesson 36.174.4: Falls (1.9%).
    Lesson 36.174.5: Angioedema, all patients (0.5%); in black patients (2.4%).
  Mini Chapter 36.175: Contraindications
    Lesson 36.175.1: Hypersensitivity to any component.
    Lesson 36.175.2: Concomitant use with aliskiren in patients with diabetes.
  Mini Chapter 36.176: Cautions   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.177: Drug interactions
    Lesson 36.177.1: Dual blockade of the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system
    Lesson 36.177.2: Avoid use with an ARB, because drug contains the angiotensin II receptor blocker valsartan.
    Lesson 36.177.3: Concomitant use with aliskiren is contraindicated in patients with diabetes.
    Lesson 36.177.4: NSAIDs including selective cyclooxygenase-2 inhibitors (COX-2 inhibitors)
    Lesson 36.177.5: Effects are usually reversible; monitor renal function periodically.
    Lesson 36.177.6: Lithium
  Mini Chapter 36.178: Mechanism of action
    Lesson 36.178.1: Metabolic and reno protective effects
    Lesson 36.178.2: SGLT2 inhibitors promote natriuresis and uricosuria.
    Lesson 36.178.3: SGLT2 inhibitors reduced liver steatosis and the accompanying hepatocellular injury.
    Lesson 36.178.4: Cardiac effects
    Lesson 36.178.5: Another unproven hypothesis includes possible cardiac anti-fibrotic effects.

  Mini Chapter 36.199: Adverse effects
    Lesson 36.199.1: Hyperuricemia (40%).
    Lesson 36.199.2: Consider allopurinol prophylaxis (not initiated during acute exacerbation).
    Lesson 36.199.3: For symptomatic gout use colchicine for pain relief. Avoid NSAIDs.
    Lesson 36.199.4: Hypokalemia (14-60%).
    Lesson 36.199.5: Glucose intolerance.

    Lesson 36.200.1: IV route twice as potent as PO.
    Lesson 36.200.2: Food delays absorption but not diuretic response.
    Lesson 36.200.3: Possibility of skin sensitivity to sunlight.

    Lesson 36.204.1: Patients allergic to sulfa may show cross-sensitivity.
    Lesson 36.204.2: In primary adrenal insufficiency, avoid use of diuretics to treat hypertension.
  Mini Chapter 36.205: Drug interactions

    Lesson 36.213.1: Hepatic impairment (monitor electrolytes closely).

    Lesson 36.214.1: Check baseline blood chemistry and blood pressure.
    Lesson 36.214.2: Monitoring is recommended at least 3 monthly thereafter.
  Mini Chapter 36.215: Drug interactions
    Lesson 36.215.1: ACE-Is and ARBs: increased risk of severe hyperkalemia with aldosterone antagonists.
    Lesson 36.215.2: Alpha-blockers: enhanced hypotensive effect
    Lesson 36.215.3: Antidepressants: plasma levels of eplerenone reduced by St Johns Wort, so avoid concurrent use.
    Lesson 36.215.4: Antifungal drugs: plasma levels of eplerenone increased by itraconazole and ketoconazole.
    Lesson 36.215.5: Cardiac glycosides: spironolactone may increase plasma levels of digoxin.
    Lesson 36.215.6: Ciclosporin: increased risk of hyperkalaemia
    Lesson 36.215.7: Lithium: increased lithium levels with associated increased risk of toxicity
    Lesson 36.215.8: Potassium salts: increased risk of hyperkalaemia
    Lesson 36.215.9: Tacrolimus: increased risk of hyperkalaemia.
    Lesson 36.215.10: Vasodilating β-blockers: mediated via
    Lesson 36.215.11: Lipophilic drugs
    Lesson 36.215.12: Hydrophilic drugs
    Lesson 36.215.13: Balanced clearance drugs
    Lesson 36.215.14: Esmolol is an ultra short-acting drug. It is administered i.v. (half-life 9 min).

  Mini Chapter 36.222: Adverse effects
    Lesson 36.222.1: Symptomatic hypotension: consider dose reduction, exclude heart block.
    Lesson 36.222.2: Cold extremities: protect fingers and toes with gloves/socks in cold weather.
    Lesson 36.222.3: Sleep disturbances: consider a water-soluble agent.
    Lesson 36.222.4: Erectile dysfunction
  Mini Chapter 36.223: Contraindications
    Lesson 36.223.1: Asthma or history of bronchospasm
    Lesson 36.223.2: PR interval > 0.24 s, any second‐ or third‐degree AV block.
    Lesson 36.223.3: Symptomatic hypotension
    Lesson 36.223.4: Severe decompensated heart failure.
    Lesson 36.223.5: Vasospastic angina
  Mini Chapter 36.224: Cautions
    Lesson 36.224.1: Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (if evidence of a reversible component)
    Lesson 36.224.2: Severe (NYHA class IV) HF.
    Lesson 36.224.3: Current or recent (< 4 weeks) exacerbation of HF (e.g., hospital admission with worsening HF).
    Lesson 36.224.4: Sick sinus syndrome
    Lesson 36.224.5: Patients on diltiazem
    Lesson 36.224.6: Peripheral vascular disease
    Lesson 36.224.7: Pregnancy and breast feeding.
  Mini Chapter 36.225: Monitoring
    Lesson 36.225.1: Obtain baseline BP and pulse before initiation and after dose changes.
    Lesson 36.225.2: In the absence of side-effects review within 4 weeks and consider dose titration.
  Mini Chapter 36.226: Drug interactions
    Lesson 36.226.1: Alpha-blockers: enhanced hypotensive effect
    Lesson 36.226.2: Aluminium salts, cholestyramine, and colestipol may decrease the absorption of b-blockers.
    Lesson 36.226.3: Anti-arrhythmics
    Lesson 36.226.4: Amiodarone—increased risk of myocardial depression, AV block and bradycardia
    Lesson 36.226.5: Flecainide—increased risk of myocardial depression and bradycardia
    Lesson 36.226.6: Lidocaine—increased risk of toxicity with propranolol
    Lesson 36.226.7: Propafenone—increased plasma concentration of metoprolol and propranolol
    Lesson 36.226.8: Antidepressants
    Lesson 36.226.9: Citalopram, escitalopram, paroxetine—increased metoprolol plasma concentration
    Lesson 36.226.10: Enhanced hypotensive effect with monoamine oxidase inhibitors (MAOIs)
    Lesson 36.226.11: Antimalarials: mefloquine—increased risk of bradycardia
    Lesson 36.226.12: Calcium-channel blockers
    Lesson 36.226.13: Enhanced hypotensive effect
    Lesson 36.226.14: Diltiazem—risk of AV block, bradycardia
    Lesson 36.226.15: Verapamil—severe hypotension and heart failure
    Lesson 36.226.16: Ciclsporin: increased plasma concentration with carvedilol
    Lesson 36.226.17: Clonidine: increased risk of withdrawal hypertension
    Lesson 36.226.18: Diuretics: enhanced hypotensive effect.
    Lesson 36.226.19: Indomethacin and other NSAIDs antagonize the antihypertensive effects of b-blockers.
  Mini Chapter 36.227: Mechanism of action   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)

  Mini Chapter 36.232: Atrial fibrillation   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.233: Adverse effects
    Lesson 36.233.1: Symptomatic hypotension: consider dose reduction.
    Lesson 36.233.2: Flushing: dissipates over time—encourage patient to persist with therapy.
    Lesson 36.233.3: GI disturbance: may make constipation worse.
    Lesson 36.233.4: Ankle oedema: often dose related, consider dose reduction or combine with ACE-I or ARB
    Lesson 36.233.5: Gingival hyperplasia: withdraw CCB and consider alternatives.
  Mini Chapter 36.234: Contraindications
    Lesson 36.234.1: Cardiogenic shock, advanced aortic stenosis, within one month of acute MI, unstable angina or ACS.
  Mini Chapter 36.235: Monitoring
    Lesson 36.235.1: Obtain baseline BP and pulse before initiation and after dose changes
    Lesson 36.235.2: In the absence of side-effects, review within 4 weeks and consider dose titration.
  Mini Chapter 36.236: Drug interactions
    Lesson 36.236.1: Alpha-blockers: enhanced hypotensive effect
    Lesson 36.236.2: Anaesthetics: enhanced hypotensive effect
    Lesson 36.236.3: Anti-epileptics: variable effects on plasma levels—consult product literature.
    Lesson 36.236.4: Antifungals
    Lesson 36.236.5: Antivirals
    Lesson 36.236.6: Barbiturates: effect of CCB reduced
    Lesson 36.236.7: Beta-blockers
    Lesson 36.236.8: Digoxin: verapamil increases digoxin levels, with increased risk of AV block/bradycardia
    Lesson 36.236.9: Ciclosporin: variable effects on plasma levels—consult product literature.
    Lesson 36.236.10: Ivabradine: plasma levels increased by diltiazem, verapamil—avoid concomitant use.
    Lesson 36.236.11: Sirolimus: plasma levels increased by diltiazem and verapamil.
    Lesson 36.236.12: Tacrolimus: plasma levels increased by diltiazem and nifedipine
    Lesson 36.236.13: Theophylline: increased plasma levels of theophylline.

    Lesson 36.238.1: Intermediate-acting nitrates: Isosorbide dinitrate (oral).
    Lesson 36.238.2: Long-acting nitrates: Isosorbide mononitrate (oral).
  Mini Chapter 36.239: Indications & clinical evidence
    Lesson 36.239.1: Oral nitrates should be considered as adjunctive therapy in the prophylaxis of anginal chest pain.
  Mini Chapter 36.240: Adverse effects
    Lesson 36.240.1: Hypotension: patients should be advised to sit down before taking sublingual nitrates.
  Mini Chapter 36.241: Contraindications
    Lesson 36.241.1: Anticoagulants: infusion of GTN reduces the anticoagulant effect of heparins.
    Lesson 36.241.2: Sildenafil, tadalafil, vardenafil: hypotensive effects of nitrates are significantly enhanced.
  Mini Chapter 36.242: Classification
    Lesson 36.242.1: Nonselective α blockers: Phenoxybenzamine, Phentolamine.

  Mini Chapter 36.255: Hydrophilic statins   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.256: Adverse effects
    Lesson 36.256.1: Statin-associated muscle symptoms (SAMS)
    Lesson 36.256.2: Myalgia: Statins are associated with muscular pain and tenderness without CK elevation.
    Lesson 36.256.3: Insomnia
  Mini Chapter 36.257: Adherence issues   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.258: Contraindications
    Lesson 36.258.1: Hypersensitivity to the individual statin
    Lesson 36.258.2: Pregnancy and lactation
  Mini Chapter 36.259: Cautions
    Lesson 36.259.1: Hypothyroidism should be corrected before initiation of a statin.
    Lesson 36.259.2: Patients with a high alcohol intake
    Lesson 36.259.3: Patients with risk factors for myopathy or rhabdomyolysis
    Lesson 36.259.4: Acute porphyria (rosuvastatin is thought to be safe)
    Lesson 36.259.5: Renal disease—dose reductions may be necessary.
  Mini Chapter 36.260: Drug interactions
    Lesson 36.260.1: Antibacterials
    Lesson 36.260.2: Antifungals
    Lesson 36.260.3: Antivirals: use under specialist supervision only
    Lesson 36.260.4: Calcium-channel blockers
    Lesson 36.260.5: Colchicine: increased risk of myopathy with statins
    Lesson 36.260.6: Danazol: possible increase in myopathy with simvastatin
    Lesson 36.260.7: Nicotinic acid: increased risk of myopathy with statins.
  Mini Chapter 36.261: Monitoring   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.262: Testing lipids
    Lesson 36.262.1: After starting treatment: 8 (± 4) weeks.
    Lesson 36.262.2: After adjustment of treatment: 8 (± 4) weeks until the goal is achieved.
  Mini Chapter 36.263: Monitoring liver and muscle enzymes
    Lesson 36.263.1: Before treatment.
    Lesson 36.263.2: Once, 8-12 weeks after starting a drug treatment or after dose increase.
    Lesson 36.263.3: If ALT < 3 ULN
    Lesson 36.263.4: Continue therapy.
    Lesson 36.263.5: Recheck liver enzymes in 46 weeks.
    Lesson 36.263.6: If ALT rises to ≥ 3 ULN
    Lesson 36.263.7: Stop lipid-lowering therapy or reduce dose and recheck liver enzymes within 46 weeks.
    Lesson 36.263.8: Cautious reintroduction of therapy may be considered after ALT has returned to normal.
    Lesson 36.263.9: If ALT remains elevated check for the other reasons.
    Lesson 36.263.10: Pre-treatment
    Lesson 36.263.11: Before starting therapy.
    Lesson 36.263.12: If baseline CK is > 4 ULN, do not start drug therapy; recheck.
    Lesson 36.263.13: Monitoring
    Lesson 36.263.14: Routine monitoring of CK is not necessary.
    Lesson 36.263.15: Check CK if patient develops myalgia.
    Lesson 36.263.16: If ≥ 4 ULN
    Lesson 36.263.17: If CK >10 ULN: stop treatment, check renal function, and monitor CK every 2 weeks.
    Lesson 36.263.18: Consider the possibility of transient CK elevation for other reasons such as exertion.
    Lesson 36.263.19: Consider myopathy if CK remains elevated.
    Lesson 36.263.20: Consider combination therapy or an alternative drug.
    Lesson 36.263.21: If < 4 ULN
    Lesson 36.263.22: If no muscle symptoms, continue statin (patient should be alerted to report symptoms; check CK).
    Lesson 36.263.23: If muscle symptoms, monitor symptoms and CK regularly.
    Lesson 36.263.24: Consider rechallenge with the same or another statin.
  Mini Chapter 36.264: Pleiotropic properties of statins   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.265: Mechanism of action   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.266: Indications and Clinical evidence   ← (No Lessons → treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 36.267: Adverse effects
    Lesson 36.267.1: Key adverse effects include GI disturbances, which are usually self-limiting.
    Lesson 36.267.2: Fibrates are associated with a slightly increased risk of pancreatitis.
  Mini Chapter 36.268: Contraindications
    Lesson 36.268.1: Known hypersensitivity.
    Lesson 36.268.2: Active liver disease.
    Lesson 36.268.3: Gallbladder disease.
    Lesson 36.268.4: Nursing mothers.
  Mini Chapter 36.269: Cautions
    Lesson 36.269.1: Paradoxical decreases in HDL cholesterol (HDL-C) level reported.
    Lesson 36.269.2: Rule out secondary causes of hyperlipidemia before initiating therapy.
    Lesson 36.269.3: Withdraw therapy if no adequate response seen after 2-3 months.
  Mini Chapter 36.270: Drug interactions
    Lesson 36.270.1: Antibacterials: increased risk of myopathy with daptomycin
    Lesson 36.270.2: Anticoagulants: enhance effect of warfarin and other coumarins
